THE BED PAGE.
Thiebault’s Memoirs.
General Thffibault was one of the brilliant
soldiers who fought for France in the Napoleonic
era. Born in 1769 at Berlin, where his father
was French envoy, he came to Paris at 15; com-
manded a company of the National Guard at the
Revolution, saved his neck in the Terror, adopted
the profession of arms, and rose gradually under
Napoleon to the rank of general of division. He
fought the lazzaroni at Naples, was with Massena
at Genoa, received a severe wound at Austerlitz,
went with Junot to the Peninsula and fought long
against Wellington, then, returning to Germany,
was inactive under Davout when Leipsig came.
He ’verted to royalty at the Restoration and
back to Napoleon during the Hundred Days, but
did not fight ab Waterloo. Making peace with
the Bourbqns, he passed the rest of his life quietly
at Paris, and died in 1846.
Thi6baulb was a handsome, clever, courageous
fellow, with a line talent for war, who should
have been marshal. Bub he lacked tact, missed
opportunities of advancement through pride or
folly, and spoiled his career at the outset by
offending Berthier and Napoleon. His rank as
general was a grudging homage to achievements
which had earned him a much higher position.
As his life wore on, disappointment soured him,
and he shone less and less.
His memoirs are charming reading. The recent
English translation represents only a portion of
the French original, which seems to hold high
place among its famous kindred. Thiebault had
a good literary faculty ; he was acquainted with
many of the personages who in his day made his-
tory for France; and he mingles the personal
element with the historical as interestingly as all
his countrymen. Anecdotes and impressions,
estimates and pictures, give a lively gloss on the
standard conclusions as to the facts and people of
the epooh.
Thiebault gives some idea of the prodigious
effect produced upon the cultured youth of his
day by Rousseau’s “Nouvelle Heloi'se ”
I do not know under what circumstance or with what
idea I opened this dangerous work, but before I had read
more than the first pages I was in a delirium. In truth,
I did not read, I devoured. The days were not sufficient.
I employed my nights in reading ; and passing from one
emotion, one catastrophe, to another, I arrived at St.
Preux’s last letter, no longer weeping, but shrieking and
howling like a wild animal. I still remember it. Ic was
three o’olock in the morning when, in the middle of one
of these fits, I came to myself. I was terrified at the con-
dition I was in ; I threw away the book, I put out my
candle and tried to go to sleep, but could not. I was in
a violent fever, and when day came I tried to piok myself
up by going to breathe the morning air in the Champs
Elys6es. It was a week before I ventured to go on with
the last volume of the story, and I could finish it only by
reading half a page or a quarter of a page at a time.
Thus I was more than another week getting to the end
of that letter, and in spite of these scrappy readings,
often interrupted in the middle of a sentence, I got ill
and nearly went out of my mind. The impression which
the novel left on me is more than I could say ; from that
day I have never ventured to try another reading of it,
nor should I venture even now, though many years of
trials have weakened or destroyed, if not my power of
feeling, at least my imagination.
There is a picture of that remarkable person-
ality, the Chevalier de Saint-Georges, in the last
days of the Monarchy:—
La Boessifere’s fencing-school was the meeting-place of
the best fencers in France—Fabien, Lebrun, Leprince,
Lamotte, and several others, who formed the escort, one
might almost say the court, of the Chevalier de Saint-
Georges, a very king of arms, and the first man in the
world at anything requiring activity, strength or skill.
The effect which he produced on me, who yielded to no
one in the way of admiration and enthusiasm, may be
Imagined. In truth, I saw him do most extraordinary
things: he would put his baoa against the edge of a door,
seize the top with both hands, double himself upwards,
passing his feet, legs, and loins over his head until he sat
astride the door. Similarly I have seen him jump up and
seize a beam in the ceiling between his fiat hands, remain
hanging, and touoh the beam with his two feet. Though
a big man, and already portly, he was still a perfect
dancer, a wonderful rider, and a first-class skater. He
played the violin like an artist, and composed concertos
which were long performed by amateurs. But all his
talents, however great, seemed to disappear before the
incalculable advantage which he always had with the foil
over the strongest fencers in the world. All were
ambitious to fence with him, not with any idea of dis-
puting his supremacy, but simply for the sake of saying,
“I have fenced or am fencing with Saint-Georges.” A
simple fact will give an idea of the inconceivable rapidity
and accuracy of his movements. I have seen him more
than once win a bet, from the masters whom I have
named, that he would engage their sword in tieroe, dis-
engage, tap the ground with the point of his foil, touch
them with a chalked button, and meet their sword again
in carte before they could make a movement to parry.
That was to make at least three movements before these
strong and clever fencers could make one.
Here is the evidence of Louis Sixteenth’s bruta-
lity which shocked London Spectator, a loyal
animal-loving paper which refuses to believe that
a king can do wrong to a dog. Thi4bault’s
story, however, is naturally told, and is probably
Srue enough.
As we drew near the terraoe on the river-side, the king
oame out of the small gate near the Pavilion of Flora
accompanied only by two gentlemen. Just then a lady
came through the gate. She had a pretty little spaniel
with her, which, before she noticed it, ran close up to the
king. Making a low courtesy, she called the dog hack in
haste ; but, as the animal turned to run to its mistress
the king, who had a large cane in his hand, broke its
back with a blow of his cudgel. Then, amid the screams
and tears of the lady, and as the poor little beast was
breathing its last, the king, delighted with his exploit,
continued his walk, slouching rather more than usual
and laughing like any lout of a peasant.
Thiebault, by no means an adulator of Bona-
parte, gives many instances of his irresistible
energy, his genius for war, his wonderful power
over the soldiery. And he lays stress on his good
fortune, particularly at the outset—
Jt was undoubtedly a great piece of luck for Bonaparte
that his first oommand was with the Army of Italy and not
with that of the Rhine. As yet he had little or no know-
ledge of taotics, he could not even have handled a regi-
ment, and war in Germany was essentially a matter of
tactics and prompt manoeuvring under fire. In the
mountainous country of Lombardy and Piedmont, on the
other hand, where the main object was to mass superior
forces on one part of the enemy’s lines, to cut his com-
munications, to attack his rear, able strategy was wanted •
individual courage and intelligence went for a great deal,’
and the valor of the humblest soldier might be an impor-
tant aid to the ability of his chiefs. This, rather than the
other, was the kind of warfare suited to the age and the
nature of the new commander-in-chief, and at the outset
his genius was favored by opportunity.
Attention, indeed, is rarely directed to the ticklish
episodes in Bonaparte’s early career. When he
drove out the Council of the Five Hundred it was
touch-and-go with the First Consul. But for
brother Lucien, as President, and Lucien’s address
to the wavering soldiers, the event might easily
have been otherwise; and, given a Danton or a
Mirabeau among the Councillors, Bonaparte could
have been executed for treason at the beginning of
his ambition. Or, later, had he failed at Marengo
—as he was so near failing—it might well have
been all over with his prospects. Between Massena
and Desaix, he was saved for St. Helena.
Thi4bault didn’t like Berthier, Napoleon’s
right-hand man, and says he was “never anything
more than a good man to convey orders.” Duhesme
pleased him better, though not on first acquaint-
ance. There is a story of Duhesme in the Italian
campaign of 1799 :
General Duhesme took up the conversation with Girard
as follows: “ Who commands at Precara?” “There are
two commanders.” “ What is the rank of the one in
superior authority?" “Brigadier.” “His name?”
“Marquis of Pietramaggiore.” “How old is he?”
“Nearly seventy.” “Stout? High coloured?” “No;
thin and pale.” “Sonorous voice?” “No; weak, and
he isdeaf.” “How does he wear his hair?” “In little
curls, with a great deal of powder.” “ Has he boots and
spurs?” “No; silk stockings and shoes with large
buckles” “Large buckles!” cried General Duhesme;
“advance the artillery and commenoe firing. The plaoe
is mine.”
It would be easy to quote similar good things ad
infinitum ; but with an extract introducing Thie-
bault himself leave must be taken of his most
readable volumes:—
The amount of thrashing I did in the Austrian cam-
paign of 1305 is incredible, and it must be admitted that
I did not go at it with a slack hand, for I broke two canes.
The incident that cost me the second is one that depicts
our French soldier admirably, and in a sense does him
honor. I had let my brigade go forward, I think, while I
stopped to breakfast; several other corps had passed me,
and I was trotting on to rejoin it, when I heard fearful
shrieks coming from a solitary house a few vards to the
left of the road. _ I dismounted at once and ran to the
house. I found it full of soldiers, rummaging in all the
cupboards, even in the drawers, to see, as one of th6m
had the impudence to say, that there were no Austrians
hidden there ; others had got very tight hold of the wife
and daughter of the house. Using my cane freely, I made
them bolt out of door and window. . . . On resuming my
road Isaw that it made alongcircuit round a hillock, on the
other side of which was some level ground, which seemed
to offer a short cut. I urged my horse that way, but he had
gone only a few steps when his hoofs sunk in ;
he tried to back, I spurred him ; then he leapt forward,
and gave a great many bounds as though paddling
through water ; at last he was right in the mud, and, as
it were, floating on his stomach. I was in one of those
abominable swamps, frequent enough in Germany, which
there is nothing on the surface to show, while they often
cover a wide space, and in some cases are bottomless. I
soon saw that I could look for no support save from my
horse ; but he sank imperceptibly, and the least effort
that he made to get out involved him further. Riche-
bourg and my orderly were in despair, not knowing how
to help me, and we were getting very uneasy when the
same soldiers whom I had just been whacking and who
had sworn to kill me, but who at bottom knew quite well
that I had done my duty, and in this case, at all events,
made no mistake as to theirs, having reached the point
where the road looked down from the hill, saw
the danger I was in. With a spontaneous move-
ment they sorambled down the steep side, threw
down their knapsacks and arms, ran to the wooden fence
round the house from which I had just driven them,
broke it up and returned each with his load of planks to
the edge of the quagmire. Making a floating floor on
this, they suoeeeded in reaching and saving me; and not
oontent with their first exploit, they pushed their planks
ail round my horse, and then, by the help of cords, lever
and girths, they lifted him and dragged him by his head
and tail out of the horrible mud, and brought him back
to me. “ Come,” I said, patting one of them on the
shoulder, “ there is a bit of devil about you sometimes,
but you are good fellows all the same. Here,” I added,
pulling out two napoleons, “take this to drink the
Emperor’s health with.” “We have drunk that quite
often enough,” said one of them, and they went off laugh-
ing, but nothing would they take.
The translation, by A. J. Butler (translator of
Marbot’s Memoirs), seems to be very capably
done.
Contributors’ Commentary.
One of the Rear-Guard : “ Lawson’s verses
on “The Men Who Come Behind ” seem to me to
arrogate leadership, to assume a sort of don’t-get-
m-my-way-or-I’ll kick-you-out-of-it air that ‘ rubs
every hair on my back the wrong way.’ The
poet denounces competition and imitation. But
everybody imitates, everybody competes. Law-
son didn't start the first oyster-shop. Even if he
started the first in his little district he competed
with the butcher. The man who does not imitate
does nothing and is an embodiment of human
stagnation, and he who does not compete is a
nerveless slouch. Nowadays the non-competitor
is chiefly lying in the Domain, where perhaps
Mr. Dawson visits him and encourages him in the
pursuit of his calling, viz., Not ‘starting oyster-
saloons where be might get a little of the trade.’
What is the difference between ‘Faces in the
street , and that old song called ‘London
Bridge ? Give the palm to which you will, they
have precisely the same idea, and I am certain
that neither H. Lawson nor the other man in-
vented it. It came aeons upon aeons back, out of
the brain of the first man who felt sorry for
some other man who wasn’t as well off as he was.”
Maxillus : “A part of Olive Schreiner’s
parable on the pursuit of truth (in the impressive
‘The Hunter ’) seems merely an amplification of
Pope’s lines :
So pleased at first the towering Alps we try,
Mount o’er the vales, and seem to tread the sky,
Th’ eternal snows appear already past,
And the first clouds and mountains seem the last:
But, those attained, we tremble to survey
The growing labors of the lengthened way,
The increasing prospect tires our wandering eyes,
Hills peep o’er hills, and Alps on Alps arise.
H.F. : “Be your formula for creating
genius: ‘You want from one parent a good-
quality blood with plenty of red corpuscles;
from the other a good-quality brain and a touch
of nervous disease.’ Are you not attempting the
difficult feat of seeking a simple answer for a
complex problem ; a formula that may be true in
some instances, but, unless you narrow down the
definition of genius to arbitrary limits, not true in
all. There are authorities who have traced genius
to a fortunate blend in parentage—the happy
mixing of Saxon, Celtic, Norman or Gallic blood,
and have raked up the ancestry of great minds
in corroboration. These gentlemen would pro-
duce you a poet, painter, or whatnot, as Derby
winners are bred at Hobartville, by the study of
pedigree. The writer has another theory to add.
It is, that the exceptional product known as
genius is often the result of favoring influences
received by the mother and impressed
through her on the embryo, and further,
that in embryology we shall find one of the miss-
ing links of evolution; which rests ab present on
' chance ’ variation producing the fittest. Bub
‘ chance ’ is not scientific ; there must be law, even
for variation. Your date, a.h. 250,000, for a sen-
sational development of human brains, appears
premature and likely to raise up vain hopes, Two
thousand years ago the Greeks showed an all-
round brain-power in arts, philosophy, and
imagination which, in mere power, we have nob
yet passed. The Book of Job, dating back, say,
4000 years, shows the highest intellect (conditions
considered), and the ancient writing of other lands
would seem to bear out the contention that
—historically—we have no evidence that in 6000
years the quality of the human brain has percept-
ibly improved. Types as low as the cave-dwellers
are found on the earth to-day, and some unre-
corded Homer may have chanted the slaughter of
mastodons. Nor do the' races of mankind of
whom we have any knowledge help the theory of
brain evolution. The Hebrews, Chinese, and
Arabs appear to have preserved their mental
qualities and limitations unvaried through the
ages.” [Man has been on this planet, say, 200,000
years. He discovered iron and alphabets, com-
paratively speaking, only the other day. Now he
is commencing to forge ahead. His own records
cover too small a space to permit deductions, but
the records of the rocks are conclusive as to his
skull-development in, say, 50,000 years. By
anno hominis 250,000 he wifi assuredly have
developed much further.]
W.E.S. : “Be genius and disease, and the
comment to ‘C.’ (17/10/96) to effect that the peou-
liar intensity of genius on any line is due to
hypertrophy of some part of the brain con-
cerned. When sheep first get fluke, they
increase in weight immensely, owing to the
greater activity of the liver in trying to ejeot
the fluke—which creates more blood, and induces
a far livelier habit in the sheep. Then comes the
reaction on the other organs, and final break-
down. Doesn’t the analogy hold ? ” It does, and
there is a still more familiar domestic illustra-
tion. Any mother of any child : “Jimmy’s too
lively : he musb be getting a cold.” And why
lively ? Because Jimmy’s heart is pulsing away
at its hardest, endeavoring to revivify the morbid
cells in the lungs or air-passages, and his brain is
temporarily congested with blood —as genius’s all
the time.
Aubrey Beardsley’s verse and illustrations
in last issue were taken from The Savoy magazine.
The Portrait.
The man who told this tale is not
Hither you- , or I, dear friend ;
Who may therefore, glad of our better lot,
Hear his story told to the end.
Midnight past! Not a sound of aught
Thro’ the silent house, but the wind at his prayers.
I sat by the dying fire, and thought
Of the dear dead woman upstairs.
A night of tears ! for the gusty rain
Had ceased, but the eaves dripping yet;
And the moon look’d forth as tho' in pain,
With her face all white and wet:
Nobody with me, my watch to keep,
But the friend of my bosom, the man I love ;
And grief had sent him fast to sleep
In the chamber up above.
Nobody else, in the country place
All round, that knew of my loss beside,
But the good young Priest with the Raphael-faoe
Who confess’d her when she died.
That good young Priest is of gentle nerve,
And my grief had moved him beyond controul;
For his lip grew white, as I could observe,
When he speeded her parting soul.
I sat by the dreamy hearth alone:
I thought of the pleasant days of yore:
I said—“ The staff of my life is gone:
The woman I love is no more.
“ Gem-ciasp’d, on her bosom my portrait lies,
Which next to her heart she used to wear—
It is steep’d in the light of her loving eyes,
And the sweets of her bosom and hair."
And I said—“ The thing is precious to me:
They will bury her soon in the ohurchyard clay;
It lies on her heart, and lost must be,
If I do not take it away.”
I lighted ray lamp at the dying flame,
And crept up the stairs that creak’d for fright,
Till into the chamber of death I oame,
Whore she lay all in white.
The moon shone over her winding-sheet
There, stark she lay on her oarven bed;
Seven burning tapers about her feet,
And seven about her head.
As I stretch'd my hand, I held my breath ;
I turned as I drew the curtains apart:
I dared not look on the face of death:
I knew where to find her heart.
I thought, at first, as my touch fell there,
It had warmed that heart to life, with love j
For the thing I touch’d was warm, I swear,
And I could feel it move.
’Twas the hand of a man, that was moving slow
O’er the heart of the dead,—from the other side 1
And at once the sweat broke over my brow—
“ Who is robbing the corpse?” I cried.
Opposite me, by the tapers’ light,
The friend of my bosom, the man I loved,
Stood by the corpse, and all as white,
And neither of us moved,
“ What do you here, my friend ? ” . . . The mao
Looked first at me, and then at the dead.
“ There is a portrait here . . . "he began ;
“ There is. It is mine,” I said.
Said the friend of my bosom, “Yours, no doubt,
The portrait was, till a month ago,
When this suffering angel took that out
And placed mine there, I know.”
“This woman she loved me well,” said I.
“ A month ago,” said my friend to me:
‘ And in your throat,” I groan'd, “ you He i **
He answer’d . . , “ Let us see."
“ Enough 1 ” I return’d, “ let the dead deoidei
And whose soever the portrait prove,
His shall it be, when the cause is tried,
Where Death is arraign’d by Love."
We found the portrait, there in its places
We open’d it by the tapers’ shine:
The gems were all unchanged: the face
Was—neither his nor mine.
“ One nail drives out another, at least!
The portrait is not ours,” I cried,
“ But our friSnd’s, the Raphael-faced young Priest,
Who confess’d her when she died,”

Medical.
COUGHS, COLDS ON THE CHEST, BRON
CHITJS AND ASTHMA RAPIDLY
CURED BY Hearne’s Bronchitis cure.
Those who have taken this medicine are amazed
at its wonderful influence. Sufferers from any
form of bronchitis, distressing cough, difficulty of
breathing, hoarseness, pain or soreness in the
chest, experience delightful and immediate relief,
and to those who are subject to colds on the chest
it is invaluable, as it effects a complete cure. It
is most comforting in allaying tickling and irrita-
tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voice,
and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,

Did you ever read about OLD SCROOGE and
the XMAS CAROL?
What was OLD SCROOGE like before he saw
MARLEY’S GHOST? A miserable wretch,
begrudging a starving cur a lick out of the gutter,
the children their Xmas feast, and all mankind its
Xmas enjoyment.
What would you be like if you didn’t see
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
XMAS SHOW.
Not so bad as OLD SCROOGE, surely, for he
was a bad case indeed. But you would not, nay,
could not, be happy. Your heart would yearn
for something, and you wouldn’t know what. You
would be low-spirited, snappish, and bilious. There
would be no brightness in your eye, no elasticity
in your step, your weary feet would be heavy as
lead, your flesh would fall away, and instead of
the sleek, WELL-GROOMED CITIZEN, a lean,
woebegone, CADAVEROUS PANTALOON

skin. Friend, don’t risk it, make a point of seeing

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MOTHER BED AND MR. SLEEP.
In the hours of evening—sleepy hours
When a glamor, resting on the flowers,
Dreams from out the twilight to the dusk —*
Dreams and dies—
The playing children’s poppy-tinted faces,
Full of weariness, of gambols, and of races,
Turn-unto the ever-welcome shaded places
For tired eyes.
No more tiring with the lessons, nor with playing,
The moon sends just one argent beam a-raying—
A sleepful beam that never tires staying
On a baby’s head ;
And weary worn by days of careful scheming,
I stood outside the track of the moon’s gleaming,
And heard the children praying, half a-dreaming—
A-praying from the bed—
“ Oh, Mother Bed ”
The children said
In the light of the argent beams),
“ Show us the house of Mr. Sleep,
Who lives in the Town of Dreams ! ”
And Mother Bed told the way so lowly
That none could hear but the young and holy—
For the man of cares they always keep
From the Town of Dreams and the Street of Sleep.
In the hours of evening—waking hours —
Wakeful devils lurk among the flowers,
Active thoughts that swing and sway like fiends
Make light my eyes !
Active thoughts within my brain are swinging,
The curse of commerce unto me is clinging,
New schemes spring off from old, and still are
springing,
And not one dies !
The loud, loud clock-ticks hammer through the
curtain,
Nothing is dim, nor blessedly uncertain—
The world is plain—
Clearly the eye takes toll of glass and casement,
No shock—no calm—no fear—and no amazement;
No joy—no grief—no pride—and no abasement !
Only the accurate arithmetic working
Of the tense brain,
And so worn out with ravelled night and day
I long for that good time when I shall say—
“ Oh, Mother Bed !
When I am dead
And safe from my plans and schemes;
Show me the house of Mr. Sleep
In the Town of the Children’s Dreams ! ”
And Mother Bed shall whisper lowly,
Of the track so clean, of the road so holy,
And I shall go and drink long and deep
In the Town of Dreams—at the house of Sleep !
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
WHEN THE COLD WINDS BLOW.
“ I tell you it’s true,” said the city tramp,
At the bitter birth of a wintry day,
As hunger and thirst and ague and cramp
Drove him abroad on the street to pray
To the passers-by who might chance his way.
“It’s here in the town that the wi. d< play queer ;
Out on the plains they are straight and fair;
They blast you in front or sc mr you in rear,
But a man can make re; dy to face them thei e
For he knows where he are, though ever so bare.
“ But here in the city they crawl and sneak
By gable and gate, till a victim nears,
And before he can tuck in his head or speak
They’re down on the back of his neck or ears, /
Or are pinching his eyelids into tears.
“ I’ve stood on the blacksoil plain and said
‘ Come on ! ’ and marched in their teeth all day,
And the heat of my body was fanned and fed,
As the racing blood through my veins made way,
By the vigorous, open, straightforward fray.
“ But here in the city you never can say :
One minute it’s coijy as on you go,
And next through some-.ehink or right-of-way
Sneaks the sinister breath of the South Pole
snow ;
Oh, gimme the plains when the cold winds
blow ! ”
RHO.
Edward Dyson’s book of verses is now
published. It includes all his best Bulletin
work, and is handsomely bound in cloth. The
title is “ Rhymes from the Mines and Other
Lines,” and a big sale is confidently anticipated.
More than any other, Dyson is the miners’poet,
and his verse swings with the pick, has the clash
of hammer on drill. Post-free from Bulletin
office, os. sd.
Down at Warrnambool (Vic.) there is a
chance of -£2O reward for anybody who cares to
excavate an alleged Portuguese galleon, which is
said to have been last seen burying itself in the
sandy sea-shore about 50 years ago. The mythical
vessel is believed to have been wrecked half-a-
century previous to its final disappearance, and
according to aboriginal tradition the crew were
“yellow skinned.” Under these circumstances,
Warrnambool feels convinced that they were
pirates conveying great masses of treasure from a
distant land, and the oldest local inhabitant has a
dim suspicion that the ghost of a desperado with
two unwieldy pistols in his belt will be found
jjacing the main deck when the missing galleon
comes to light.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 26, 1896.
The Kanakaland Stumbling-block.
The failure of Premier Reid’s “ mission ”
to Queensland on the Federation question is
not a matter of much surprise to anybody—
save, possibly, to Premier Reid himself. It
was a small, cheap, common, ordinary
failure with no noticeable features about it,
and it was the natural result of a half-hearted
apostle preaching the gospel he only half-
believed in to a blankly indifferent assem-
blage which was too little interested either to
believe at all or to disbelieve at all. A whole
combination of circumstances led up to this
mission and its failure. The present Govern-
ment in Brisbane is undoubtedly opposed to
Federation, but in the passive, inert fashion
of one who yawns and says that it really
doesn’t matter. Premier Nelson introduced
the Enabling Bill, remarking, in almost so
many words, that it was a small affair which
made him feel tired, and that he didn’t care
what became of it. He roused, in a small
way, the hostility of the masses and of the
Labor members by refusing to entrust the
people with the right to elect the Convention
delegates, and, having arrogated the right to
Parliament, he carefully created a quarrel
with the Council by refusing that half of
Parliament any share in the election. He
aggravated the quarrel thus created by re-
fusing to entertain any scheme of com-
promise, and when the Enabling Bill was
lost, as it was obviously intended to be, the
Government merely yawned among the
ruins and said again that it didn’t matter.
* * * *
There was no necessity after this for any
“ mission,” or any fatted ambassador, or any
fuss whatever. The other five provinces of
Australia and Tasmania were all in line,
having been got there with much difficulty.
They represented four-fifths of the area and
six-sevenths of the population, which was a
sufficiently good basis to start with. No
reasonable person ever counted with any
certainty on starting the Federation with all
the provinces at once. The Canadian and
United States Federations began with a very
few provinces, and grewincour.se of years. The
Swiss Confederation began in a still smaller
way and took centuries to reach its present
dimensions. If a Federation of five pro-
vinces could be started, with a uniform tariff,
railway system, military, postal, telegraph
and other departments, a consolidated debt,
a Federal judiciary, and with no internal
customs barriers, the advantages of the
system would be so great that the adhesion
of Queensland later on is a matter of abso-
lute certainty. To get five provinces in line
at once was so much more than could reason-
ably have been expected that it was a sheer
tempting of Providence to waste time wait-
ing for the one that stood out, with a fair
chance that two or three of the others might
grow cool and fall away in the interim. But
Premier Reid, the lukewarm Federationist
whose mission it is to create imaginary
obstacles and then show his zeal by laboring
ponderously to overcome them, stood in the
way of this very simple and rational view of
the case.
* * * *
Premier Reid, first of all, created the idea
that the adhesion of Queensland was essen-
tial, which it certainly was not. Then he
showed his useless enthusiasm by going on a
mission to overcome the obstacle he had
created, and practically guaranteed the failure
of his mission by professing to speak in the
name of the five provinces without obtaining
any form of authority from any of them.
And, when his mission met with the antici-
pated failure, he commenced to throw cold
water on the movement by vague statements
to the effect that it was necessary to seriously
consider the position before anything further
could be done. This was the first definite
suggestion that the defection of Queensland
should be assigned as a reason for dropping
the whole movement, whereas in reality it
was the best of all reasons for urging it on
with increased energy. The Queensland
Government, in the days of the ’9l Conven-
tion, was seemingly in earnest (the fact that
its earnestness was probably owing in the
main to the private ambitions of Samuel
Griffith makes no difference), but five years
delay gave it, or its Griffith, time to grow
indifferent. The suggestion that this state of
things should be remedied by giving the other
provinces more time to fall away also is the
most inverted Federalism on record. Finally
the Reid Government decided to fix the
Federal Convention for next April, which is
a moderately remote date with no unseemly
haste about it.
* * * *
The various informal suggestions put for-
ward as to the terms on which Queensland
might be willing to come in are so grotesque
that the hopelessness of waiting for the
Kanaka province, in its present frame of
mind, is obvious. One informal demand was
that some arrangement (of what kind does
not appear) should be made to prevent the
trade of Brisbane being drawn away to other
ports. As one great object of Federation is
to destroy internal barriers, and let trade go
to the most convenient port, and as Sydney,
Melbourne, Newcastle, Adelaide, and Perth
all agree to take their chance, and as Bris-
bane will be at full liberty to annex their
trade if it can be got, this one-sided demand
was hopeless and impossible. Another plaint
was that, if the Customs were taken over by
the Federal Treasury, Queensland, which
has a Customs revenue of £2 16s. per head
against £1 Bs. per head in N.S.W. and £1
9s. in Victoria, would be giving more than
her fair share, and therefore that each pro-
vince should maintain its separate tariff and
retain its Customs revenue for its own pro-
vincial purposes. There was really nothing
in the complaint, for Queensland collects a
large Customs revenue merely by levying
heavy revenue duties on herself, and if her
tariff was made uniform with that of the
other provinces her contributions would
gradually decline to about their level ; but
the proposal was a fine instance of grotesque
selfishness. The Northern province is
quite willing to put into the common stock
the biggest debt per head in Australia, and
to federate her interest bill of £2 16s. per
head per annum, the largest in Australia,
with Victoria’s bill of £1 14s. per head, also
to throw in the worst-paying railways (except
Tasmania’s only)and aboutthe most expensive
post and telegraph system, but while hand-
ing over all these debit items she seriously
demanded that her Customs revenue—the
only counterbalancing item in the list—
should not go with them. Also it was
seriously proposed up North that there
should be special conditions made for Queens-
land’s special benefit in the matter of colored
labor. Then a fui’ther proposal was to the
effect that instead of creating an “ artificial ”
constitution Australia should preserve the
existing Federal Council and let it gradu-
ally develop into a complete Federation.
This last idea is just as hopeless as
waiting till a cow grows into a prize
ram or a camel. Things that are dis-
similar by nature can’t develop into each
other. The Federal Council has no autho-
rity, no representative basis, no finances, no
credit, no executive or legislative powers, and
it can’t grow any of these things ; they must
be given to it by provincial enactments, and
to give them would be going back to artificial
constitution-making once more. Moreover,
the Federal Council is withering, not grow-
ing.
* .* * *
The inflated value which Premier Reid
insisted on attaching to Queensland as a
member of the proposed league, has pro-
bably done something to fill that province
with an erroneous belief in its power to make
conditions. Yet Federation can go on per-
fectly well without the assistance of the
Kanaka province, because circumstances
would make its adhesion later on a matter
of almost absolute certainty. A combination
of the other five provinces, as already men-
tioned,. would include four-fifths of the area
and six-sevenths of the population of Aus-
tralia and Tasmania. By leaving Queensland
out the Federal debt would be about £4B
per head, whereas by taking in the Northern
province it would be something like £5O 10s.;
also the interest bill would be reduced by
the defection of Queensland by about 2s. per
head per annum. The Federal railway
revenue would be improved by leaving
out the Queensland system, which is much
less profitable than the average of the other
provinces, and in all these respects the
Federation would be a direct gainer
and Queensland a direct loser. In-
directly, however, the Kanaka country
would lose much more, and in many
ways. First of all it would lose its chance,
whatever that may amount to, of having the
c ipital located in its territory. Its vote in
the Commonwealth Parliament might turn
the scale and locate the Federal city either
within its borders, or so near them as to bring
much wealth and population to Queensland,
and by maintaining its policy of isolation
this chance is necessarily lost. It would not
only lose the opportunity to pass part of its
debt and interest burden on its neighbors,
but would also lose the advantages to be
gained by a consolidation of the public debt
at a reduced rate. Its future loans would
have to carry a higher rate of interest than
those guaranteed by the superior credit of
the Commonwealth, which means an addi-
tional loss. It would miss the further saving
to be effected by a consolidation of the mili-
tary, railway, postal and other departments
—a small item, possibly, but still one that
helps to make up a large and solid considera-
tion.
* * * *
Further, the Commonwealth tariff would
seriously injure its commerce. Its exports
would only have a look-in, so far as the rest
of Australia was concerned, when the other
five provinces were unable to supply each
other, and if. the southern beet industry
ever readies the dimensions expected of it
the surplus of Queensland’s nigger-grown
product will be in much want of a market.
If there was a Federation of all Australia,
and border tariffs and differential railway
rates were abolished, the South Queensland
railways would be in a position to draw some
of the trade of Northern N.S.W. to Bris-
bane, which trade the isolated province
will probably lose. Again, the Federal
Government, when it comes to consolidate
and unite the scattered railway systems of
Australia, must some day face the problem
of building a transcontinental line to open
up Australia from north to south. If Queens-
land joins the Commonwealth that line may
go through her territory, to her great ad-
vantage, and connect her three long
straggling railways, and make the first great
northern port on the shores of the Gulf. If
Queensland stands out, then the only pos-
sible transcontinental line is across South
Australia, and the outlet will be at Port
Darwin. Reckoning in the assured losses
and the ones that are only possible—the
direct losses which Queensland will sustain
now by staying out, and the future ones
which will keep impelling her to come in—
considering how well the other provinces can
do without Queensland and how ill Queens-
land can do without the other provinces,
provided only that they are united, the
future adhesion of the isolated North may
be regarded as an absolute certainty. There-
fore, Queensland’s present defection is
rather a reason why Federation should be
pushed on than a cause for further delay.
Judicially Exonerated.
Some months ago, in the N.S.W. Leg. Assem-
bly, Varney Parkes charged N.S. VV. Public
Works Department with having such friendly
relations with a chameleonic firm of contractors
—mostly known as Carter, Gummow & Co.—
that the interests of the country suffered badly,
while the interests of certain individuals em-
ployed in the Dept, did not suffer badly.
District Court Judge Murray was appointed a
Commissioner to investigate the matter, and he
has reported wholly in favor of the Works
Dept. Nevertheless, although it doubtless had
every confidence in Judge Murray, it must
have been an anxious time for the Works
Dept. It seemed to be the general opinion of
contractors that Carter, Gummow’s connection
with the Dept, had been exceptionally for-
tunate; and Carter, Gummow had given em-
ployment to the son of the Dept.’s engineer-in-
chief, who generally superintended their
contracts; while Mr. Carter had borrowed
£2OOO at 6 per cent, from one of the Dept.’s
engineers, who had occasionally superintended
one or other of their contracts; had lent
£250 without interest to another of the
Dept.’s engineers, who had also occasionally
superintended one or other of their contracts ;
and had lent £6OO without interest to a third
of the Dept.’s engineers, who had also occasion-
ally superintended one or other of their con-
tracts. It must be very satisfactory to these
officers that Judge Murray finds that, “Mr.
Hickson and the other responsible officers of
the Works Dept, stand exonerated from all
suspicion of improper conduct in the matter ”of
enquiry. Then there was the matter of the
bluestone pitchers. Bluestoue pitchers are
handy little stones used as a border for man-
holes in the street sewers, and 255. per cubic
yard is a good price for them. The Works
Dept, wanted about 38yds., but by a little
mistake it only set down 12yds. as wanted.
Carter, Gummow and Co. tendered at
,£5O per yard, and the Dept, accepted the
tender and allowed them to execute the work
and charge some £l3OO more than its value.
One of the officials of the Dept., an objection-
able person named Bagge, seems to have
pointed out that according to clause 16 of the
contract-specifications the Dept! had “full
power” to “order material or work of a dif-
ferent description to that specified ... or
to substitute one class of work for another ; ”
and under clause 17 it had power “ to direct
the omission of the carrying out of any part of
the said works; ” but the Dept, generously
decided to bust the specifications, and the
objectionable person named Bagge was sat
upon with great despatch.
* * * *
It was also suggested that the Dept, might
stop short at the 12yds. which it specified as
wanted, but no ! the Dept, resolved to go the
whole animal, and another 25yds. or so of blue-
stone pitchers worth 255. a yard were duly
supplied and charged and paid for at £5O a
yard. This little transaction might seem at
first blush not wholly creditable to the Works
Dept., but Judge Murray puts it in its true
light:—
The excavation quantities stood in the final voucher as
Nature had arranged them; it was no action, no mani-
pulation, of the department, but it was Mr. Snodgrass’s
perception that resulted in the coincidence of that
arrangement with the contractors’ interest.
The Bulletin hereby passes a stern vote of
censure on “ Nature” for buying blue-metal at
£5O a yard. Then there was the matter of
Bond and Hudson’s contract. Bond and
Hudson tendered successfully for a contract
which Carter and Co. were very anxious to
get. Bond and Hudson’s tender price was
£36,900 ; Carter and Co’s was £2990 higher.
Bond and Hudson’s tender, however, was not
accepted, because the Works Dept. “ found
that it had not enough funds to go on
with the work.” Still, their tender was
recommended for acceptance, and it was
recognised that they had first claim on
the Dept, when the work should be car-
ried out. Mr. Hudson came continually to
know when the Dept, intended proceeding with
the work, and was told it was not likely to
be going on with it for two years. Then Mr.
Carter offered Bond and Hudson £750 cash
to withdraw their tender, and £750 more if his
firm got the contract. Bond and Hudson ac-
cepted; and three months afterwards the Works
Dept, proceeded with the work, and, without
calling for fresh tenders, gave Carter and Co.
the contract at a price £2900 above Bond and
Hudson’s figures. Varney Parkes suggested
that this looked as if the Works Dept, was
privy to the little transaction between Carter
and Bond and Hudson—with a bias in favor of
Carter. But Judge Murray’s luminous de-
cision goes to the root of the matter. He says :
If what honestly appeared to be the best course to adopt
under the circumstances was taken, then no blame
attaches to any officer of the Department.
* * * *
Precisely. If what honestly appeared to be
the best course to adopt under the circumstances
was taken by Butler, then Captain Lee
Weller has only himself to thank for being
revolvered and jammed into a tight-fitting
grave at Glenbrook. Then there were other
matters—the matter of the partners in the
Carter firm whom the Works Dept, accepted
as independent bondsmen for the firm’s stability;
the interesting matter of the Monier arch ; the
matter of that other objectionable person,
Inspector Reid, who was bounced from Car-
ter’s contract apparently because he made
himself a nuisance in prying into the proceed-
ings of Carter’s workmen; the matter of
the cavity 7ft. 6in. long, 3ft. wide, and Ift.
deep—supposed to be packed with cement
at 10s. per cask—and the other cavities, or sus-
pected cavities, found in Carter, Gummow’s
North Shore sewer-contract; and a host of other
matters which there is no space to elucidate.
Some of these matters had certainly a queer
appearance ; but Judge Murray conclusively
showed that the charges based upon them had
very little in them. The Bulletin willingly
subscribes Is. 6d. in order that Judge Murray’s
bumps may be felt by a competent phrenologist.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Maoriland Referendum.
The national vote on Prohibition, which was
taken a few days ago along with Maoriland
general election, was the second instance within
recent times when the Referendum has been
adopted in Australasia. The other occasion
was in S.A., a few months back, when the
Bible-in-Schools issue was submitted to the
electors in exactly the same fashion. In each
instance the Referendum distinctly refuted all
the prophecies which Australian Toryism lias
made concerning it. There was nothing dis-
tinctively “ un-English ” about it, or if there
was, the un-English characteristics didn’t come
to the surface, and for that matter it would
have made very little difference if they had.
It didn’t lead to any revolutionary upheaval,
or result in any rash or “ experimental ” legis-
lation, or shake the pillars of the constitution,
or place the power in the hands of the lowest
elements of society, or do anything else that it
ought to have done, according to the frequent
jeremiads of the Eat Man. On each occasion
the popular vote was distinctly a Conservative
force ; in went in the main for keeping things as
they are, which is the principal feature of the
Tory policy. Also, it tended to preserve the
greatest and most beautiful feature of the Con-
stitution—Government by the majority—which
was otherwise in danger of being lost, and,
therefore, it would seem that the Referendum
is more strictly constitutional than the
Constitution itself. In S.A. the Bible-in-
schools party, by sheer vociferation, almost
managed to pass itself off for a majority, or, at
least, a very powerful and increasing minority.
Parliament might in time have given way to
it under the impression that it really was a
majority, it being impossible, under the ordin-
ary electoral system, to sort out the issues, if
the Referendum had not put the matter to a
definite test and shown what a small and cheeky
fragment the Scriptural crowd really was. In
Maoriland the Prohibition party was also, by


sheer assurance and noise and self-assertion,
getting on well towards passing itself off as a
majority till the Referendum put it in its right
place. The Bulletin advocates the Referen-
dum because it believes that the majority should
prevail; that if the majority votes for, or is
too tired to vote against, a bad system
it ought to get that system, and that
it should have every facility for making
its political circumstances just as bad or as
good as it deserves they should be. And
at the same time it believes that the Fat Man,
who regards the popular vote as a scheme of
red revolution, is suffering from visions. The
mass of the community is probably more apa-
thetic than the Fat Person gives it credit for.
It doesn’t want change for the mere sake of
change ; it isn’t absolutely crying for new fads
simply because they are new ; in some respects
it moves a great deal more slowly than its Par-
liamentary representatives, and the Referendum
would probably be a brake upon the Legisla-
ture quite as often as it would be a motive
force. At all events the experiments that have
been made in these provinces go to show that
the Referendum is quite safe—it is a perfectly
peaceful and rational affair, which goes to work
methodically, and does none of the dreadful
acts of smash and Vandalism ascribed to it.
And curiously enough the Fat Person is never
tired of pointing out, and truly enough, that in
Switzerland, the home of the Referendum, it
lias been quite as often a conservative influence
than any other kind. And then having thus
demonstrated that it is perfectly safe, and that
it doesn’t result in a general rush after every
new delusion, he denounces it furiously on the
ground that it would lead to general upheaval
and break the Constitution into small dust.
The Fat Man is a sadly illogical person in many
ways. ' /
4 /
To the Fat Men of Australia. /
The Australian Fat Man and the National Ass
and the Patriotic League and the Leg. Councils
and all the other property-representatives of
this great country are a tolerably comprehen-
sive fool, looking neither before nor after. The
Fat Man crowd has one big chance now if it
could only realise the fact and grab its oppor-
tunity with both hands. Its chance lies in
becoming the Anti-borrowing party. The
weak point of the Democrats in several of the
provinces is that they are content, in too many
cases, to drift along with the old borrowing
disease and let the consequences take care of
themselves. The Fat Man crowd arouses no
public sympathy when it wails about the
wrongs of Proputty, but if it piedged itself up
to the hilt that, when it came into power, it
would do no borrowing whatever except for the
renewal of old loans, it would attract the votes
of almost everybody who has any definite
idea as to where Australasia is going
to. Also, it would have a tangible policy
which it much wants ; it would attack
its enemy on his weakest point and make a bad
exhibition of him ; and it would be working for
its own interests—if it was only capable of
realising the fact. It is the interest bill on
borrowed money which creates the difficulty of
making ends meet ; and it is this dif-
ficulty which causes Parliament to levy
land and income taxes and other things
that make the Fat Man have bad dreams at
night. Further, the more money is spent now
for non-productive works the bigger the interest
bill to be met out of taxation in the future, and
the more land and income tax will follow. If
the Upper Houses of Australasia would re-
morselessly throw out every item of every
Loan Bill which provided for expenditure
on works not yielding direct revenue, they
would furnish good reason for their con-
tinued existence as a cheek on the foolish-
ness of the people’s representatives, and would
do a great deal to avert their approaching ex-
tinction. Also, they would make it possible
for the era of Fat Man Government to come
back again ; in fact, they would make many in-
telligent Democrats feel inclined to try a spell
of Fat Man administration as an experiment.
It is a lamentable essay on the Conservative in-
telligence that it can’t see the weak point in its
enemy’s administration and avail itself thereof.
Every other party except the absolute anti-bor-
rowing party has been tried already ; this is the
one chance for a new crowd to start with a new
battle-cry, and, if a really strong leader arose,
and opened his mouth to a diameter of eight
feet, and let out the whoop of the new party in
accents that would rend the heavens, it ought
to be the beginning of a political revolution.

Anothur Public Complication.
The N.S. Wales Civil Service law has got still
further tangled up. Under the old system a
retiring civil servant was entitled to a gratuity
of a month’s salary for every year’s service;
this was part of his recognised contract with
the Government. When the new C.S. Bill was
introduced in the Assembly Premier Reid drew
special attention to the fact that this right was
carefully preserved ; the only difference was
that it was no longer compulsory to contribute
to the Superannuation Fund. As the Superan-
nuation arrangement simply means that a C.S.
buys a certain alleged benefit for his own old
age with his own money, the refusal to contri-
bute to the Fund should certainly not carry
with it any penalty, and Premier Reid ex-
pressly stated that it didn’t. Later on
he moved, without assigning any reason,
to omit certain words, and the obedi-
ent Assembly omitted them without asking
the reason or the probable result. Later still,
after the Bill was passed, the same Premier
Reid announced that, in the opinion of some
legal authorities whose names were not given,
the Act was so fixed that every civil servant
who ceased contributing to the Superannuation
Fund thereby forfeited absolutely his right to a
retiring allowance. If this is true, many people
who ceased contributing to the Fund on Pre-
mier Reid’s assurance that they could do so
without any loss have thereby been entrapped
into sacrificing retiring allowances worth, in
some cases, hundreds of pounds. But every-
thing is nebulous and uncertain, and the service
is in blank ignorance of its own whereabouts.
The questions it wants answered are seven
ia number—(l) Was it the omission by
Premier Reid of that one clause from
the Bill which cancelled the retiring allow-
ances ? (2) Was it omitted with that end
in view ? (3) If the clause did not
make the difference, then the Bill itself was
never what Premier Reid claimed that it was,
and in that case was it misrepresented by sheer
stupidity, or how ? (4) Who are the legal
authorities who say that the retiring allow-
ances are forfeited ? (5) If they really are for-
feited, does the Govt, mean to take a mean
advantage of this accidental injury (it must
have been accidental after Premier Reid’s
assurances), or will it put the matter right ?
(6) Is there any intention in the matter to bull-
doze civil servants into further contributions to
the Superannuation Fund by thus penalising
those who withdraw, and thereby to keep the
Fund on its staggery legs a little longer ? (7)
Supposing a C.S. keeps on contributing to
the Fund so as to preserve his compen-
sation rights in case of retirement, and
instead of retiring he hangs on until he is
superannuated in the regular course, and
then his pension goes bung through the insol-
vency of the Superannuation Fund, and he is
left with neither compensation nor pension,
how will that pan out ? (8) And, in the face of
all this, wouldn’t it be best to wind up the
miserable Fund as quickly and equitably as
possible, and abolish all retiring allowances so
far as concerns future members of the service so
as to make the allowance difficulty as short as
possible, and simply make every public servant
insure his life in a State Insurance Department,
so that he will have some chance of knowing
his own whereabouts more or less ? These
questions are all the more pertinent inas-
much as the Government has just taken
two eminent legal opinions (apparently not
' the same opinions alluded to above) as to what
it means by its own enactment; and one emin-
ent legal authority says it means one thing
while the other says it means almost the exact
opposite. Also, each says that the Act is so
hopelessl) r confused and stupid and muddle-
headed that his opinion is practically worthless,
one view of the case being about as probable as
the other. And this is how lawyers make law.
+
Dibbs, ex-Republican.
Dear old George Dibbs arose the other day at
a meeting of the N.S. W. Bankers’ Institute,
and inside balf-an-hour furnished all manner of
good reasons why no constituency in Austra-
lasia should ever again send that lengthy poli-
tician to Parliament. There was a time when
a man named Dibbs used to declare that he was
a Republican. This is a sample of what a man
named Dibbs, K. C. M. G., declares to-day :
Were they prepared to make the huge sacrifices which
would be necessary for the benefit of the other colonies?
At the time of the original convention the people boasted
what a glorious thing it would be to have a flag of our
own. Such a thing would be impossible. Our flag must
be the British ensign. (Hear, hear.) The desire under-
Ivin i the question of federation was eventually to cut the
painter. (Cries of “ No, no.”) He had no hesitation in
saying that what was wanted by most of those who advo-
cated federation was at once to set up a kingdom of our
own. (Cries of “ No, no.”) That a population of four
millions of people not one million of bread-winners—•
should have the audacity to propose to set up a flag of
their own and become an independent nation was too
ridiculous to be seriously entertained. They would
be the sport of other nations within comparatively
a few hours. ... In these colonies they were
over legislated for and over governed over legis-
lated for because they had the curse of payment
of members. By the creation of the Federal Par-
liament as proposed by the Commonwealth scheme they
would be creating another set of hungry political loafers
to be provided for by the working bees of this country.
As long as they had payment of members they wouid
have confusion and dilliculty. . . In any case, New
South Wales, the wealthiest and soundest of the colonies,
would have to make the greatest sacrifices. He felt that
New South Wales would be able to borrow as advan-
tageously as federated Australia. The combined colonies
wouid only be able to borrow more cheaply because the
loan would be endorsed by New South Wales. What
Queensland would like to destroy was the enormous in-
fluence which the Labor-party exercised over her politics.
By creating a sentimental union to-day they would be
taking a step which in the end would bring about a
general break up and general bloodshed.
This is the new Dibbs —the parochial, conser-
vative, imperialistic Dibbs, K.C.M.G. And
the logic of the new Dibbs, K.G.M.G., is
pitiable reading. There is nothing wrong with
payment of members, save that it helped to
bring about the downfall of Dibbs, whose ser-
vices the people apparently regarded as good
enough to be accepted gratis, but not good
enough to be worth paying for. There is no-
thing wildly absurd about Australia starting as
a nation with a population of four million souls ;
about half the independent states of the world
contrive to struggle through under much the
same circumstances, and unless the Australian
is a very poor, mean creature, and there is no
fight at all in G. Dibbs’ National Guard, this
country might do the same. The idea that all
the nations of the earth are hungering to possess
this plain, ordinary, drought-cursed land and
to annex Jamberoo and Booligal is probably
quite erroneous. Also there- doesn’t seem to be
much in Dibbs’ tale of the special glories of
N.S. Wales. That province may be able to
borrow as cheaply as a Commonwealth would,
but, so far, it hasn’t been able to borrow even as
cheaply as Westralia. There is nothing behind
the theory that N.S.W. would have to
make “great sacrifices” for federation; at
least, no one has ever shown what these
sacrifices are. There is nothing, save the
assertion of that Dibbs, who used to be a repub-
lican, behind the statement that Federationists
are necessarily disloyal and want to up-end the
blessed Throne ; loyalty or disloyalty has
nothing to do with the matter. Anyhow, no
Australian disloyalist ever proposed founding
a “ Kingdom ” of our own, and Dibbs the
First, as a prospective monarch, is yet un-
dreamt of. There is no evidence, save
the unsupported statement of the man who
says he didn’t damn Chicago, to show
that Federation must lead to disaster and
the shedding of nose-blood. The declaration
that Queensland yearns to get rid of the incubus
of the Labor-party rests on the ipse dixit of
the long, splashy orator, and on nothing else.
The new Dibbs, K.C.M.G., is a truly awful
example of the decadence which comes of sell-
ing one’s soul for a gewgaw and a big cigar.
Other K.C.M.G.’s have “ decaded ” likewise,
but none of them have done it so frankly and
openly and shamelessly and suddenly as G.
Dibbs, who now advocates disunion on the
ground that it will keep Australia weak
and dependent on England. And yet Dibbs
was, or thought he was, some kind of a genuine
republican—at least this paper believes so—
until the big cigar tempted him and he fell, and
what a fall it was !

A Lost Opportunity.
The Board appointed to inquire into the disor-
ganisation of Melbourne Post and Telegraph
Department, and the alleged orgies and high-
kicking carnivals which went on there, and the
general aspect of wassail which pervaded the
place, has presented a report of weak white-
wash. It suggests feebly that the official
engine-driver should be a man with the “ same
qualifications which are necessary for the same
position outside the service.” In other words,
the Government shouldn’t break its own laws
by employing an uncertificated man, and
thereby run needless risk of the boiler bursting
and lifting the building into the air. The Board
also says that there is an “ atmosphere of dis-
loyalty ” about the establishment, but doesn’t
saddle the responsibility upon any one ; and
it is “surprised ” that the Deputy Postmaster
didn’t know of the drunks and the high-kicking
and the general atmosphere of Flossie which
filled the department. It doesn’t go so far as
to hint that if the D.P.G. had been reasonably
competent lie would have known about these
matters, and that the State ought to get a man
who would know ; it is only “ surprised.” The
wine and woman aspect of the case, the awful
frauds which seem to have been perpetrated on
the Government, the wholesale disappearance
of property, the system of giving most of the
orders to one favored firm at apparently about
three times what the work was worth, the
stealing, swindling, back-biting, “tip” and
general incompetence, the leaving of important
work to casual laborers while the bosses leaned
up against bars, the system by which no one
even knew what stores were on hand, the irre-
sponsible way valuable State property was
habitually “ chucked down ” in a yard or any-
where and left to rot and be forgotten—all
these things are left out. They always are
in Melbourne. And in hushing up the ugly
scandal the Board lost its chance of pro-
ducing a really interesting document. Its
report, if written by a man of real literary
capacity, should have been a bacchanalian docu-
ment with vine leaves in its hair—a publication
with wine and song and love and Tottie in it,
and stories of ankles and high-kicks and stock-
ings and silver-buckled shoes and warm, round,
white arms hugging the postal official during
office hours. And in the background of the
picture should have appeared the grim, metho-
dical German contractor pegging away at
charging three times as much as the work was
worth, and the doubtful engine-driver sitting
on the safety-valve in the basement. There
were great possibilities about that report, and
it panned out for nothing but weak apology.
Epileptics in Insane Asylums.
With reference to the horrible mediaeval prac-
tice in N.S. Wales and some other provinces
of shutting up epileptics in lunatic asylums—
without occupation, without amusement,
without distraction, though perfectly sane
and rarely dangerous even in their occa-
sional paroxysms The Bulletin has re-
ceived this letter from the mother of an
epileptic patient now in an asylum near Sydney.
The case will easily be recognised by many,
and the lady’s name will be made known to the
Col. Secretary if desired :
Editor Bulletin, —The statement of “ Medicus,” in your
issue of Oct. 31, that it is “ an accursed outrage ” to im-
mure epileptic persons in insane asylums is full of bitter
truth for me. My son, when four years old, was kicked
behind the ear by a horse ; he lay unconscious for some
time, but, under medical care, recovered. In time, he
went to school, and grew up very smart, healthy, and
very strong. When he left school he was employed as
invoice-clerk in a very well-known wool-store at the Cir-
cular Quay, for over four years ; he was a member of the
School of Arts, and received a first prize for shorthand at
the age of 15. Was clever in all gymnastic exercises, and
a good swimmer and diver, having, before many on-
lookers at Circular Quay, jumped in and rescued a
drowning girl.
When between 16 and 17 he had his first epileptic
attack, and for some years they always came in the night,
and he would be quite right all day. First I took him to
Dr. Sydney Jones, who said the injury to the head was
the cause, and recommended three courses of treatment—
the last being an operation to remove the depression of
the skull. I asked why the fits did not come on before ;
the reply was the brain had not grown sufficiently to
come in contact with the skull. In course of time it was
found necessary for my son to resign his position as clerk,
following the advice of Drs. Jones, Manning, the late Dr.
Fortescue, and two others, mental work not being con-
considered good for him. His employer regretted his
resignation, and gave him a cheque, with good wishes for
his future career.
Some time was spent in medical treatment, until it was
decided to send him to Alfred Hospital for the operation.
After 13 weeks as an inmate, the doctors thought the case
not bad enough to risk his life for ; so, with the advice of
Dr. Manning and others, he was kept to assist Mr. Jesse,
who I think at that time was secretary in the hospital.
There he remained, being also employed in the dis-
pensing at times. Then the hospital was found to be
heavily in debt; expenses had to be lessened, and a
great many of the employes were discharged—among the
rest, my son. Next year, he again went to be operated
on, but the operation did not stop the fits.
Since then he has gradually got worse, and has been
supported by his family, who have done all they possibly
could to keep him. He was twice in the asylum, and with
great effort, his brothers obtained his release; and for
nearly four years we kept him as happy as we could, until
one day he took a fit in William-street, Darlinghurst. A
crowd soon gathered, and the police dragged him to the
watchhouse, thinking he was drunk. On finding himself
in a strange place he became violent, and made desperate
efforts to escape. ■ Next morning, being Saturday, he was
remanded until Monday, then again brought up before
the Court, where it is the custom to have two doctors to
certify as to insanity. One of them, Dr. Goode, recog-
nised him as “a fine, smart young fellow,” He was sent
to the asylum.
He is quite sane between the fits ; in the prime of life,
and willing to work, yet he has nothing to do from one
week’s end to another. If in the well-swept yard he can
pick up a bit'of anything to pick his nails with that is his
life's occupation. Could any sane man stand such a life ?
He has said to me, “Will the gate ever open for me,
mother?” What could I answer? There are notices
hungup: “ You are required to look cheerful ” —which I
try to do, though my heart is torn. He told the doctor
he wanted to earn his own living, and I have politely
asked on many occasions might he have some work to
relieve the monotony of his existence. The answer is :
“He is subject to fits.” Then might he have a little
weeding in the garden ;he would want no tools. Reply :
“He would want tools for that.” Then might he
paint? He can do that very well, and used to work
with his brother, who is' in the building trade.
Answer, “ No.” Might he have a game of cricket ? “ No,
he takes fits.” Yet he always played at home, and loves
it, and I say it would not make'the fits come on any
oftener. lam told I must not interfere. There are a
favored few who go into the cricket-ground, hut he never.
His hands and arms are becoming weak for want of exer-
cise, yet he must never hold a bat. As “ Medicos ”
says, as I say : “ Far better kill him than put him
where he is to fret away his existence.”
When first I had occasion to go to the asylum I met a
very old lady who said she had come to see her boy. “ I
call him my boy,” she said, “for is what he was
when he came here, though now he is 45 ; and how
thankful I would be if some one would give him a dose of
poison, that I might know the end of him.” A shudder
passed through me, but I was not long until I thought
the same. I would be glad to go and see my son hung
so that his hopeless life might be ended. He is worse oft
than the prisoners in the gaols, and the others are like
him, for no fault of their own—with nothing to employ
their minds. Many years ago, as a child, I remember
prisoners working on the roads, and on their heads
they would have six or seven cabbage-tree hats,
which, if they could sell, would buy them some
little luxury. Why is some similar occupation noj
found for epileptics? My son can make door-mats ; he
used to make children’s barrows and a lot of other things,
and he is only one out of a great number similarly miser-
able.
His fate is far worse than death. I have been keeping
hot a grown-up son’s dinner, awaiting his home-coming,
and received a telegram to say he was drowned ; but that
grief has passed away. This is the worm that never dies.
I lie down in tears, and get up in tears. I know a great
many suffer as I do, and if only someone would take the
matter up, what an everlasting blessing he would have
from us !— An Unfortunate! Mother.
The statement that Queensland requires special
consideration in the matter of nigger-labor—
that her climatic conditions make the cheap
heathen a necessity, and she can’t be expected
to federate unless there is some special guaran-
tee that the Commonwealth Government will
not interfere with her particular Kanaka—
has been made so often that through sheer
iteration it is beginning to find acceptance.
Yet it is a poor, thin, transparent excuse at the
best. The Northern Territory is equally in
want of a guarantee that the Commonwealth
won’t expel Chinamen ; if it did the Territory
would be practically depopulated. Also the
Territory and the northern part of Westralia
have practically the same climate as North
Queensland, and are just as much or as little
in want of nigger-labor to give their industries
a start. The summer climate of N.Q.,
again, is very little more dreadful than that of
north-western N.S.W. Moreover, if Queensland
requires special concessions because it is hot,
S.A. and Westralia are equally entitled to ,
them because they are dry ; these two are the
most droughty provinces of this continent,
and their right to demand special guarantees in
the matter of irrigation works is quite as good
as Queensland’s to demand assurances as to the
continuance of its Kanaka. In reality there is
nothing special in the situation of Queensland,
and there are no special points about its nigger
question, even assuming that the Kanaka is
necessary (which he isn’t) to the Queensland
sugar-industry. As things now stand, Federa-
tion offers very fair terms to the Northern
planter. First, it might not abolish the
Kanaka at all. Secondly, it certainly would
abolish the interprovincial sugar duties, and
give Queensland the free run of Southern mar-
kets, and this item should be worth all the
Kanaka labor in the north. As Queensland
would go into the Federation with a possibility
of keeping her Kanakas as well as a certainty
of getting the markets of the other Australias
opened to her, and gaining a valuable prefer-
ence over foreign competitors, it is a very fair
gamble. The demand of the North that it
should get the run of the Southern markets and
receive special cheap labor concessions as well
is a huge aggressive grab which is beyond all
reason.
Last week Vic. Leg. Assembly concluded its
enquiry into the reconstruction of the Com-
panies Act Amendment Bill by Sargood’s
special committee, and the Bill w r ent back to
the Council for further insult. All, or nearly
all, of the alterations inflicted upon the original
measure in the Upper House were “disagreed
with.” The political stomach of the Lower
House (and the L.H. in this matter represents
the stomach of bank depositors and shareholders
generally) seems to revolt against the “ whole-
some legislation ” which Sargood’s committee
recommended. On the other hand, the Sargood
and his following are unanimously resolved to
block financial reform wherever it threatens
danger to their private interests—especially
W’liere it interferes with the right of directors
to lend themselves the shareholders’ and
depositors’ money. Therefore the Companies
Act Amendment Bill is not worth talking
of again, except as a casus belli. It cannot
be passed in a respectable form ; it never was
expected to pass, and unless Premier Turner
intends to use it for lighting a constitutional
fire the document is merely a record of idle
debate and wasted time. Victorians have suf-
fered much under the bad company-management
which this Bill would preserve them from in
the future, but up to now there is no indication,
outside Parliament, of the victims caring a
button whether they get preserved or pickled.
Change the scene to England—imagine the
House of Peers fighting on behalf of financial
jobbery and corruption, whilst the community
was still suffering the after-effects of a crisis !
The community would at least amuse itself by
making a noise, especially on Sundays.
Dear the youth of 19 and the
girl of 16 who became the possessors of an un-
welcome infant, the boy repudiating responsi-
bility by urging age of consent and his parents’
veto. Doubtless in this, as in most cases of
the sort, the real issue was, “ Shall the boy’s or
the girl’s parents support said infant, the young
people themselves being out of the question? ”
Now, marriage would throw the onus on the
boy’s parents, non-marriage on those of the
girl. Nature takes car e that the female, married
or unmarried, shall do her share towards
the reproduction and preservation of the race.
This share frequently prevents her in a civilised
state of society from supporting herself or her
infant. Society has decreed in marriage that
the husband and father shall provide. Why
not the same when there is no marriage ? If it
is no hardship for a married man to suppoi t
his children, it is surely none for an unmarried
man ’—provided, of course, that his fatherhood
is proved. In the case on hand, it was no more
unfair for the boy’s than the girl’s parents to
lift the heavy end of the log, but the former
escaped through the gate of the marriage “ age
of consent,” while the other side tried to get
even by means of the W.C.T.U. “age of con
sent.” The social question will never be fairly
settled till every child is legitimate and able to
demand a father’s support.— Warra.

Lew Din, of Melbourne, a heathen laundry-
man, fined for washing and ironing when he ought
to have been in bed, or for some other such offence
against the Factories Act, would seem to be a
quite up-to-date person. He cursed the “lallikin
Parliament ” for making a “ lallikin bye-law,’’and
was ultimately led out of court shouting—“Me
pay fine? No damfear ! Take it out in plison.”
This is civilisation, or next door to it.
The N.S.W. National Shipwreck Relief
Society is working on wrong principles altogether.
At the annual meeting last week Gov. Hampden
was put up to complain that big merchants and
others didn’t come forward with subscriptions as
they ought to. Who is going to subscribe to
swell a fixed deposit of =815,000, which is what the
Society now boasts of ? President Storey seems
to think it is the Society’s business to accumulate
large funds and dole out small charities. Last
year the Society could only find ,£705 worth of
distress to relieve, though, as Gov. Hampden
said, 078,824 seamen passed in and out of Sydney
alone in ’94. It may have been that there was
wonderfully little distress, or it may have been
that the Society didn’t want to relieve distress if
it had to break into its precious .£15,000 at fixed
deposit. The President says the Society is saving
up for a big disaster. But the public can always
be relied upon to subscribe liberally for a big
disaster, which hits its emotions hard; it is the
little, every-day, one-man disasters which the
public doesn’t care about that the Society should
relieve—and apparently doesn’t. The Govt, is
still contributing £250 a year to this institution,
though a Society with £15,000 at fixed deposit can
very well do without a subsidy.
