Her Majesty’s (late Alexandra) “The Serpent’s Coil.”
r lhe difficulty between the two stout
gentlemen re the new Palace Theatre raged more
or less intermittently right up to the opening
night. The larger and more commodious of the
two was last seen advertising that the newly-im-
ported co. would appear on Saturday under his
management at Sydney Opera House. But when
Saturday came the Palace opened in due course,
and the dingy temple in King-street, above the
public-house and adjacent to the cheap banana-
shop, remained as usual, deserted and given up to
the ghosts and the echoes and the casual rat sit-
ting in the moonbeam. Also, Arthur Garner’s
advt. disappeared about this time from the papers,
and it is understood that the future combat, what-
ever it is about, will rage in the law-courts per
medium of the usual 6s. «d.
« » * *
The Palace Theatre opened with a loud
bang. It is a small place, but it is probably the
most gorgeous theatre on earth, or thereabouts.
There is such a vast mass of decoration about it
that only a very bloated capitalist will be able to
keep it in complete repair. If it is ever allowed to
grow old and run to seed, there is so much color
to fade, and so much glitter to fall into disrepair,
and such a lot of art and loveliness to drop into
the sere and yellow leaf that it will be suggestive
of the painted wreck that one meets at the
street-corner after dark, with the grease-mark
on her satin dress and the broken ostrich-
feather in her smashed bonnet. To “do
up” the Palace, when it wants to be done up,
would cripple the fortune of an ordinary capi-
talist. George Adams will probably die of a
broken heart by-and-bye through the effort to
preserve his little bit of Paradise in its original
freshness and glitter—he will die struggling with
a paint-pot to the bitter end in the effort to keep
the moth and the dust and the blow-fly off 14,000
square-yards of fresco and other artistic beauty.
The Palace is very beautiful indeed, but the pre-
sent writer wouldn’t have such a place on his
hands for a great deal.
* * * *
The co. isn’t up to the magnificent charac-
ter of the theatre ; the one is extraordinary, while
the other is, on the average, only respectable.
Some of the performers are brilliant and
unique, but a lot of decent mediocrity
weighs them down. Mademoiselle Clotilde
Antonio is one of the most remarkable acro-
bats The Bulletin ever saw. She only does a
few acts, but they are unusually clever, they are
done smartly and neatly ; there is no needless
delay or padding ; and the performer is a pretty
little woman whose grace doesn’t desert her even
when she is standing on her head and playing a
violin solo at the same time. Knowles is a
comedian who is also far above the average. His
alphabetical joke was done here nine years ago by
that beloved humorist Frank Lincoln, and there
is a misguided tendency to say “ damn ” in quite a
number of his stories, and his condensed state-
ment in verse about a model with nothing on being
found sitting on the artist’s knee is more coarse
than funny. Still there is a vast amount of real
humor about Knowles, and he promises to boom
exceedingly. The three Helevines are also excel-
lent comedians, and the rest of the show is nothing
in particular. Syd. Deane and Henry Weir are
two pleasant singers in evening dress. Miss Lottie
Moore (who should stick to the nigger items and
eschew the serious) is most vivacious and charm-
ing, and her somersault is hereby strongly re-
commended. The Winterton sisters are pretty
and graceful, and dance a nice, ordinary dance
in a nice, ordinary way. Another lady has a
banjo solo and a short frock to her credit,
and about the female tenor the less said the
better. Henry Lee’s personations of great
men are very clever indeed, but it is impos-
sible to extract much soul-thrill from this sort
of thing. Two of the people, by the way, sing
melancholy songs about “ mother. ” Taking every-
thing into account, the Palace co. is a very good
ordinary co. ; it has some extraordinary people in
it, and some others who are so very ordinary that
the general average is one of respectable ordinari-
ness. Some of the ordinary people, however, are
pretty enough to be put in’a picture, which is at
least something in their favor.
** * *
The Potter-Bellew Co. made its last Aus-
tralian appearance on Saturday night in “ Fran-
cillon.” This is the drama in which Mrs. Potter
claims untruly to have gone away in a cab late at
night with a strange male person, and sinned after
an oyster supper ; and it suits her, partly because
there are dukes and other noblemen in it, and
partly because it is the lowest - necked evening-
frock piece in her repertoire. The last recollec-
tion which Australia retains of Mrs. Potter is a
vision of two white shoulders and an alarming
quantity of back appearing from out of a garment
that had no visible means of support, unless the
top of it was attached by some unseen agency to
a balloon in the flies. About 38cwt. of legitimate
vegetables were passed over the footlights on
Saturday, and a much larger audience than
usual applauded vociferously, and insisted on
the curtain being hauled up fifteen times in
all. Apparently Mrs. Potter departs just as
her popularity is really setting in—as for
Bellew, he has been popular all along, and his
vanishment creates a soulful void among the
jewelled dames and demoiselles in the d.c. The
second Potter season at Sydney Lyceum was cer-
tainly much better than the first, inasmuch as the
star didn’t paint herself with such a reckless flush,
and didn’t writhe half so much. In her first
season she ranged from bad to awful ; in her
second she began by being from bad to medium,
and gave signs of further improvement, until, as
Therese Raquin, she surprised everybody by being
really admirable. This was her very best per-
formance, and Tosca, with the vegetable hat and
the bait hanging all round it and the violent
jumps and wriggles, was probably her very worst.
The Bulletin knows of no other actress whose
best and worst are so very far apart, and it bids
Mrs. Potter a regretful farewell as an interesting
gamble, whose uncertainty left the stiffened horse
and the faked bicycle-race absolutely nowhere.
** * *
This week, a Christian community cele-
brates the greatest episode in religious history by
eating and drinking more than it can comfortably
accommodate, and leaving an unusual amount of
banana-peel on the pavement, and swearing witli
more than its wonted fluency, and having many
free fights, and appearing at the police court in
the morning with a blackened eye and a shock-
ingly depraved hat. During all this week the
Drunks’ Train will be more than usually crowded,
and the last man will fall overboard more regularly
than usual while jumping for three ferry-boats at
once and not knowing which is the real one.
Therefore, this beinsr a great and holy festival as
aforementioned, all the theatres break out in an
unwonted blaze of glory. Westmacott’s panto-
mime of “ Sinbad the Sailor,” alleged to be a
specially brilliant production with an enormous
cast, starts at Her Majesty’s to-night (Thursday).
Geo. Rignold’s military drama, “Tommy Atkins,”
erupts at the Royal same night. The Brough
Comedy Co., with two new men, H. R. Roberts
and Percy Perris, and one new lady, Miss Hope
Mayne, of whom many good things are prophesied,
opens Sydney Cri. on Saturday with “Nancy and
C 0.,” a comedy which is wholly new to Sydney.
Charles McCarthy, the American Irish comedian,
makes his advent at the Lyceum also on Saturday;
and J. Cosgrove begins a season of melodrama at
the Gaiety. Altogether there will be seven
theatres and various other casual shows raging in
Sydney simultaneously, and something must
break.
XX X X
Jimmy M‘Mahon, the “Mighty Atom,”
has laid in a series of new cinematograph pictures
for his Xmas outbreak, a needless extravagance,
partly because the old pictures were good efiough
to satisfy anybody, and partly because the average
Xmas patron is too much of a wreck to trouble
about details. Sestier and Barnett’s “Cin.” also
announces a special effort, and Sydney Tivoli pro-
poses to do something more than usually extra-
ordinary in the variety way with the great Chirg-
win in the cast for the first time in Sydney ; and
in fact the lurid glare of Krishmish and the foot-
prints of the Xmas turkey are over everything.
** * *
All the Melbourne playhouses intend to
hang out a banner this Xmas, and the fear that
one or more of them will suffer a bad time is
haunting The Bulletin’s breast like a piece of
pudding. Nobody asked for a re-opening of five
theatres on Boxing Night. Of late the city has
been fully satisfied with three, hence the awful
sadness of the coming situation. Pive showmen
are about to draw lots, and he who draws the
smallest lot must expect to go under.
XX X X
“Matsa, Queen of Fire : or, the Apples of
Isis,” &c., &e., is the pantomime extravaganza
arranged by Bert Royle and manager Williamson
for the Princess’. This vast undertaking opens in
a Mist of Past Ages and concludes with a Flying
Ballet. Incidentally it will illustrate the return
of Rameses 111. to Memphis on a P. and O.
steamer ; also the Nile will rise during the even-
ing in honor of Leoni Clarke, who conducts an
exhibition of trained cats. The “ Djin-Djin ”
company, supported by the Royal Ballerinas, have
been rehearsing “ Matsa ” for many weeks, and
rumors of impending splendour are in the air.
XX X X
Bland Holt, at the Royal, will produce
“ For England,” a melodrama with much horse
and British valor crammed into ten tableaux.
Bland Holt’s army has of late been spoiling for a
fight, so this Yuletide opportunity to rally round
the dear old Flag on the stage will answer the
same purpose as a seasonable assault upon a police-
man in private life. “For England” is due on
Boxing Night.
XX X X
A rival melodrama, “ The Serpent’s Coil,”
will happen at the Alexandra on Xmas Eve. The
Alexandra, by the way, has renounced its old, un-
lucky title and become born again under the style
of Her Majesty’s. A new name, together with a
redecorated interior and a new manager (William
Cosgrove), may change the fortunes of the Haleck.
Anyway, the creepy drama from Sydney ought to
appeal to the neighborhood, seeing' that a number
of serpents recently broke loose at the top of
Bourke-street, and are now understood to be
chasing rats around the northern suburbs.
XX X X
The Opera House bill for Saturday next
includes Coleman, Alice Leamar, the Vaidis
Sisters, and Edgar Granville, a comedian from
’Ome, or some other foreign part, who has not yet
illumined these shores.
X* X X
Frank Thornton, who has brought several
new faces out with him, will start his fourth Aus-
tralian campaign at Melbourne Bijou on Boxing
Night. “ Charley’s Aunt ” was the particular suc-
cess of Thornton’s last visit, and “The Strange
Adventures of Miss Brown,” with himself in the
name-part, promises to be joyfully reminiscent of
the elderly female from Brazil. At all events,
Thornton is a reminiscence that never fails to
raise a profitable laugh.

On Xmas Night the Philharmonic Society
will “ Messiah” at the Town Hall, whilst W. J.
Turner submits a sacred festival to an overflowing
and overfed audience at the Melb. Exhibition
Building.
** * *
Adelina Antonio, who lately renewed her
engagement with the Fitzgeralds’, appears to be
somewhat of a martyr to art on the high trapeze.
The graceful lady lives a life of severe self-denial
that she may not come to sudden death in the net
when she makes her unique back-fall. Rising at
seven o’clock Madame Adelina goes for a brisk
walk, followed by a light breakfast of fish and
fruit at about 8.30. Five hours later she makes an
athletic dinner—a well-cooked steak, fruit, and a
glass of claret—and at 4.30 she drinks a cup of
very strong tea by way of stimulant for the nerves.
From then until the time of her appearance, about
9.15 usually, the artist puts nothing into her mouth,
nor allows many words to come from it. Perhaps
she! takes a turn on the trapeze a while after
breakfast or during the afternoon. If she
be giving two performances a day her
dinner arrangements are altered and modified
accordingly, but, anyhow, on the completion of her
evening task Madame sups, by rule, and goes to
bed always on the safe side of midnight. There is
no joyous lingering into the small hours, no cham-
pagne festivity, for the trapeziste. Off duty
Madame is a sad-looking woman, and if perchance
a friend comments on her seriousness she points
upwards and says, “My smiles are for ze audi-
ence.’’ Performing in mid-air she appears light-
hearted. She is proud of her work, and the
smiles come easy to her until at last she stands
white and weary upon the highest bar. When the
drop to come is much more than 30 yards, as at
Melb. Exhibition Building, her face is a shade
paler than usual. She places her feet together
with careful exactness, stiffens herself, leans
backward as far as her arms will stretch—
and drops. The revolution in the air is cal-
culated to a nicety, with a regard for grace that
testifies eloquently to presence of mind in the
falling body. Madame’s existence depends upon
the timing and management of that somersault,
but she studies effect, like the artist she is, even
at the dread moment. A wonderful woman,
truly. Her performance is most admired by those
who see it oftenest, not by the ordinary unenthusi-
astic Australian who, after seeing it once, blames
himself for having been surprised into applause.
An average visitor seems loth to acknowledge the
full extent of the difference between a second-class
swinger on the horizontal bar and Madame
Antonio in her “ single rope ” act.
At Poverty Point .
Mrs. Potter knows all the tricks. A very big crowd
assembled at Sydney wharf to see her off, and she took
care to arrive very late, thus saving herself a heap of
worry.
A Hobart tram-conductor named Yallance has succeeded
to an English title and a fortune, and has signalised the
occasion by marrying a local damsel of very humble
parentage who used to be on the stage.
Falk’s plans for cinematographing Gov. Brassey and
party in the act of marching up the Lawn at Flemington
were ingeniously laid. Escape was impossible for the
noble baron, so when he found himself roped into taking
a certain course, with the eye of the apparatus gazing
straight at him, he accepted the situation and walked
along somewhat like a cat upon hot bricks, as shown in
the picture.
Regina Nagel has gone on tour in Westralia. She was
strongly advised to stay in Melb. and follow up her
success, while her singing of “Ben Bolt” in “Trilby”
was fresh in the public mind. But a tempting offer was
too much for Regina’s strength of mind. The unwise
virgin is now doing her utmost to ruin a good voice by
singing to barbaric audiences in an open rotunda, which
is darkly alleged to be run in connection with a pub.
Melbourne Opera House is under condemnation again,
unluckily for Rickards. Eight years ago, or more, the
building was said to be in urgent need of reform, but
many tenants have come and gone since then without
exciting wild alarms among the “ authorities.” Probably
it is as sound and safe for crowding purposes as the
average theatre. This time the Board of Health talks of
closing the gallery forthwith, and insisting upon imme-
diate alterations and improvements elsewhere. Yet
Rickards was lately allowed to spend money in “ doing
up” the interior, when he might have been told to post
pone operations until the other job was ordered.
R. : “ Willie Cosgrove, much daring, tackles the awful
Aleck, on a 12 months’ lease, hoping- bv re-christening it
Her Majesty’s to exorcise its Jonahesque spirit. No good.
Boomed. Firstly, the Aleck building is not theatric in
plan, but barn-like, incompact, and apparently specially
designed for the convenience of gallery humorists desirous
of dropping pea-nuts on d.c. bare necks and bald heads.
Secondly, it struggles vainly against the reminiscent
smellfulness of old Stephen-street, into which its stalls
and d.c. entrances open, against the present evilness of
Lilly Boulke-street, which accommodates the other doors,
against the awful scarlet-womanliness of present Lons-
dale-street, which east and west suburbanites must
traverse to reach it. Thirdly, it has no bars. Its only
chance is to heave all seats out of its basement on to the
stage, tier them there as a d.c., turn the well into a per-
manent circus-ring, and run circus and variety all the
year round.”
White-Eyed Kaffir Chirgwin angles enthusiastically off
St. Kilda pier. His haul to date consists of one ancient
bell-topper, one long-dead dog, one defunct dog-fish, a
nail-keg, two boots (odd), three squid, one pair of
trousers, and one 3oz. mullet, over which latter he grows
quite enthusiastic.
Tiie Bulletin is incredibly assured that the wife of an
East Melb. divine called upon Mrs. Bland Holt and im-
plored her to try to induce her misguided husband to
abandon the stage and take to preaching at large. Mrs.
Holt refused this request, on the ground that she pre-
ferred her sinful lord in his original capacity.
A certain actor, who has been missed of late years, is
said to contemplate an early return to Australia and the
flesh-pots of the South. Should he carry out his inten-
tion, a society scandal which has lain in its peaceful grave
for quite a iong time will probably upset its tombstone
and climb out to meet him on arrival. It is rather a de-
cayed scandal now, but still quite good enough to do a
lot of service. The beginning of it was a swell supper ;
and an infuriated chorus-lady who burst in and banged
the provender and the wines about considerably with her
umbrella, and made revelations before she could be sup-
pressed which caused all the adjacent hair to stand on end.
Do Clement Scott’s theatrical criticisms carry weight
with the Hinglish public? The exalted Clement was
loud in praise of “ The Duchess of Coolgardie,” at Drury
Lane, an absurd drama which ran a month or so to pro-
fusely “ papered ” houses. He wrote : “ ‘ The Duchess of
Coolgardie ’ is a success, John Coleman was rewarded with
a triumph.” The play was a palpable failure on the first
night, a free and independent gallery “guyed ” it. Which
fact must be considered to discount the value of the
critic’s assurance that Hilda Spong, in the name-part, was
likewise a triumphant success.
It is part of the perversity of the theatrically-interested
public that it betrays no desire to be informed upon the
matrimonial (and other, if any) relationships existing in a
score of visiting “ co’s,” of even the most stellar brilliancy.
It is only when a Gaiety troupe comes along that it pro-
jects an eager ear in the direction of the gossiperwho can
tell it “ who is married to whom,” and who isn’t married
to—hm ! Is this due to the natural depravity of the
species, or is it that legs and gauze being one pole
(mentally) and lawn-sleeves and orange-blossoms being
the other, a contact in thought is the electrical resultant ?

Block kerb. The most powerful voice ever I heard was
that of a woman. She used to sing al fresco in the
gardens of the wealthy at night, accompanied by two
men on the cornet and brass euphonium pompom. I have

Parliament one of these fine days.”

If there is anything in omens, G. Rignold has plai n I
“ Henry V.” for the last time in Sydney. On the i: .-it.
night of the season Henry fell over a property rock as lie
rushed on to the battlefield, and came down like a crate
of fenders and fire-irons on the stage, full-length. The

pans out.
Melb. demise : William South, a player for whom the
times had long been out of joint. The London thirst for
romantic dramas such as the “ Prisoner of Zenda,” “ For
the Crown,” and “Under the Red Robe,” suggests that
utilities of South’s calibre will again become truly such,
but while the grass grows ! He was a truculent.
villain on the boards, while off them a.benevolent gentle-
man, somewhat reminiscent of Irving’s Vicar of Wake-
field. South was sometimes known as Montague on the
bills, sometimes as Willie. He was Johnny Hall’s brother-
in-law, and the husband of clever Fanny Wiseman.
R. S. Smythe is taking a Christmas holiday in Sydney,
and is for the moment out of harness. Meanwhile Smythe,
jun., is attending to business in London and driving the
weary celebrity for all he is worth.

The Wild-Cat Column.
There has been so much indefinite wail about
the sacrifices Queensland and New South Wales must
make if they federate with the other provinces that it is
. worth while to put the sacrifice in plain figures and see
what it amounts to. As the Federal Government will
levy similar customs and excise duties all over Australia
there is no sacrifice under that head ; if one man or pro-
vince chooses to contribute more customs revenue than
another by using more foreign goods it is inflicting the
loss on itself by its own deliberate choice, and has no
special claim for consideration. In any case, a uniform
tariff will presently bring imports down to about the
same level all over Australia. But, considering the
revenues and charges which each province would put into
the common heap, things are as follows, according to
Coghlan’s latest figures -
Provincial Expenditure Charged to Federal
Government.
The income which each province supplies to
partly counterbalance these outlays is as follows :
Consequently the totals work out thus: —
The deficit to be made good out of general taxa-
tion is, therefore, over £3,932,000. N.S.W., which makes
so much lamentation about its “sacrifices,” supplies the
biggest deficit on the list. Queensland, which feels the
sacrifice so badly that it won’t come in at all, would sup-
ply the biggest shortage per head of the lot; in fact, its
deficit would be almost twice as large per head as the
average. As Queensland stands out, the combined
shortage of the five remaining provinces to be
made good out of the Federal Customs revenue
will be just about £3,000,000, or about £1 per
head of the population. Of the five provinces, Tas-
mania supplies the largest deficit in proportion to popu-
lation ; S. A. the second largest: N.S. Wales (which is
making the most row about the losses it will incur) the
third largest; and Victoria and Westralia the smallest.
In other words, on the present basis of things Westralia
will make the largest cash sacrifice for Australian unity ;
Victoria the next largest; N.S.W. will make the smallest;
while S.A. and Tasmania will make no sacrifice at all, but
will make a profit out of their neighbors. In the end, all
the provinces will no doubt make a profit by economy of
administration through the consolidation of departments
and by reduction of the interest on the aggregate debt,
but taking things as they are just now the above repre-
sents the situation.
* • * •
Last financial year, according to Coghlan
(“Seven Colonies,” page 363), New South Wales had the
biggest deficit (£634,000) in all Australasia, also it had
ten times as big a deficit as the other six provinces put
together. For the same year, also according to Coghlan
(page 409), it spent £559,000 of loan money on unpro-
ductive works which should have been paid for out of
current revenue, a far larger sum than was spent by any
other province. Between the two, N.S.W., in one year of
Premier Reid’s finance, went nearly £1,200,000 to the bad 1
Also it spent nearly twice as much loan money in all as
Westralia, over twice as much as S.A., Queensland, or
M.L., and six times as much as Victoria. And it is just
here that the blessed Freetrade boom comes in.
* * * »
For its last financial year Tasmania pub up a
borrowing record. It spent £113,278 of loan moneys, of
which £2318 went in revenue - producing works and
£110,960 was spent on works which yield no revenue—in
other words, it went to pay expenses which should, by
any system of honest finance, have been defrayed out of
current revenue.
* * * *
Vic. municipal authorities are apparently try-
ing to straighten up and be decently honest. The total
bank overdrafts of Vic. local government bodies in ’93
were £174,000. Now the lean times have made them
swear off building beautiful shire halls, and the aggregate
overdraft is only £73,000.
• * • *
Vic. Upper House has a tenderness for the
insolvent. According to its latest amendment in the
Insolvency Bill the bankrupt is in future to rank as one
of his own creditors, and is to get a percentage, from 6d.
to 2s. in the £, of the dividend. Suppose there is another
land-boom, and a boomer starts, as many of the last lot
did, with £lOO and unlimited cheek, and land-booms on
credit till his liabilities are £40,000, and assets £20,000, he
having blewed or lost the other £20,000. And suppose he
gets 6d. in the £ on the dividend, then he will come out
with £5OO, and thus get a clear £4OO as his reward for
doing his creditors out of £20,000. The small bankrupt
who goes broke through sickness and want of employ-
ment will just get about the price of a beer out of his
dividend, but the stupendous boomer will make a very
respectable thing of it.
* * •
A reason assigned by Vic. Leg. Council for
refusing to pass the clause in the Companies Bill pro-
hibiting directors from lending themselves the share-
holders’ and depositors’ money is that one bank has one
director who does a large business with it, and if the
clause was passed he must either resign or take his busi-
ness elsewhere. Six years ago there was much better
reason to show, for then about 40 directors were doing
a large business with their own banks. But, as the other
39 have since mostly gone smash and smashed their banks
also, the above survivor is now quoted as a lonesome ex-
ample of the advantages of the system whereby the board
lends itself money on its own security approved by itself.
* * * *
The latest income-tax trouble is the demand of
the N.S.W. Commissioners that municipal councils shall
pay income-tax on their debenture interest and deduct it
from the debenture-holders. The councils curse on the
ground that it is none of their business. Also, a lot of
their debenture-holders haven’t sufficient income to bring
them under the tax, therefore the councils will be paying
a lot of taxation which isn’t legally chargeable. Then
either they will have to deduct the illegal tax from the
debenture-holder, and let him prove that he isn’t legally
chargeable and go through the slow process of recovering
the money ; or else the council must recover the money
itself. The Commissioners have made the various coun-
cil clerks responsible for the payment of the tax; if the
council won’t vote the amount, the clerk will either have
to pay it on his own responsibility by misappropriating
the funds and get into trouble, or else be liable to heavy
fine for failing in his duty as an unsalaried tax-collector.
Further, the councils want to know if they can’t be com-
pelled to pay their debenture-interest in full, especially
in cases where the debenture-holder doesn’t come under
the income-tax provisions? and if the case won’t be an
ordinary default, ending in a receiver being put in ?
* * * *
It is practically decided that the long-postponed
Melb.-Collingwood railway will at last be built. The old
railway-builders brought lines from Heidelberg, Preston,
Whittfesea, and Shenley as far as Collingwood, and then,
instead of finishing the remaining two miles into Flinders-
street, they connected them via the dreadful Outer Circle
line with Spencer-street, a distance of about a dozen miles.
And ever since they have been wondering why these lines
didn’t pay. The only way to make them pay is obviously
to complete them, and Commissioner Mathieson has
harried the authorities about it till at last the experiment
is apparently to be tried. Another stump of a railway
breaks off ■short at Fitzroy, and the necessity of either
completing it or tearing it up and selling it for old iron
also sticks out conspicuously.
* * * *
The horseless car will shortly be running for hire
in Melbourne. Five cars are being locally manufactured,
and twenty more are on order. This is the beginning of
the end for the clammy cab and the catsmeat.
* * * *
The Fourth Victoria Permanent Building Society
(Melbourne) shows a profit of t'3520 for the past year;
pays a 2 per cent, dividend (£1917), and adds £1603 to
reserve-fund, making it £4314. Last year the profits were
£2711, and there was no dividend. The year before, the
Fourth Victoria wrote off £28,113 of capital (equal to one-
fourth of the whole), and £5262 of reserves to cover depre-
ciation, therefore the present report shows a great
improvement.
• * * *
The situation of the other banks and financial
institutions in Queensland with regard to the Q.N.
is peculiar. They want it to close that they may secure
its business—its Government business especially, the
Q.N. having little of any other kind that anybody would
envy. They don’t want it to close, for if its vast mass of
properties was put in the market it would make a slump
which would depreciate their securities and everything
else besides. They would like the State to prop it up to
avert the slump ; yet they wouldn’t like the State to do
so, for State interference means State audit more or less,
and the State audit system might spread. There is no
knowing what might happen if the State audit system
once got fairly started. The situation, in fact, is tied in
a hard knot every way.
* * * *
That large and once-prosperous concern, the
Haymarket Building Society (Sydney) reports that things
are so far looking up that it has earned a net profit of £B6
for the past year. This represents about 3s. per cent,
per annum on capital, so it isn’t a very large affair to feel
glad about—at least, to feel unreasonably glad about.
* * » *
The amalgamation of the National Mutual Life
Society and the Mutual of Victoria has been endorsed by
the policy-holders of both societies, and is now practically
accomplished. Now that this good work has been done,
the present should be as good a time as any for the
National Mutual to swear off that dubious item in its
balance-sheet, “ Deferred Instalments of Annual Pre-
miums on Policies in Force, £44,985.” There is room for
a whole lot of discussion about this item, and anyhow,
even supposing the balance of opinion to be that it is
allowable, it isn’t a very realisable asset, or one to be
specially proud of.
* * * •
An Anglo-French syndicate offers to complete
the S.A. transcontinental railway, on condition it gets a
99 years’lease of an immense area of land —S.A. Parlia-
ment having definitely refused to part with the freehold.
It also proposes to employ white labor, to make its rails
at Port Augusta, and to extract the iron from the Flinders
Range. It sounds very well, but, judging by past sad ex-
periences, this is about how it would pan out: The co.
would raise the requisite money on debentures, and, so
long as its directors could have good times with the
debenture-money, it wouldn’t matter to them whether
the railway was built or not. Possibly there is no inten-
tion of building the line, but there must be a scheme of
some kind to furnish excuse for selling debentures. A lot
of local M’sP. would be appointed directors, so that they
would worry Parliament for “ concessions ” to keep the co.
and their salaries going. There would be a constant
demand for concessions. The work would either crawl
along slowly or not at all. There would be costly law-
proceedings and arbitrations, and rows in England about
the brutal injustice of the S.A. Parliament. If the line
was finished the State must buy it some day, after more
law-suits and arbitrations. Long before the 99 years was
up the co., by judicious squeezing of its tenants, would
raise a popular demand that the State should buy back its
lease, in order to release them from its tyranny. If the
year 1996 did find the lease and the syndicate still alive,
it would probably by that time have bought up the Legis-
lature, and would get a renewal till 2095. The land-grant-
foreign-syndicate-raihvay business, every time it has
been tried in Australia, has been a hopeless failure and a
disastrous loss inside of 12 years, and it should be left
alone always, under all circumstances.
« * * *
The Sydney Harbour Collieries Co. has bought
land at Balmain, facing Iron Cove, and has let a contract
to sink two shafts to a depth of 2960 feet in search of coal.
There is reason to believe that coal will be found 2700 feet
down. The work of turning Sydney into a coal city and
disfiguring the beautiful harbor with coal and dross and
ashes and black dust and mullock-heaps starts immediately.
At present the N.S.W. coal-supply so far exceeds the
demand that coal is selling at the lowest prices on record,
and dividends are scarce, and wages are simply abject—
consequently the present is regarded as a good time for
tearing out more coal and making things still worse.
Private enterprise can’t sleep at night for fear it should
fail to tear all the coal out of the earth at once and sell it
for a song in a glutted market, and be thus betrayed into
leaving some of it for the use of future generations. The
new enterprise is mainly English, so that J. Bull’s mort-
gage on Australia still keeps moving. But no one expects
the cornstalk to dig even a decent-sized post-hole without
the assistance of British capital.
* * * *
Vic. railway revenue is about <£sooo a month
better than it was last year. This is the first time that
the great incubus has lifted at all since ’9l.
* * * *
The Federal Building Society reports that it
has got on so far with its liquidation that the unpaid lia-
bilities now stand at only £144,000, while the book-value
of the remaining assets is £373,000. The society has now
paid 7s. in the it', and another Is. dividend is declared.
The final result may not reach 20s. in the £, but on the
figures it certainly ought to. Year before last the appar-
ent surplus of assets over liabilities was £253,000. Last
year it was £232,000. This year it is £229,000, so the
paper value of things still keeps up remarkably well. If
the surplus means anything at all, the Federal ought
never to have gone into liquidation, and only the most
execrable management could have sent it there.
The once prosperous Sydney Omnibus Co. goes
out of business at the end of this year. It has paid no
dividends since April, ’95, and its shares (15s. paid up) are
unsaleable at 3s. Five years ago they stood at 225., but
the depression killed them, and the proposed electric-tram
system in George-street caused rapid decomposition to
set in.
* * • *
The Pinnacles (near Broken Hill), in which so
much cash has been lost, is to be started once more in
January if nothing goes wrong. Half-a-dozen men are at
present engaged cleaning the machinery, pumping plant,
etc. Reported also that the Lady Berry, near the Pin-
nacles, is, at the expiration of labor suspension, to be
worked again with London capital. Poor capital!
* * * *
The trials of the relative merits of the Hancock,
or percussion, and the May or plunge-action jigs, at
Broken Hill, have resulted in the compilation of figures
favorable to the plunger. Whereupon Captain Hancock
objects, and his barrackers object even more strenuously,
that the trial was not a fair one. The principal conten-
tion is that the British-mine people, on whose Hancock
mill the trial of the percussion-principle was made, would
not allow the sieves to be altered. Some time ago it was
stated in this column that the May plant was prepared
bv the B.H. Proprietary officers ; that was before the
trials took place. Fact probably is that professional
jealousy has a little to do with the matter. The Mays
are engineers, not metallurgists. Hancock and his
right-hand man, Greenway, are metallurgists. Conse-
quently, if the Hancock machine was adopted by the Pro-
prietary-well, Greenway would probably have been put
in to build and for a time run the machinery, which
would have hurt some people's feelings. This possibly
did not affect the result of the trials at all; it certainly,
however, gives the Hancock peoples complaints more
weight than they would have otherwise had. The
present writer said in the par. before alluded to that
May’s jigs would probably be found to do the cleaner
work, Hancock’s the more rapid. But on these trials
May’s are said to have excelled in both. Anyway, it is
not a very conclusive test of concentrating machines’
capacity to run them on one particular class of ore only.
Wallaroo and Moonta copper people have begun
work on the Balaclava copper-mine on the outskirts of
the B.H. district, with men imported from the Peninsula
mines.
B.H. South is getting in funds again, and once
more a div. is talked about—whether to induce share-
holders to hang on and resist the offers of the Howell
(Illawarra) people or not this writer doesn’t know. He
used to write with a lot of confidence about the South,
but is tired now ; hardly less tired than the shareholders.
The British is working in from the Junction
North on a good concentrating sulphide body. No mines,
bar the Junction and Junction North, have had better
recent developments.
Chairman Home tells Adelaide shareholders
in the Junction that they have entered into contracts for
the sale of ore which it is estimated will yield a profit of
±172,800 during the next nine months. A shilling monthly
div. will absorb t'45,000, and £17,500 will, it is proposed,
be put into machinery extensions. Concerning this
policy, consider Block 10. It tore out its richer ores in
just the same way, intending to devote some of the pro-
fits to the erection of machinery for the future treatment
of its poor stuff. But when the rich stuff was all gone
shareholders became utterly demoralised, and the com-
pany has had about the worst three years of any big
mine at Broken Hill. In fact, in the end it took a lot of
scraping and the sacrifice of an enormous dump of sul-
phides to furnish the moderate-sized plant which is now
running with only fair success. It’s like a man with a
pot of beef extract, a dry crust, and a tough job, who
spoons out the extract first on the plea that then he'll be
so strong that he will easily be able to get through on the
dry crust afterwards.

Corainba, N.S.W. : “ The manager of Taylor’s
Reward mine has been instructed to give no information
to the press either with regard to the working of the

mine, and will be sure to know how the present co. own-
ing it pull out on the deal.
* * * •
The Homeward Bound case goes badly for
the jumpers, the N.S.W. Full Court ruling that two of
them had been guilty of misrepresentation, and had
fraudulently become possessed of the ground, and that
the others had participated in the fruits of that misrepre-
sentation. All the jumpers were declared trustees of the
co. to the full extent of their interests, and the co. is en-
titled to a reference to the Master-in-Equity to ascertain
the amount of damage it has suffered by reason of the
stoppage of the mine. The bad time of the co. extends
over three years. It paid £19,000 for the mine, spent
£16,000 in machinery, and all this trouble arose simply
because its agent, one F. De Courcy Browne, an ex-
warden and M.P. and now a mining expert in Westralia,
omitted to pay £5 rent, causing the lease to be cancelled.
Yet, in face of such cases as this, no attempt is ever made
to reform the law to make such jumping an impossibility.
Stated that there is to be a Privy Council appeal.

John Howell and Munday have both written
encouraging, but by no means sensational, reports of the
Overflow gold, silver, lead, and copper-mine. Some very
heavy talk has been done, chiefly by squatters, who sel-
dom interest themselves in mining save in an antagonistic
way, but when they do enthuse it is on the top note. The
mine wants plenty development to ascertain what is in it.
The squatters want water-jackets and lots of other plant.
Water-jackets are very fashionable, all on account of the
Cobar tributors. They will make a big spoon or spoil a
big horn with the Overflow.

* * * *
Another big English co., with nearly .£200,000
capital, working an alleged gold-mine in Westralia, has
had a horrible night-mare—the Hit or Miss Proprietary,
near White Feather, formerly known as Hayes’ Find.
When some £35,009 of the working capital had been spent
the battery started to tell its story : 891 tons returned
BS9oz.—and the rest was as usual declared to be in the
tailings. Pro. Nicholas was cabled to report. Since that
clean-up another 300 tons have been crushed for less than
50oz. Not a word has been said about this. Perhaps
time is given Jay Bull to recover from the first shock.
The manager, a well-known Bendigo man, has resigned,
and if the directors act on his recommendation, they will
be satisfied with the first loss and shut up shop.
* * * *
A well-known barracker for the General Gordon
swears that a first crushing, at the end of January, will
yield 2000 oz. from 400 tons. But why only 400 tons,
anyhow ?
* * * *
The manager of the Boulder explains that the
rich assays of sulphides recently wired east were from
isolated patches of ore. The mines around the Boulder
can afford to stick to the truth, but so accustomed are
the newspapers out there to lying about mines that good
shows continue the victims of it.
* * * «
Over two years ago a strong syndicate was
formed at Broken Hill to find and work mines in Westra-
lia. One show at the Ninety-Mile was considered the
“daddy" of the lot prospected, and the Bushman's
United Co. was the result. The mine could not be sold,
and the effect of working the mine is the winding up of
the co.

From Kalgoorlie: “ Rumored that the Per-
severance mine is unable to declare all the gold turned
out in the lirst crushing, owing to the return being sensa-
tionally high.” How very considerate of the feelings of
shareholders ! So many poor returns from first crushings
in Westralia are concealed and manipulated, and share-
holders have survived the shock, that it is a pity the full
result of this one was not declared, if only to see how
shareholders would stand it.
* * * *
Let us all hope the manager of Queensland
Menzies’ mine, W.A., will try and keep a little ice under
his head. His last wire, posted on all the eastern ex-
changes, reads : “ Had to rough-clean boxes, which were
getting choked with amalgam.” But was the amalgam
itself clean ? If so, then he should have many thousand
ounces before the boxes showed signs of choking. If the
amalgam was dirty—and such are the chances—it should
have been so stated. It is not uncommon in Westralia,
what with manganese in the ore and manganese in the
water, to get ten times as much amalgam as gold.
• * * *
S. F. Van Oss, in the course of a Nineteenth
Century article on Westralia, says the gold-mines are a
palpably unreliable investment. ' The formations are un-
certain and erratic, water and labor are scarce and ex-
pensive, the yields are spasmodic and can never be
depended upon. There are not ten reefs developed to a
depth of 200 ft., yet 70 millions have been sunk, whereof
the promoters have taken about 60 millions. When this
is all realised a collapse is inevitable in this region of
pocketty mines. By-the-bye, is not this the man who had
so much to do with the buying of thousands of Ivanhoe
shares, long before the richness of the mine was generally
known ?

Dr. Holtze, it is wired, has declared that the
Kalgoorlie mines will shortly create a marvellous sensa-
tion in the mining world. He is a Norwegian geologist
sent out by the Venture syndicate, which, by the way,
has some mines that have already created a painful sensa-
tion. The hundreds of outside “cats” are now being
carried on the shoulders of Kalgoorlie, and it has some of
its own. It is a terrible burden, but, like the old man of
the sea, it can’t be shaken off.

If any Australian registry office invited ap-
plicants for employment—say, as housemaids —to
attend on a given day for examination in the use
of dusters, each applicant to deposit a ss. fee, and
if the office accepted 20 applicants, rejecting some
hundreds, but sticking to the fees in all cases,
would not the public howl? Yet this is exactly
what N.S.W. Public Service Commissioners are
now doing in regard to invited applicants for Go-
vernment work.

The partial destruction OI Judge -Hodges,
through colliding with another cyclist at St.
Kilda (Melb.), raises the question whether
weighty,, elderly public servants should be allowed
to pedal into danger. A bikist is bound to come
croppers more or less otten, and his risk ot serious
injury is great or small, according to his age and
weight. The Governmant, paying its Judges a
handsome salary whether they work or lie dor-
mant in bandages, and, furthermore, being liable
for a huge pension in the event of a Judge being
permanently disabled, surely should have some
voice in this matter. Justice on wheels is one thing:
a nine-stone boy “scorcher” is another. The heavy
citizen is ten times more likely to break his leg
or neck than the lightweight with the bicycle
face (whose decease would, in most cases, be a
gain to humanity at large) therefore the Hodges
class of acrobat might fairly be brought within
the Act of Parliament which regulates public per-
formances.

If Lady Brassey’s reading of the vice-regal
law be correct, it would appear that dear Lady
Loch of yore had no right to accept a =£3ooo neck-

away.

One of the Adelaide hospital doctors had a
bad experience in the witness-box. After telling
about the death of a patient from a certain opera-
tion, the case-book was placed before him ; it
showed four operations of that kind for the year
in question —all successful. Being asked where
the dead man came in, he admitted that he couldn’t
tell. Then, after another tale of a corpse, it tran-
spired that the report showed five cases of his
complaint in that particular corpse's year, all five
cured. Invited to locate this remains also, he stated
that it seemed likewise to be missing. Then,
being further invited to state the value of these old
hospital statistics which were used so freely to dis-
credit the new management, he said in effect that
he“dunno.” The inquiry adjourned just then,
and he walked out like a doctor in a dream.

• * *
The Australian
miner is essentially
an adaptive animal.
At Bamboo Creek
(W.A.) the country
is ridgy and shafts
have been sunk on
reefs and then levels
driven to the shafts.
These levels when
abandoned are most
popular residences
with the men, being
much cooler than
iron humpies and also free from insect pests.
Why not be cave-dwellers again?
* * * *
Townsville (Q.) item :
To-morrow afternoon at four o’clock the foundation-
stone laying of the Wesleyan Church at West End will
be performed by Alderman P. F. Hanran, Mayor of
Townsville. In addition to the chief corner stone, Aider-
man McClelland will also lay a memorial stone, and
several other lesser stones will be laid by several ladies
and gentlemen representing the Sunday-schools and some
other societies in connection with the Church. Arrange-
ments have also been made by which any who desire to
do so may lay a brick on the piers in connection with
their offerings on the stone.
Observe the beautiful gradations of the business :
the great man who lays the corner-stone ; the. in-
ferior man who lays the memorial-stone; the
other people who are allowed to lay a smaller
stone apiece ; and the casual people who, provided
they plank down their offerings, are kindly allowed
to lay a brick.

Any youth

is certainly not
for want of op-

the University

receives notice
to attend at a

The doors of the

the candidates
take their seats.
There is no en-

identity, andno
words are spok-

al announce-

nection with
the set subjects
of examination.
There is appa-
rently nothing
to prevent a
“ smart ” youth
from passing
this exam, on
behalf of less brainy individ-uals.
* * * *
In Launceston (Tas.) recently a Scripture-
reader who visited an invalid for the ostensible
purpose of reading to her comforting extracts from
the Bible, and who took the invalid’s loose change
away with her when she left, was brought up in
court and pleaded guilty. Then the law sternly
ordered her to come up for sentence when called
upon. Yet the ordinary person who makes away
with a half-sovereign, and doesn’t aggravate his
sin by reading a chapter to his victim, generally
gets a month right off. In Tasmania Bible-read -
ing is evidently an alleviation of the offence.
♦ * * *
X. : “A coffee palace—the desolate home
of immorality and weak tea.”
* * * *
Tasmania is a mere fly-speck on the world’s
map, but it is a contented place. A big slice of it
has already been given away to a foreign syndi-
cate, and now another and a bigger slice is going
to another syndicate on condition that it makes a
railway which isn’t wanted, and won’t pay, and
which will probably be left unfinished. Tasmania
has a huge Governor who eats and sleeps and
fishes, and does little else except exist in a state of
great circumference. It is overrun with China-
men and nigger hawkers, and now and then the
island wakes up to a knowledge of these things
and gives a great yawn, and goes to sleep again.
* * * *
What the daily papers are pleased to call a
“ love romance ” was lately fished out of a creek
at Mordiallac (Vic.). The romance was quite
dead and very muddy, and there were 300 love-
letters, all from the same girl, in its pockets. It
seems that it left Scotland about 1873 to seek its
fortune in Australia, and promised to send for its
“A MERRY CHRISTMAS TO ALL!”
(His little joke.)
best girl and get married as soon as there was a
home ready. Then the poor, beery romance drank
steadily for 23 years, and in between its drunks it
wrote letters to the girl in Scotland, in which it
swore-off the drink with painful regularity. And
every month, or oftener, for three-and-twenty
years the girl in Scotland wrote back saying how
glad she was that her lover had at last given up
the drink, and pointing out that if he only kept
straight the long-expected home would soon be
ready and they might be happy after all. Finally
the romance, in his usual state of beer, wobbled
one night into a creek, and probably the dead
weight of the faithful girl’s letters helped to drown
it. As the home was no nearer being ready than
it was in ’73, this was, perhaps, as good a way out
of the difficulty as any other.

A Sydney constable, who lately marked
some money for detective purposes, admits that on
arresting the suspected person he found part of
the money, or some money that might
EXIGENCIES OF THE OFFICE.
BUSINESS MANAGER: “By the way, in that notice of old Slither’s death you might
pile it on about the man’s innate nobility of character, unimpeachable
honesty, and so on.”
EDITTOR : “ Why, he teas the d—dest-”
B.M.: " Yes, yes, I know all that; but he has owed us an advertising bill for the
last eight years, and I’m going to send it in again. We don’t run this
paper for our health!”
have been part of the same lot, but a
marked half - crown was not accounted
for. Just so. It is a notorious fact that
almost all policemen, when laying the old, stereo-
typed trap, mark coins in almost the same way,
and that not one or two but scores of such coins
are at present in innocent circulation. The
danger of this is obvious. If Ann Smith or Bill
Jones falls within police purview and be found in
possession of one of these coins the weaving of a
police web around her or him will be a work of
little trouble. When such coins are used to sheet
home a case, the court should satisfy itself that
the marked coin was marked for use on that par-
ticular occasion, and was not one of the fugitive
marked coins which come to the trader in the
ordinary course of business.
* * * *
Any good Australian democrat desirous of
studying the effect of the presence of a titled per-
son upon a local community should visit Sunbury,
Vie. There a condition of things prevails almost
analogous to that existing in waybaek, one-horse
English villages, where the “common people”
abase themselves continually before some purple,
plethoric scion of “the great family.” At Sun-
bury, Swilliam Clarke has his country residence,
and throughout the district the Australian baronet
and his family are the objects of one eternal
grovel. The smallest cowboy of the largest public
dignitary goes, about prepared to flop at any
moment should any member of the Clarke family
chance along. And seeing that one or other is
always chancing along the flop becomes very
monotonous.

• * * •
One day recently three
policemen filed their schedules
in Melbourne. There was no-
thing of that kind in the boom
days, when there were old,
affluent, hazy gentlemen loaded
up with champagne to be found
in the streets almost any night
after the banquet, and thought-
fully assisted home. The old,
bell-toppered gentlemen don’t
stay out late now ; there is no
banquet; they aren’t hazy, for
the champagne has run out,
and if they were there is very
little loose money about them.
Consequently the policeman
walks his thankless beat out-
side the insolvent coffee-pal-
ace, and says “ Ichabod ” in
big capitals.

A furniture - time - pay-
ment collector relates that he
called at a house in Woolloo-
mooloo (Sydney) the other day.
A girl child opened the door,
and, before he had time to put
in a word, gasped out with
eager excitement sparkling in
her eyes—“ Oh, please, Mister
man, don’t call to-day. Call
some oder day. Mummy’s
bought a noo baby on time-payment, an’s too
sickerer than anyfing.”
* * * *
When a renewal of the licence of a Melb.
hotel was applied for, the other day, a constable
bearing the name of Bobsein rose and entered a
protest. Blushing all over, Bobsein said the hotel
wasn’t respectably conducted ; when he called there
in “ purshoot of his jooty,” a nice-looking girl
put two warm, white arms round his neck and
kissed him. Bobsein, therefore, thought the
hotel should be suppressed. The bench looked
hard at Bobsein, and evidently wondered whether
he was an anchorite or only a very new member
of the force, but couldn’t sort the matter out. The
idea of a real live-meat “ trap ” objecting to be
hugged by a nice girl was beyond all the bench’s
experience ; in fact, the bench looked as if it
wouldn’t mind having been there itself.

(Sydney Address)—OXFOßD STREET, or GEORGE and MARKET STREETS,
peared in The Bulletin over the name of
“Sappho Smith” was written by the wife of a
Sydney doctor—the number of persons who, at
different times, obtained credit for part or the
whole of the late Miss Wildman’s clever letters
was legion. Miss Wildman was not alone in this
respect. The lady who now writes over the pen-
name of “ Cleo,” though her advent on this paper
is very recent, has already had her identity
jumped by a somewhat well-known Sydney woman
—possibly by half-a-dozen, but The Bulletin
knows of one, at all events. The occasion that
brought her to the surface was a death. A very
few hours after it occurred the aggressive female
in question arrived with that huge note-book
which marks the person whose contributions are
never inserted, and demanded to “ see the body ”
for purposes of society journalism. The Bulletin
regrets to hear that she was permitted to see the
body. And, in case she should be again heard of,
this paper hereby gives notice that no one con-
nected with it is in the habit of viewing bodies,
and any alleged Bulletin pressman or press-
woman who uses the name of this paper as a
means towards inspecting anybody’s deceased
relative had better be treated with ignominy or
any other article that may be handy.

The Great Dan O’Connor examining a
witness re the proposed harbor at Port Kembla
(N.S.W.) :
And now, sir, you assure the committee on your oath
that you are Consul for Chili ?
Yes.
And you are Consul for six other countries ?
Yes.
And now, Mr. Consul, on the solemn obligation of an
oath, is it part of your duty as Consul to report to your
Governments on the magnificent, boundless resources that
Almighty God has vouchsafed to this colony?
Yes.
And do you think, from our great possibilities as a coal-
producing country second to none in the world, that we
shall be able to hold our own in the markets of the East if
sufficient facilities are afforded ?
Yes.
And then the Universe didn’t crack after all.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —The public is repeatedly
bamboozled when its servants fall into disrepute.
Not many months back a Sydney policeman was
proved in court to have severely “dealt with” a
drunken person and fractured one of his limbs.
That trap’s old haunts knew him no more, and
you will excuse me for supposing that he obtained
a merited sack. A couple of days back I bumped
up against him, still in blue, and he explained
that after his trouble he had been quietly trans-
ferred to his present district. In this connection
it is interesting to note that several of the recently-
retired civil servants have already crept back to
Govt, positions, vide that most reliable authority,
the Government Gazette.— T.W.
* * * *
In each of the three Govt, pilot schooners
in Port Phillip Bay there is a preponderance of
Scandinavians in the crew, while the galley is
managed by a Chinese cook. The preference of
the pilots for the yellow variety of Soyer is in one
or two cases attributed to the fact that the Cau-
casian “ doctor ” takes care to do the work he is
paid for and no more, whilst a Chow can be taken
ashore while the boat is berthed and made useful
privately at Govt, expense. Only one Govt,
billet in the land should be open to Chinamen—
the hangman’s—and that shouldn’t exist.

Melbourne is
the paradise of cy-
clists,from a Sydney
point of view.
Broad,' smooth
streets, almost level,
no loose metal, no
killing hills, and a
cooler climate make
it perfect both for
the machines and
the riders thereof.
Standing onPrince’s
Bridge on a recent
Sunday morning,
the writer saw what
he believes to be
thousands of bi-
cycles speeding
along in the direc-
tion of St. Kilda and
Brighton men,
women, boys and
girls, buoyant youth
and “wizened,
hoary age,” petti-
coats and pants, cor-
pulence and lean-
ness, on every con-
ceivable type of
machine, cutting
along as if several
devils were after
them on pacing
tandems or fours.
But one thing
struck our Sydney man as the height of gross in-
congruity, viz., an elderly gentleman pedalling
along in a very shiny top-hat. This must be one
of those hats which survived the “boom time.”
The man vyho goes out cycling in a top-hat pro-
bably has but one hat.
“ My invariable rule,” said a too-talkative
damsel in the precincts of Divorce Judge Simp-
son’s court the other day, “is to keep whatever
presents gentlemen make me under engagement.
A lady is legally entitled to retain them, and is
morally obliged to punish a gentleman in this
way. I’ve done so in four cases.”

A lady, just returned from Westralia, ex-
plains how it is that Sydney wives receive such
EXPLOSION .
DEJECTED ARTIST : “ I wonder which magazine I had better take these
sketches to? ”
HIS CHEERFUL FRIEND : “The powder magazine !”
pessimistic accounts from their husbands absent in
the Land of the Golden Fleece :
Why I know two different men —now in Perth—with
whose wives and families in Sydney I am well acquainted.
Both have married again, and living in tip-top style. One
of these husbands has been away nine months, and all he
has sent his wife here is £2. The other party hasn’t re-
ceived even that yet. Will I show them up ? It isn’t
worthwhile. When they get tired of their Coolgardie
ladies they will be sure to come back.
When a man has two wives dependent on him you
can’t improve things by sending him to gaol and
leaving them both out in the cold.
Any ardent Tottie who hankers after the
clingsome hug of salvation, likewise the kiss of
peace, might do worse than throw in her lot with the
Melb. Christian Assembly. The C.A. is a
quite old-fashioned sect, comprising a hundred
souls and a small mob of “adherents,” under the
guidance of Pastor X. All was going well
within this religious circle until lately, when
Brother Z. was found guilty of too much
Christian behaviour, and suspended from practice.
This Brother, according to the pastor, “ has a law-
ful wife and family living in Victoria, and he does
not keep her, but all the time he is running after
another woman, a single girl, kissing her and
hugging her in pretty well every corner.”
Smarting under a sense of having been thrown
out, Brother Z. recently convened a meet-
ing to enquire into Pastor X. and his
alleged larks. Various charges were hurled at the
boss saint. It was declared in open tabernacle
that a small boy had seen pastor kissing a
“ sister ” under dim religious circumstances. Fur-
thermore, the brother accused his shepherd,
firstly, of grabbing a small handbag from off a
“ sister’s ” lap in a railway-carriage, and, secondly,
of taking therefrom a piece of preserved ginger,
which he held up to view, crying “ Ginger for
pluck ! ” On top of this revelation he produced
two letters “ supposed ” to have been written by
other sisters who wanted to love their pastor
violently. Then some more brother rose, and
started to reveal domestic details, until several
ladies retired blushingly from the fray. Finally,
the enquiry broke up into fragments, and pastor
X. went home triumphant, with several devoted
spinsters in attendance.
Anyway, Pastor X. is an improvement upon
a previous leader of the sect, who was driven
out of the pulpit on a charge of not being
able to produce a marriage-certificate when called
upon. The evidence of the small boy is unreli-
able, and the letters said to have been written by
the two sisters are wrapped in doubt. Also the
significance of “ Ginger for pluck ” is question-
able. Therefore, The Bulletin advises Pastor
X. to continue his ministry as before, paying
particular attention to the welfare of the single
girl whom Brother Z. is alleged to have kissed
and hugged in pretty well every corner. Probably
there are still a few corners where she hasn’t been
hugged up to the present, and it seems to be some-
body’s duty to see that these corners are duly
attended to.
The pastor, by the way, says that he
forgives Brother Z. everything. “As for that
poor deluded implement of the arch-enemy
of souls, he (X.) feels that the Almighty love,
which will enable Judas to eventually be saved,
will also enfold the present mischief-maker.”
How comforting. The brother, of course, can’t
expect immediate salvation. As an implement of
the Old ’Un he will be required to wriggle on the
grill Below for a few million years or so. But
eventually, O eventually, even Brother Z. may
be brought back. How these Christians tempo-
rarily damn one another!
AT THE CROSSING OF WARRIGAL
CREEK.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Through the ranges an echo in vain you may
seek
That can rival the echo on Warrigal Creek,
Where the swart basalt mountain frowns rugged
and still
O’er the Crossing—where Warrigal Creek skirts
the hill.
There the sad mopokes wait till the evening stars
blink ;
There the little rock-wallabies hop for their
drink;
And there, whisper or warble whatever you may,
The Crossing re-echoes each word that you say.
But when stars shine above and the world is at
rest,
Folk will tell you the echo sounds loudest and
best;
So it fell yesternight that my sweetheart and I
Wandered off in the moonlight the echo to try.
When we reached the still Crossing, distinctly
each word
That we whispered each other re-echoed was heard;
’Twas a spot where we knew that no listeners
were nigh—
There were only the echo and Nelly and I.
That echo repeated : “ the — sweetest — and — best: '*
That echo bore witness : “ far — more — than — the —
rest! ”
Yet again—although never a word did we speak—
There was heard a faint echo on Warrigal Creek.
And the modest moon dropp’d ’neath the rim of
the sky,
And the leaves of the currajong stirr’d with a
sigh,
Whilst the darkening night hid the blush on
Nell’s cheek
As a kiss was re-echoed on Warrigal Creek !
