A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Dec. 22, 1896.
Mr Dear Myee—
Weekly shivoos have been held at the Victoria
Club in Phillip-street until last Friday, which was
the end of the season, and the club goes into
seclusion for the summer months. The entertain-
ments have been of the mildly-musical order, songs
and recitations. There was also an evening re-
union last week, when lightning-sketches were a
feature ; a lady artist fondly traced the manly
beauty of Kyrle Bellew—not including his bandy
legs—on the blackboard, and observers sighed in
admiration. Two hostesses provide refreshments,
and there is always a disposition on the part of
guests to be stand-offish and refuse to drink tea
supplied by a hostess with whom they are not on
calling terms. With these little thorns of etiquette
some women love to stuff the cushion of life. All
members dress ceremoniously for the function.
The club wants some strong, vulgar person to get
in and make a startling speech and shake the
whole thing up into more life. Like the guests at
David Copperfield s first dinner-party, “ they
would all get on a great deal better if they were
not so genteel.”
The Town Hall was lively-looking on Wednes-
day evening when the Liedertafel concert was
given. The “Revenge Cantata” was very well
sung, and the man at the big-drum banged for all
he was worth. It’s rather a mistake to print
words of part songs on the programme, because the
absurdity of a body of about 100 men, most of
them bald and married, chanting passionate
strains to some “little youthful maiden” and
beseeching her to come into its collective “great
heart,” is glaringly apparent. Miss E. King, in
a pink dress, sang charmingly. The audience was
the average prosaic Liedertafel one which always
looks as if it had risen hastily from a meat-tea in
the remoter suburbs,__ wrapped its head in a
woollen “cloud,” and come forth with the firm
determination of getting its money’s worth out of
the annual subscription.
The Palace Theatre opening on Saturday night
was attended by that wild and frantic demand for
seats and scampering up and down of distracted
ushers which mark a popular boom. Full dress
decorated the stalls as well as the dress circle,
which has a well-like depth from back to front,
and the gallery is lost somewhere in the heights of
the “mooned dome aloft.” The little building is
so Oriental that it is like the “ Thousand and One
Nights” condensed into a single paragraph.
There are effects of “ fretted gold ” and “ broad-
based flights of marble stairs ” and all the splen-
dor and havoc of the East, and if you saw houris
and caliphs and hookahs and agas among the
audience it would seem quite natural. Above the
proscenium there is a brazen image of Buddha
sitting holding the sacred lotus, which flower is
used as a pattern for much of the glittering deco-
ration. For its size it is, I should think, just
about the prettiest music-hall in the world.
The curtain, said to have cost hundreds, deserves
a par. to itself. Peacock-blue plush is the ground-
work, glorified with much embroidery of gold and
silver braid worked into the lotus pattern. A
first-night audience applauded in a puzzled sort of
way at each turn of an average music-hall pro-
gramme. Australian audiences haven’t yet got
accustomed to that type of entertainment where
nobody is expected to sit primly from beginning
to end of the show and criticise each item
seriously. Knowles, Harry Lee and his large
spotty dog were the stars of the evening.
More and more one notices at the Xmas break-
ing-up parties how beautifully Australian girls
dance. I don’t mean the grown-ups, for they
sometimes get lazy and lose their spring, but the
little fairies of 10 or 12—clad in snowy-white frocks,
with long, thin legs in white silk stockings and
satin shoes—who clasp each other and spin round
in innocent gyrations, “ Betsy Jane with Betsy
Ann.” They do “butterfly” dances and “fan”
dances and “minuets” and “cotillions,” with a
sweet seriousness on their little faces as if their
steps were the most momentous they were ever to
take. They have very proper and correct ideas of
ball-room manners, too, and only dance with boys
who have been introduced. The well-meaning
but mistaken adult invited to help make the party
“go off,” is often reduced to limp flabbiness after
being chillingly snubbed by one tiny mite after
another whom he has dared to approach without
a formal presentation.
A mild flare-up occurred in military circles in a
sea port lately owing to the captain’s wife sending
out the orderly to the local chemist’s for three-
penn’orth of rouge to enable her to “make up”
for a toney ball, whereby the manly dignity of
the soldier was outraged, and he used bad lan-
guage and was consequently reproved from head-
quarters. Which reminds me that Australian
women are putting up a world’s record for “ mak-
ing-up,” and badly at that. Globe-trotting
Englishmen invariably notice the brightening in
color of hair with decreasing latitude overland
from Melbourne to Sydney. Waitresses on the
Vic. side are brown-haired, occasionally bronzed
with a little peroxide perhaps. On the Murray
the golden-haired zone is entered. Albury waitress
girls’ heads positively glitter, and nearly every
girl in Sydney sports straw-colored locks. Arti-
ficial bleaching produces a deadly pallid effect
which must be rectified with dabs of rouge. “If
they would only do it intelligently,” wailed a
fashionable hairdresser from whom I sought infor-
mation on this subject, “they would be improved
20 percent., but they don’t apply the powder-puff
and haresfoot with any discrimination.”
A high-toned Anglican church in a far-away
city burst forth into alleged “frolics” early this
month, with, of course, the usual eye to. the
“ gate.” Some of the revels were mildly hilarious,
including a tall and much-worshipped curate
giving threepenny donkey-rides to the children
inside the Sunday-school, and much afternoon
tea-drinking; all the doings were freely snap-
shotted with the same cleric well in the picture
every time. But a daring innovation on
the part of a well-known doctor’s daughter, assisted
by a youth of original ideas, was hiring an ice-
cream cart and doing a roaring trade as they drove
through the streets on the way to the orgies.
Some of the best-dressed women are lamenting
the departure of their favorite clothes-artist.
Tempted by filthy lucre, she has left for another
city and a higher-salaried appointment. And her
clients mourn that her hand and brain will no
longer plan for them sensational confections. The
modiste is a tall, showy woman herself, and,
dressed up to fashion-plate ideals, has always
been much observed at Randwick and other dress
parades.
An out-of-the-common wedding happened at
Parramatta on Wednesday last in St. John’s
Church, when Mr. Cecil Champion, of Harris
Park, wedded Miss E. Byrnes. In the gaslight
the bridal smiles and tears were artistically toned
down, and the dresses showed up twice as well as
in the garish glare of day. Rose-petals were sent
fluttering down from the gallery upon the heads
of the intrepid couple, and by way of a brilliant
novelty the choir sang the “ Voice that Breathed ”
as the knot was tying.
A concert was given on Monday night by pupils
of Mine. Charbonnet - Kellermann, and the
Y.M.C.A. hall was crowded. Children’s music
has been the subject of some interest lately, as
some of the infant performers are so brainy that
they had to be classed as seniors in the recent
exams., although their tiny fists can’t yet grapple
with an octave. It is quite astounding to see these
babies, their leglets yards too short for pedal-
reach, and hardly long enough to reach the floor
when the infant stands on its feet, rattling up and
down the keyboard in sonatas, and fugues,
and ops., or struggling valiantly with misfit
violins several sizes too large for them. A
son of H. Poussard glided easily over the difficul-
ties of a “Gondolied.” Otto Phillip, a tough
veteran of ten or eleven, repeated one of his many
Serformances of a classical violin piece. Frieda
Idler was the star pianist, and baby M‘Bride,
who had to be lifted on to the stool, played a per-
fect staggerer of difficulty. A child in blue
paniers and powdered hair acted Lady Teazle
most quaintly, and managed her fan and train
adroitly to a pink-clad Sir Peter with a childish
treble.
As the folks from wayback stroll along the
Block, at this holiday season, their fashions are
reminiscent and bring back modes of the year
before last. Innocent of “ godets ” or “fibre
chamois,” skirts cling to their wearers’ forms as in
the days of “sheaths”; and the most casual glance
at the style of hair-dressing shows that the netted-
“ bun ” is dying hard, and still lingers in Thargo-
mindah and Tumbulgum, and maintains a pre-
carious existence at Gobarralong. The men “do
the toff,” and a clean shave reveals a vast patch of
white skin against the sunburnt surrounding
expanse of face, and they curtail their already
curtailed pants by turning them up at the hem
unconvincingly.
The recent exhibition of children’s needlework,
at Govt. House, was rather a frost from the exhi-
bition point of view. Invited by Lady Hamp-
den to show samples of sewing, the children put
forth their best efforts, and the teachers had to go
to some trouble and expense to get the work ready
for show. They received a vice-regal command to
attend at Govt. House and hear the report of the
exam., when they were consolingly till, per
imported representative of the Needlework Guild,
that the “colonial ” work very nearly approached
in excellence that done by English children. Also
if they continued in the right path and didn’t
grow weary in well-doing, the young exhibitors
might expect some prizes next year. No gifts
this time ; the delirious joy of being considered
nearly as good as ’Ome needleworkers is quite
enough for one year.
A small boy had a little adventure one night re-
cently in a stuccoed Toorak mans''on, of which he
has the run. Bribed by the eldest girl’s young
man to place a letter under her pillow, the minute
go-between got into the wrong bedroom, and put
the tender document under the wrong pillow.
Then he just managed to hide under the bed in
time to escape the watchful eye of Ma, who went
innocently to sleep and snored above the mis-
placed document and the equally misplaced mes-
senger. The plucky little chap waited all night
until the room was vacant next morning, and then
he recovered the missive and delivered it all right.
It resulted in an elopement, and nobody except the
principals suspected the small friend of any part in
it.
A fashionable charity confab was lately broken
up by an open quarrel between two of the Nicest.
One lady refused to sit on the committee because
another not in No. l’s “ set ” had been appointed
a member. Then the two belligerent dames, with
great heat and excitement, proceeded to turn over
each other’s antecedents with a muck-rake, only
stopping short of actual blows. Judging by each
one’s description of the other, that charity organi-
sation is a very bad lot indeed.
Sydney Rowing Club gave a moonlight concert
at the branch - shed at Abbotsford on Thurs-
day night. The “ Lady Manning ” was
crowded with the party, which was enlivened
by a persevering band not to be daunted by the
hooting steam whistle yelling for the bridge to be
opened. The president gave the prizes won at the
last regatta, then some ballads warbled, and one
performer came out strong and gave several comic
songs. There were also refreshments and every-
body seemed to have a good time.
The largest Xmas tree ever shown here was
exhibited at the German church in Goulburn-
street on Sunday night, where wildly-hilarious
revels were held and worship in an up-to-date
form was celebrated, including the distribution of
presents “ made in Germany,” songs, recitations,
and other worries. The Teuton’s idea of keeping
up Xmas is to get as far away from Germany as
ever he can—the farther the better—surround him-
self with old-world associations, and talk much
sentiment about the Dear Old Fatherland, to
which he never wants to go back. The gift-dis-
tribution idea is a good one to keep up a failing
congregation.
The school-concert pianist, much in evidence
just now, is often a study in ungracefulness. As
she trots blushingly to the piano and nervously
tucks her skirts under her, she dives sideways at
the audience and clutches at the piano-stool,
which action pulls her dress tight on that side,
and, as she works the pedal, makes her look like
a bicycliste in bloomers. If she were a little
more self-possessed she would glide gracefully to
the instrument and, grabbing the stool on the
off-side, present a full sweep of skirt to the eyes of
the onlookers.
Paling and Co. have bestowed a boon on a
public which is haunted by the new melody
ground out by the automatic nigger at the steam
riding-gallery at Manly, by publishing it as “ The
Washington Post ” waltz in their Xmas annual*
It is one of those tunes as catching as an epidemic,
which stage it has already reached in Amurka.
“ Banjo ” Paterson and R. Goring-Thomas per-
petuate the historic “Nicest People ” phrase m a
song in the same up-to-date collection, and several
other lively songs and dances adorn the book.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ A point that amused many at the Melbourne
children’s dancing-shows—that dreary carnival of
Whirling Skirts which comes with every Xmas—
was the eagerness to play ‘ God Save the Queen ’
for the arri val of the Govt. House party. In one
instance it was started about half-a-dozen times—
false alarms, all of them. There is no imperial
authority for hailing Lady B. with the National
Anthem, but the heartrending melody gets there
all the same.
“ The best amateur violinist in Melb. is Dr.
Lazarus, who was a favorite pupil of the great
Johannes Woolfe. Dr. L.’s wife, formerly a Miss
Gollan, divides the honor of being the handsomest
Jewess in Melb. with Mrs. Isaacs. She is of a
very fine Oriental type, with splendid coloring.
Dr. and Mrs. L. leave their gorgeous stucco man-
sion on the Orrong Road early next month to re-
side permanently in the fog and grey atmosphere
of the Big Smoke. Melb. sassiety folk lose thereby
a couple of their most generous entertainers.
“The conversazione given by the trustees of the
National Gallery on Friday evening eventuated
very brilliantly. The occasion was the exhibition
of students’ work for the year and the awarding of
the prizes. This function has, up to now, always
taken place drearily in the afternoon, and tne
change to the evening was a distinct success.
Melb. smart folk, who always rush a free show
eagerly, turned up festively attired in the
now rather damaged finery of a declining
season. Lady Brassey, in black moirt an-
tique, wore some magnificent jewellery. Be-
sides embellishing her well-accentuated neck
with a collar of large diamonds, she further
added a gold serpent studded with turquoises and
diamonds, and the front ot her gown was like to a
jeweller’s show-case. The petite wife of a gaunt
professor displayed much white shoulder and arm
in a bodice built on Potteresque lines. Mrs. Ber-
nard Hall was in black, and the wife of a gallery
instructor attired herself weirdly in one of those
shapeless, colorless velvet gowns in which would-
be aesthetic folk love to upholster themselves to
the utter bewilderment of the average Philistine.
There are some amongst the female students this
year who have pretensions to beauty, generally a
rare gift among women artists. One girl with
Rubens-red hair and a rose-leaf complexion is
called ‘ Venus ’ by her fellow-students, who love
to try to catch her tints on canvas ; and a tall,
graceful, grey-eyed girl, known as ‘ Trilby,’ is
perhaps the handsomest of the lot.
“ Coates’success was most popular ‘ up at the
gallery,’ and his was a sort of Newhaven victory
over other competitors for the scholarship, though
the youth whom the body of students believe to
be the cleverest artist among them did not com-
pete. Becoming impatient at the repeated criti-
cism of the instructors, the student blocked out
the whole of his picture at the last moment. The
work of a lady student who has since gained a
South Kensington prize supplied the comedy
element to the exhibition, the weird perspective
and strange arrangement of light making the
picture a wholly original outbreak of unconscious
humor.
“Melb. women, it would seem, are the most
superstitious and gullible in Australia. In Melb.
flourish more ‘palmists,’ ‘futurists’ and
‘spirit mediums’ than anywhere else in these
provinces, though Sydney conies in a good second.
And the gulls who enrich these sharks are women
who belong to the so-called ‘higher-class.’ One
suburban medium, whose crude methods of decep-
tion have been exposed over and over again, cannot
give ‘sittings’ to more than half the enquirers
who would gain revelations from the ‘Seven
Heavens ’ which this astute woman has invented
on her own, for the accommodation of the spirits
of her clients’ relatives. This ‘ medium ’ sits from
9 a.m. till midnight, and, though her charges are
as high as half-a-guinea, she is besieged at all
hours by women who eagerly swallow the absurd
clap-trap she ladles out to them. A half-crown
‘ palmist ’ is shortly to retire from the business,
having accumulated a fortune after a few years’
practice up a back-street in a squalid neighbor-
hood.”
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
Probably the most grotesque of all the
grotesque magisterial exhibitions in the matter of
suicide was open at a Sydney police-court one
day last week. A silly woman was brought up
charged with drinking poison. She had quar-
relled with her husband and wanted to scare him,
and the husband kindly stood up and said he had
a quarrelsome nature and he wanted to take the
blame on himself. Then the Bench, in the person
of Addison, S.M., said that “suicides were be-
coming far too frequent,” and the silly woman
must do seven days’ imprisonment—Addison being
apparently an ignorant person who doesn’t know
that suicide is a matter of statistics; and de-
pends, not on the individual, but on race, and
age, and climate, and crops, and a lot of other
things ; and that it is about as sensible to sentence
silly women to seven days for attempted suicide
as it would be to sentence Addison to seven years
for softening of the brain—supposing he had
softening of the brain. Anyhow, the silly woman
got seven days. Then a solicitor named Gannon
begged to interpose, and suggested that a fine
would meet the case, and the Bench acknowledged
itself an ass by altering its decision to a fine of
£2. Then another solicitor named Abigail begged
to interpose some more, and suggested that “ in
view of the presence of the Christmas season ” (!)
a still lighter penalty would do, and the Bench
decided that suicide was partially allowable at
Christmas, and wrote itself down some more
of an ass by altering its decision again—
this time to six hours’ imprisonment. The Bench,
it will be observed, knows its own mind so
admirably that it complacently alters its dole of
alleged justice twice at the bidding of a senti-
mental attorney. Why not have sentences drawn
from a barrel, same as Tattersall’s sweeps ?
During the year 1896 three successive
doctors to the Leichhardt Female Rechabite Tent,
Sydney, died.
Wellington (M.L.) has at present a larger
wholesale and retail shop than usual, and is calling
it an Exhibition. There is no more in the exhibi-
tion than can be-seen in the business places of the
town, and not as much as can be seen in some big
emporiums in Sydney any day of the week. How-
ever, the inhabitants of Wellington have by hard
thinking come to persuade themselves that it is a
great sight entirely, and crowd the building on the
reclaimed land night after night, and concerts
given with more or less success—especially less—
help people to forget remote troubles in the pre-
sence of those which are immediate.
Time deals gently with the woman who
uses “ Babeskin ” soap. It softly removes the
tell-tale lines of the years, and renews the delicate
bloom of youth. One shilling packages of two
cakes. All chemists.*
I Brisbane (Q.) is now “ expecting another
flood in January or February, the superstition
being that the big floods come every three years,
and “time’s up.” The impressions of the last
one are still visible in numbers of empty shops
and houses in the parts subject to inundation,
especially in South Brisbane. Yet no one ever
hears any talk now about a high-level or a suspen-
sion bridge. The new bridge, half completed (half
in width) is a gorgeous-looking affair, tied to-
gether overhead and underway with almost a
filagree of iron or steel trusses, but it's only a foot
or so higher than the last, and when the houses
and the floating forests come down against it
again, it will go, as sure as Death.
The private enterprise railway of Nathaniel
Levi, M.L.C. (Vic.), has gone bung. Many years
ago Levi obtained permission to build a line from
Kilcunda to Cape Patterson in order to develop
certain coal deposits, and open up the country,
and do other beautiful things. The term expired
without Levi doing anything, and the concession
was renewed. It was renewed five times in all,
and each time Levi was just about to commence.
It expired again the other day, and member T.
Smith started to introduce the customary Bill,
but this time the House rose up in its long-bottled
wrath and said, in effect, that Levi, M.L.C., was
an old, unpractical bore, so Nathaniel’s railway is
gone.
The Q. pearlshell fishery law (at the time
of writing in process of amendment), whether
amended or not is a fine illustration of legislative
wisdom. The law says that the inner measure-
ment of each shell removed must not be less than
so much (it was six inches, and it is now proposed
to make it five inches). Now, some shells are
thicker than others, just as some men’s skulls are
thicker than others. A shell not measuring so
much is not to be removed. But, in order to
measure it, to see whether it is within the limit or
not, it has not only to be removed, but opened.
Being opened, if it is under the limit, it is contra-
band. Being opened, it is also dead and useless.
The law requires the pearlfisher, then, to throw it
away. Not being a fool, he naturally doesn’t.
At Carlton (Melb.) court the other week,
an indignant honorary magistrate recorded a
solemn protest agai nst the leniency of his worship-
ful brethren. They had let off the culprit in an
“indecency” case with a mere 18 months’hard
labor, whereas the dissenting justice—Councillor
levers —thought that the miserable sinner “ ought
to have been flogged.” A Bulletin correspondent
instances the protest aforesaid as proof that the
Lormer Board’s provisions for the regulation of
the Vic. honorary magistracy are totally in-
adequate “on the showing of one of their own
number.” He (the correspondent) expects this
truculent journal to shriek for an enquiry into the
scandalous conduct of the man who voted for a
sentence of 18 months’ hard labor. In the mean-
time, however, The Bulletin prefers to snort at
the would-be flogger.
It is alleged by a daily paper that “it has
not yet been decided whether there shall be a
formal unveiling” of Governor Phillip’s statue in
the Sydney Garden Palace Grounds. A splendid
opportunity will be missed if this is true. There
are several gentlemen who are admirably adapted
by nature and temperament to make appropriate
remarks on the occasion of the unveiling of the
statue of “our first gaoler.’ For instance,
Minister for Justice Gould has super-eminent
claims ; and ex-Minister for Justice Heydon is a
strong runner up. A slight postponement would
enable ex-Judge Windeyer to return for the
occasion; G. Republican Dibbs might do in the
intervals of presenting addresses at theatrical
benefits ; and, as a last resource, ex-Sheriff
Cowper might be prevailed upon to make a few
well-chosen remarks while Nosey Bob pulls the
official string and unveils the figure.
Melbourne supplies a quaint tale of a
family quarrel. Thomas Hannen and the woman
that he was not married to disagreed about a £5
note which he was unable to return because part
of it had gone in drink. Thomas went to sleep on
the sofa, but presently woke to find himself
covered with kerosene and blazing cheerfully. He
rose and extinguished himself, and then philoso-
phically went to sleep again. Some little time
afterwards he woke to find that he had been kero-
sened again, and that his head and beard were
flaring up vigorously. This time he grew irritated
and after extinguishing himself a second time he
went out and complained to the police, who
arrested the lady on suspicion. As he hasn’t
blazed up in his sleep since she was locked up, tue
presumption is that she did it, and that she in-
tended to consume. Thomas to ashes on the sofa.
Anyhow, the event is regarded locally as an affect-
ing episode.

Personal Items.
Melb. : ‘ ‘ The actual firm of Dombey and
Son has just established a branch here.”
A son of a prominent Australian baronet
is employed cleaning bicycles in a Melb. cycle
shop.
“ The Vagabond’s ” grave is to have a
monument, from Victorian admirers of his pen-
work.
Archbishop Carr is said, in high R.C.
circles, to be on the imminent edge of a car-
dmalate.
In the light of recent criticisms and de-
velopments a certain Q. politician now at ’Ome
intends to get well very slowly indeed.
The late general manager of a very sick
institution left his widow an annuity of £BOO a
year. And, ye gods, what did he leave the bank !
Louis Becke’s projected South Pacific
trading enterprise has received a check by the
Phillippines revolutionary affair. At latest lie was
working with his pen at Deal, “ spending his spare
time fishing and swearing at the climate.”
Q. Governor Lamington has confessed
publicly that it is for him “almost an impossi-
bility ” to concentrate what he thinks is his mind
on any one subject at one time. His intellect, it
seems, scatters all over the place and is never
there when it is wanted.
An Australian dentist is noted for his phiz.
At an inland town, professionally visited, one of
the local dead-beats, after lengthened glances, said
to him confidentially: “By Jove, old man, no
offence, but I’ve seen faces like yours after taking
blue-gum mixture for a week.”
A. Catt, Chairman of Committees in the
S.A. Assembly, will be 63 on Monday (Dec. 19).
He was elected to Parliament in 1881, and since
then has drawn .£lOOO a year as Minister of the
Crown for five years, and £4OO per annum as
Chairman of Committees for seven years.
The fact that Vic. Attorney-General
Isaacs drafted and introduced the Usury Bill,
under which the maximum interest is 10 per cent.,
makes the Melb. synagogue rave exceedingly.
The spectacle of Isaac thus rounding on Moses is
a painful instance of dissension among brethren.
Alfred Saunders, the alleged Nestor of
Maori I and House of Reps., was among the lost at
the late election. Old, well-meaning Saunders
was called a Nestor so often that at last lie began
to live up to the character, and habitually stalked
about the precincts of the House absorbed in
alleged thought, with his head bowed down, and
his hands clasped behind his back, and his over-
whelming white beard surging over his chest. The
patriarch was ejected chiefly for looking so muoh
wiser than any one man really could be that the
country could li t stand his overwhelmingness any
longer. J
Poor old Thomas
M ‘ll wraith, according
the cablegrammer, is
“slowly improving.”
He has been slowly
improving, according
to the same authority,
for two years past, and
apparently he is only
improving into his
grave. He can hard-
ly walk at all, and
the cable-man states
that he “wishes to re-
cover the use of his
hands.” This fact was
probably sent out lest
Australians should
th ink that he didn’t
want to recover the
use of his hands ; the
cable person is dread-
fully particular about
details. M‘llwraith,
however, isn’t much
of a news subject now-
adays ; the pathetic
wreck of what used
to be Queensland’s
Strong Man hardly
casts a shadow on
local politics these
times.
The return of H.
Smith Fish to M.L.
Parliament is a proof
that virtue is reward-
ed in the long run.
The first blood of the
political campaign was
shed from Fish’s nose
on Fish’s own door-
step, a too-enthusias-
tic * democrat having
called round and
whacked the violent
old man in the centre
of the countenance.
Now the blood of the
martyr has risen up
from Fish’s doorstep and extinguished his oppo-
nents hopelessly at the polls. Years ago Minister
Mackenzie missed Fish with a pickle-bottle in a
hotel, and now that these two enemies—both hard
men with long memories—are going to meet again
in the Parliamentary refreshment-room, the bill
for broken crockery should be interesting reading.
Working at White Feather (W.A.) is an
old German miner locally known as Charley.
Twelve months ago Charley travelled overland
from Norinanton (North Queensland) via Fowler’s
Bay and Eucla to Coolgardie. From Fowler’s
Bay (S. A.) the journey was performed on foot and
alone, the ancient Teuton’s equipment consisting
of a two-gallon water-bag and such tucker as he
could carry in his swag. When he arrived at
Panton’s station on the Bight Charley ravenously
devoured a leg of mutton which was being cooked
for dinner. Next morning he walked back 30
miles for his swag which he had left on the track.
And the unobtrusive, guttural wanderer has never
once been mentioned as a “ Great Australian ex-
plorer.”
How the family names run ! Extract from
a report of a Brisbane (Q.) Full Court case last
week : —“ Mr. Edouin Lilley, with him Mr.
Arthur Lilley (instructed by Mr. Charles Lilley,
as agent for Mr. A. M. Lilley, of Toowoomba),
appeared for the applicant.” All relatives of the
ex-Chief-Justice.
Nearly all the old Sydney identities—
“ Abby Dabby,” “ Garden Honey,” “Paddy the
Ram, ” Parkes and the rest--have been taken by
death within the last half-dozen years. In accor-
dance with the fitness of things in Botany Bay
“Nosey Bob” is about the only really notable
character who survives.
American papers report that Sankey, the
man who used to grind out the hymn for the old
firm of Moody and Sankey, having gone broko in
his old age, has gone back to revivalism and isn’t
making expenses at the game. Moody bought
land with his share of the loot, and is booming.
Also the two old partners have established a life-
long feud, and hate each other just like two
hardened sinners.
It is a small matter, but it is just as well
to state, in reference to a paragraph in the Free-
man’s Journal, that the first woman’s letter in
The Bulletin signed “Sappho Smith” was not
“ written by a young lady, now the wife of a Sydney
doctor.” The late Miss Wildman was the only
lady who ever wrote for The Bulletin under the
pen-name of “ Sappho Smith.”
One of the pioneers of the Zeehan
mines, aged 00, rheumatic and generally frozen
out, is now alleged to lead a precarious exist-
ence in boilers and other makeshift residences
close to the scene of his great silver find. There
is much sameness about the story of the pioneer.
He makes the find. Then one Cohen comes along
and exploits it. Then the old explorer is run in
for sleeping in a tank on Cohen’s allotment.
Melbourne now has an undertaker who is
an ordained minister, and on occasion reads the
burial service over his corpses. Also, it would
strike a corpseing person of any imagination that
when the Rev. Crape called in to administer reli-
gious consolation he could hardly help running
liis eye over the about-to-be-deceased to size up
the material needed for his wooden overcoat.
Beautiful stories of a Southern softgoods
magnate, recently burnt out ; are going the rounds
again. The magnate, as a pietist, looks for ardent
Kerristianity from his employes, and a man seek-
ing admission to the “slops” fold muoh betters
his chances by playing the religious ticket. Some
years ago an applicant for the billet of traveller
was adopting quite an ordinary, worldly tone,
until the softgoodsman took exception to his lack
of size. He didn’t carry a sufficiently important
appearance, the boss thought. Then the billet-
seeker had a Scriptural inspiration, as it were.
“Mr. ,” he said, “my height is not a real
objection. St. Paul was only a little man, but he
was a great power for good. ” The admiring slops
manufacturer engaged Paul secundus without
more ado.
BROTHERS IN MISFORTUNE.
FIRST : “ Say. old man, lend me a couple of quid till to-morrow."
SECOND : “ Can't, old man—the wife’s just touched me for a fiver to get me
a Christmas-box.”
FIRST : “ Great Scott! so's mine!”
Mr. Tom Roberts has resigned the presi-
dentship of N.S.W. Society of Artists—owing to
Mr. Julian Ashton, a member of N.S. Wales
National Gallery trust, having been elected to a
seat on the committee of the society named. Mr.
Roberts is quite right in contending that the
positions of National Gallery trustee (in which
the holder takes part in buying pictures
for the State) and Art Society commit-
teeman (in which the same person takes part
in selling and promoting the sale of pictures
to the State) are quite irreconcilable ; also that
“ the difficulty is increased when the same gentle-
man is head of a considerable school for the train-
ing of artists, and still further by the fact that
there exists another body—the Art Society of
N.S.W.—which also holds classes and exhibitions,
and makes its very proper claim upon the atten-
tion of the National Gallery trustees, who are
almost the only purchasers of Australian works of
art in the province.” These things were urged by
The Bulletin long ago ; why they haven’t more
forcibly struck the community is a mystery.
Q. Speaker Cowley is an insignificant
shrimp of a man, as seen outside in his store
clothes. No one would look twioe at him for
“prospects.” But, in the chair, with robes on,
and so long as he remains seated, he looks bigger.
Which would seem to indicate that his back is
longer than his legs, and that nature intended him
for lumping bags of potatoes. His face, in profile,
is singularly attractive and indicative of bright
talent, while his enunciation is clear and distinct
—although he does call the sea-slug “ beach-de-
nier.”
A VICTIM OF DISASTER.
PURCHASER : “ You said this horse had no faults — why, he’s blind!"
VENDOR : “ That’s not his fault; it’s his misfortune.”
A W.A. mining expert, accused of
drought, obtains the following certificate from
publicans in outlying districts to satisfy his
clients they have had a run for their money :
This is to certify that Mr. has not taken a drink of
anything stronger than soda at my hotel for one month.
It is a lithographed document, and the publican,
having signed it and filled in the date, it is pinned
on to the report as a proof that the latter is eon-
cei ved in a strictly sober spirit.
John Hutcheson, the ship-rigger and ex-
sailor who headed the poll for Wellington City
(M.L.) with Robert Stout a bad second, didn’t
give way to undue inflation over his new glory.
Three days after the election the new M.H.R.
tramped down Wellington’s principal street in his
working clothes, with half a bale of rope over his
shoulder, on his way to do some rigging. There
isn’t much of the triumphal-arch business about
Hutcheson.
“ Harwood,” suspected of the murders of
Lee Weller and Preston, was indeed most unhappy
in his choice of aliases. The sinister associations
of the name “ Butler” have already been pointed
out, but what shall be said of “Burgess,” another
name of his? Burgess, it will be remembered,
was the M.L. criminal hanged with Levy and
Kelly for the series of Maungatapu murders, in
their way as cold-blooded and mercenary as those
of Glenbrook.
This is how the Nocent Waterspout of
N.S.W. oommenced his famous Trafalgar-day
letter to London Telegraph :
A native of England, but for over 40 years a citizen of
“ Greater Britain,” I write to thank you for the golden
eloquence of your noble article on the “ mighty seadord,”
and “ that splendid fatal day,” which, with “ justified
patriotism,” is now commemorated.
Then about two dozen more adjectives follow, and
all these garish figures of speech rush down a
■teep place until they bring up with a shock against
the signature : “ J. C. Neild, Major, commanding
St. George’s Rifles, New South Wales, 74 St.
Ermin’s-Mansions, Westminster.”
Armand Jerome, the Sydney disappearance
man, sentenced to three years for forgery, alleged,
in his plea for mercy, that the beginning of his
troubles was a desire to make a name for himself
in literature. Jerome oertainly had the literary
yearning very badly—a misplaced ambition, for he
had not a speck of the literary faculty. He might
easily have made an ideal canvasser, bagman, in-
surance agent, patent agent, company promoter,
perpetrator of boom prospectuses —in fact, he could
have shone in quite a number of the more gaudy
forms of commercial razzle-dazzle—and his desire
to be some kind of a man of letters only led to
pathetic failure.
Barwon Murray writes to The Bulletin :
The other day, there died on the Barwon, at Mogil
Mogil, one of its oldest identities, the aboriginal king
“ Gobbler,” reputed to be aged over 100 years. “Gobbler”
was unique in this —that he never drank spirits, or even
“ soft stuff,” having an abhorrenoe of any liquor tendered
him by the whites. The story goes that he was
“ hocussed ” in his youth, and had never tasted liquor
sinoe. I have often heard people ask him at the local
pub. to have a drink, and his reply was invariably, “Me
no drink; you gib it the sixpence." It was usual for
“ Gobbler,” as the oldest representative of the Barwon
aborigines, to wire to the Sydney authorities when any of
the blacks were in need of clothing, eta, and, upon his
taking ill recently, the poor old fellow got another blaok-
fellow to send the following wire to the N.S.W. Governor :
“King Gobbler, oldest Murray on the Barwon, dying;
please send it medicine. (Signed) Jimmy Dool.” And
“Cobbler” got the medicine and necessary meat-extract
up to his death, refusing brandy and other stimulants to
the last.
Ewan M‘Pherson, who died the other day,
aged 75, and who, with Lord Wolverhampton,
owned the greater part of Queensland Diamantine
country, was accidentally the means of ending
bushranger Morgan’s villainous career. Acciden-
tally, because Morgan did what he pleased with
Ewan, and would have got away all right but that
a servant girl had managed to give the word to i
neighbor. Anyway, Morgan was trapped and
shot. The details of the subsequent fight over th«
reward for the killing of Morgan make an ugly
page of police history. Quinlan, the shooter, was
merely a laboring man who, though accompanied
by police, worked wholly on his own hook and,
anticipating the police manoeuvre, felled the out-
law and claimed the booty. The police disgust at
Quinlan’s action was only equalled by Morgan’s,
who asseverated before dying that he bad not
been allowed a dingo’s chance.
King O’Malley, M.P. (S.A.), lately made a
few remarks about his enemies :
. . . diabolical rapscallions . . . carrion vul-
tures . . . blacklegs, bluelegs, and redlegs of swiping
vulgarity . . . social rats and crawling reptiles of
city slums . . . trail buzzards of death . . . im-
moral human skunks . . . that manure wrapper
called the Country . . . hypnotised, tattooed tool of
vulgar plutocracy . . . hungry mob of jackals and
leeches who lived by sucking the sores on the dying Con-
servative tiger ....
An Inspector’s Happy New Year.
Walked 30 miles to wish his Wife “ Happy
New Year.”
New Year’s Day. What happiness it brings to
the sacred family circle. What bright, happy
faces one meets in this yearly reunion of hearts
and voices, which makes it the first endeavour of
everyone to spend this day as a happy reunion
with wife, children, family and friends. Nothing
so interferes with the joy of New Year’s Day more
than if one of the family circle is stricken down
with disease, and recovery to health for that dear
one would occasion the most sincere and purest
joy.
Inspector Robt. Mackay, of Echuca, knew this,
and that is why he walked 30 miles to surprise and
gladden his wife with a new year greeting. This
page of romance from the book of life appeals te
us all, for we never know how soon we or some of
our dear ones may suffer as he did. He writes
“It is about ten years ago that I commenced to
suffer from stone in the bladder, also gravel, the
pain from which was most excruciating. I suffered
so much that I often wished I should die. There-
fore it gives me great pleasure to give this testi-
monial, not only to let my fellow sufferers know,
but to show what a benefit Warner’s Safe Cure is
to all mankind. I consulted every doctor in the
town, and was under their treatment for some
considerable time but without being cured. I
then sent to Kerang for a doctor who was con-
sidered to be well versed in complaints of my
nature, and as he had to come a great many miles
the expenses were heavy This I would not have
minded had he cured me. I must say, however,
that he relieved me temporarily ; but it camo on
again as bad as ever. I tried several other treat-
ments, but all failed, and, as a last resort (as most
people do) I tried Warner’s Safe Cure and Pills
(the latter I can speak very highly of as they are
of great assistance to the Safe Cure), and after I
had taken four bottles I experienced great relief,
and I kept on, keeping strictly to the directions,
with a result that it removed a stone twice as
large pea. Warner’s Safe Cure either softened
or dissolved it till it became small enough to pass.
Some days before this I was in such agony that I
thought I should faint, and I left instructions to
not allow any doctor to touch me, as I knew the
Safe Cure was doing me good. When this stone
passed a quantity of urine and blood passed away;
also close on 60 little stones or gravel, which
greatly resembled pearl barley. I can scarcely ex- *
press in words my gratification when this was
over. I went to bed and slept for about 24 hours
—a pleasure I had not known for some time.
When I got up I walked over thirty miles to wish
my wife a happy new year it being New Year s
Eve.

“ The Patents, Trade Marks and Copyright
Laws form one of the most complicated branches
of legal science.” This is true, and it is practi-
cally impossible for any one unacquainted with
their technicalities to draw specifications and
claims, &c., that will stand the test of a law-suit.
Hitherto expert Patent agents have made charges
simply prohibitive to most inventors, but Mr.
Arthur Griffith, M.L.A., of 39 Castlereagh-
street (next Hotel Australia), Sydney, and Bolton-
street, Newcastle, having established reciprocal
arrangement with leading Patent agents in all the
principal countries in the world is now prepared
to do the work accurately and effectively at rates
that will place a patent within reach of the poorest
inventor. For the sum of £2O (exclusive of cost of
drawings) he will obtain a certificate of Provisional
Protection (in the first instance) for any invention
in the seven Australasian colonies and Great
Britain, and full Patents all over the world at
similarly reasonable rates. Mr. Griffith also
makes a specialty of designing and registering
Trade Marks and Copyrights.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Govt, of S.A. expects to have to
advance about <£25,000 to farmers in droughty
districts for the purchase of seed-wheat for next
year’s sowing.
At recent S.A. election the one vote cast
at Braemar, an outlying polling-place, was that of
the assistant returning-officer. And he received
£5 ss. as salary and travelling expenses for count-
ing his own ballot-paper.
Wilson, one of the defeated candidates for
Wellington (M.L.), defined his successful rival,
Stout: “ Robert Stout is an agnostic. An agnos-
tic is a gentleman who spells God with a small ‘ g ’
and Stout with a big ‘S’.”
It is already reported in Melbourne that
the Toorak seat is being got ready for Duncan
Gillies on his return. Yet surely old Talking
Duncan’s many years of earnest and continuous
uselessness entitle him to a rest now.
Attorney-General Isaacs enjoys one con-
stant compliment in Vic. Assembly —viz. , the bitter
animosity of squatter Staughton. Staughton is
the unthinking capitalist who lately classified him-
self and friends as “ the respectable people.”
Jules Joubert is still struggling with his
Queensland exhibition project, but the public,
generally speaking, look upon his invitation to
subscribe with a lacklustre eye. They can go up
to Parliament House free of cost and hear Bat-
tersby any night through the session.
As generally foreseen, the Queensland
National Bank Ministry now laments that it was
the cussedness of the Labor-party which wrecked
Federation. But then, everything that goes
wrong up North, even the ticks and the drought,
is owing to the cussedness of the Labor-party.
The biggest political failure in the new
S.A. Assembly is Opposition Leader Copley, once
a Democrat and until lately the darling of the
Tory party. Copley grows weaker and more pur-
poseless every day, and the Tory crowd will
almost certainly drop its idol next session for
John Darling, jun. Poor old “ Turncoat Bill ” !
Gilhooley, from the backblocks :
In Queensland, there are some 18,000 unmarried colored
men, which means, in plain English, that the white popula-
tion has to supply women tor this crowd. Is this the destiny
of our country—breeding prostitutes for colored Asiatics,
while the noble Australian spends his time at cricket?
The colored men have lately been encroaching on the em-
ployers’ female relations. This is why the Bundaburghers
are howling for female-servant immigration.
The two Melbourne “ great ” dailies seem
equally anxious for a lessening of the Vic. Legis-
lature, and the Age goes so far as to publish the
names of democratic members who broke their
promises to vote for the alleged reform. If the
men thus held up to reprobation are well-advised
they will take an early opportunity to teach their
constituents what the Reduction of Members Bill
really means, and why the Age ought to be
against, rather than in favor of, a scheme
fashioned after Fat Man’s own heart. As for
having broken their pledges, they can plead that
it’s never too late to abandon a bad theory.
A great cold blast of explanation has
already set in among the defeated Cold Tea and
Tory parties in M.L. Each party says the trouble
was largely owing to the other splitting the vote,
and putting up unsuitable candidates. Also, the
defeat is owing to wholesale bribery, corruption,
roll-stuffing, and mis-counting of the ballot by a
“ k’rupt Guv’ment ” and its hangers on; to
bribery, corruption, personation, roll-stuffing and
the working of the dead-man vote by the publi-
cans, to woman-suffrage, trades-unionism, godless
education, and the machinations of the devil.
Further, it isn’t a defeat at all, but a “ moral
victory.” Also, neither party wanted to win, and
preferred giving the democratic Government
enough rope to hang itself with. Also 2827 other
things.
Melbourne Paunch :
The placing of this £3,500,000 with the Queensland
National Bank appears to be most reprehensible, whether
it was done in a light or careless fashion, or especially for
the purpose of bolstering up the balance-sheet of the
bank. The Turner Ministry proposed in their crude State
Bank Bill, which they so hastily dropped, to take power
to come to the assistance of any bank in difficulties. We
should imagine from the exposures which have taken
place in connection with the Queensland National Bank
and the Bank of New Zealand, also of several Victorian
banks which were not assisted, that no Ministry will ever
he so foolhardy or financially wicked in the future as to
propose to come to the help of any weak or struggling
ooncern.
Good old Paunch ! The B.N.Z. and the “ several
Victorian banks ” went wrong under private man-
agement, therefore State management is wrong.
The Queensland Government poured about
into the Q.N.B. without security,
therefore the Turner Government’s proposal to
advance money temporarily to private banks at a
crisis on the deposit of sufficient secui'ty is
“ foolish ” and “ wicked.” Beautiful logic !
Premier Reid: “All Australia under an
improved Federal Council with a scope to develope
gradually into a more complete form of federation
might be a wiser consummation than part of Aus-
tralia welded together in a final form of union.”
In other words, a large thing half-done might be
better than a smaller thing completed. And Reid
prefers six provinces under a useless and impo-
tent Federal Council to five with a solid Central
Government. Reid believes in size more than
quality, and, considering his corporation, who oan
say that he doesn’t live up to his faith ?
Maoriland Par-
liament has legalised
marriage with a
deceased wife’s sis-
ter, but when it was
invited last session
to pass a Deceased
Husband’s Brother
Bill it rejected the
measure with scorn.
This was done, too,
by a legislature
largely elected by
women. Evidently
the Maoriland
woman, after due
consideration of the
brother she is already
married to, doesn’t
want any more like
him.
At Govt, land-
sales of township lots
in W.A. some Govt,
hanger-on generally
gets the valuing, and
some other g.h.o. the
selling. These two
collude. The valuer
often proceeds sim-
ply and openly to
collect bribes. He
interviews the occu-
pier of an improved
lot; if the occupier
does not “ act sensi-
bly,” the valuation
of his improvements
is so low that any
outsider is likely to
out-bid him when
the lot is auctioned.
If, on the other hand,
he greases the
valuer’s palm hand-
somely, <£soo worth
of improvements go
down as <£lsoo, and
this is so obviously
outrageous that no one will bid for the section, and
the occupier gets it at upset price. If any fool
does buy the lot the occupier makes £lOOO, for
the buyer has to pay for the improvements at the
official appraisement.
In Maoriland, under the merciful Legiti-
mation Act, an unlawful child can be made legiti-
mate and so registered if the parents afterwards
marry, providing there was no obstacle to their
marriage at the time of its birth. The law has
been in force 15 months, and under it 79 children
have already been relieved of the stigma of
illegitimacy- equal to one-tenth of the whole
number of illegitimate births.
A CASH DEAL.
SHE : “ Can you explain how that handsome man
came to marry such an ugly woman? ”
HE: “ Cheque-mated, probably.”
S.A. has in force a Lapsed Bills Continu-
ance Act, and under this Kingston’s Land Re-
form Bill, the Municipal Single Vote Bill, and
Land Values Assessment Bill and some other
radical measures which had to be dropped this
session will be revived next June where, they left
off. The old practice was to start all the weary
argument afresh from the beginning, and hardened
Tories still assert that the present time-saving
device is “ un-English ” and “ unconstitutional.”
That brilliant Maoriland Oppositionist,
Mitchelson, who went down badly at Auckland,
fooled away his chances just as Parkes did when
he lost the leadership of the N.S.W. Foreign-
trade party. He refused to stand, and waited to
be duly grovelled to by the party and implored to
come forward. But the party didn’t understand
the situation and ran another candidate for
Mitchelson’s intended safe seat at Eden, and then
the great man with difficulty induced himself to
come forward at Auckland where he wasn’t
wanted, and the thud with which he fell to the
bottom of the poll was heard as far off as Samoa.
Vic. Constitution Act Amendment Bill
was thrown out by the Upper House at sight. It
would have been thrown out anyway, but appar-
ently the Turner Government itself supplied a
handy excuse. A Constitution Amendment Bill
must be passed by a two-thirds majority. This
wasn’t one—it was merely a measure establishing
adult suffrage and abolishing the property vote ;
but the Government called it a Constitution
Amendment Bill, and the Council promptly re-
fused to look at it because it hadn’t secured the
needful two-thirds majority in the Assembly.
PAT’S IMPRESSION.
MARY : “ Just look, asthore. She’s a foine ladyloik person .”
PAT: “Faith, that’s more than I can say for him.”
Premier Seddon, in the last few days of
the M.L. election campaign, put in a tremendous
amount of work. Leaving his wife to suppress
the Opposition candidate in his own Westland
electorate, he addressed meetings at Christchurch,
Dunedin, and all the important towns in between,
addressed three meetings in Wellington, delivered
addresses at Stratford and Eltharn- in Taranaki,
then another at Otaki, and finally addressed three
meetings at Wellington in one night. And all
this was crowded into seven days. For that last
week Kumara Dick averaged about two hours
sleep a night; all the rest was travelling and
speech-making.
In Melbourne they accept
financial smells philosophically;
in Brisbane they love them.
When the Bill authorising the
Government to reduce interest
on its Q.N. Bank deposit and
give more time for repayment
was going through Parliament,
it was suggested as an amend-
ment that the Government
should require the bank to elect
new directors before it entered
into any agreement. Rejected
with scorn and a large majo-
rity. It doesn’t seem very much
to ask—the peaceful resignation
of the directors who wrecked
the bank and issued the dread-
fully-unreliable balance-sheets,
and otherwise proved their
hopeless incapacity. But
Queensland Parliament would-
n’t hurt the feelings of its re-
presentative fat men for the
world, and is quite willing to
leave the State’s millions still
in their charge.
George Thorn, M.P. (Q.), once known as
the “boy Premier”: “If Australian Federation
is accomplished, Brisbane will be wiped off the
face of the earth or words to that effect.
Federation will abolish border tariffs and ex-
tinguish the preferential railway rates which force
business where it wouldn’t otherwise go. There-
fore any city that Australia has no use for will
naturally be obliterated.
In the last days of the session just closed,
the Tas. House of Landlords rejected Attorney-
General Clark’s pet electoral scheme—the Hare
system modified by compelling electors to vote
for at least three candidates out of six. But the
clause was rejected by one vote only. Clark
fairly clung to the buttonhole of the recalcitrant
one until he got the clause recommitted and
passed ; then he invited the old gentlemen to
“ try sumthin’. ”
The Jap. is coming with a vengeance ! At
a mill on the Lower Burdekin (Q.), 150 Japs, are em-
ployed with about half-a-dozen whites. It is not
pretended, by the way. that white men cannot do
the work, for not only white men but even white
women can be seen doing field work in the same
district. Japanese auction sales of Japanese
goods are becoming common in Townsville, and
the Yamashiro Maru is in evidence along the
coast— the forerunner of a foreign fleet and per-
haps a foreign supplanter.
The second Maoriland election under
woman’s suffrage goes to further dispel four old-
established theories : (1) Women in general don’t
(in M.L. at all events) refuse to use the suffrage ;
(2) they don’t always vote the Tory ticket; (3) the
most beautiful candidate doesn’t invariably get the
solid woman vote —in fact, the ugly politician with
shabby clothes and bow legs got as much support
as usual; (4) The baby doesn’t fall into the fire
while its mother is away voting any more than
when she is away doing anything else.
Seventeen Chinamen left Brisbane for
Hongkong last week, each taking with him a per-
mit to return. It was well-known that half of
them purposed never to return hence the
anxiety they displayed to secure the valuable per-
mit. In time a trade in permits will in China be
as vigorously carried on as the sale of return boat
and railway tickets in Australia.
“ Yooey ” Taylor, who, by his raucous
“object”—it was the only thing he said and he
pronounced it “bjk”—blocked more N.S.W.
Bills than any other Parliamentarian of his time,
announces that he is still “a Freetrader to the
backbone but a Protectionist against the world.”
And this is the sort of man who holds a seat in
Parliament longer than any other.
Recently the Tas. Government sent a
number of unemployed to do track-cutting for
about nothing a week in the Dundas district, and,
with the good old Vandemonian instinct, it had
them escorted by police lest the recipients of
relief should desert by the way. Which is vaguely
reminiscent of a certain Mexican general who was
famous for his success in beating up recruits for
the Juarist army in the days of the Anglo-French
invasion. One of his letters read :“ I send you 24
volunteers, all young men and filled with an
ardent desire to fight for the Republic. The
guard has orders to shoot any who attempt to
escape. I could have sent you more only I had no
more handcuffs.”
Theo. Fink (brother to B.J. of the almighty
ha’penny) was returned to Yic. Parliament on the
broad Liberal ticket at last election. Festive
Theodore misrepresents Jolimont and West Rich-
mond. He has devoted his legislative time to
hedging, bucking and jumping on every important
Liberal measure brought up for consideration.
Lacking the courage to vote, he paired against a
vital principle of the Suffrage Bill; he played his
party false on the tariff issues, and now joins with
shifty Shiels in thwarting the passage of the State
Bank Bill. There is said to be a rod in pickle for
Fink, who—like Alfred Deakin —figures as a legal
champion of shady balance-sheets and directors
in distress.
Vic. Council is subject to childish huffs
when its dignity is trodden on. When the
Factories Bill came before it first it agreed to the
clause providing that the weekly half-holiday
should be fixed by a bare majority of shopkeepers
in any locality, but insisted that the board to fix
wages in any trade should be elected by members
of that trade only. Then the Assembly pointed
out that’this would practically leave the fixing of
wages in the furniture trade to the Chinese, so the
Council reluctantly altered this item. Then, being
huffed at having its mistake pointed out, it pro-
ceeded to blast the shopkeepers’ half-holiday by
requiring the date thereof to be fixed by a two-
thirds majority. There was no earthly connection
between the two matters, but the Council was
mad and had to kick something.
N.S.W. ex-Premier Dibbs, doing the usual
whoop about the “resources of this great country,”
says that Australia could wipe off its entire public
debt any day by selling its railways in London.
This is another feature of the delusion about the
wonderful wealth of Australia. The debt is
<£180,000,000. The railways yield enough to
pay <£l 17s. per cent, on this amount. If J.
Bull will deliberately swap debentures which
bring in a sure <£3 15s. or more per cent,
all round, for the worry of owning and work-
ing a railway system which will give less
than half as much, then he is a stupendous ass.
The railways might fetch their cost —about 112
millions —but even that isn’t likely. When will
the Australian cease to talk gorgeous lunacy about
the wealth of his dry, parched, ordinary continent?
The M.L. Cold Tea party was a dejected
and none too affable crowd the morning after the
elections. It went down heavily in the National
Option poll; instead of sweeping the board, the
teetotallers, so far as the first count shows, failed
to carry Prohibition anywhere, and only carried a
vote for a reduction in the number of licensed
houses in one town, and not much of a town at
that. There were 23 more or less virulent teeto-
tallers in last Parliament; in this one they number
only 12 so far as is known at present. Moreover,
Robert Stout, the great high-priest of the party,
only struggled into second place for Wellington
City, 105 votes behind that objectionable ship-
rigger and ex-sailor, John Hutcheson; and if he
had been a little further down the list he would
have been out altogether. Yet the sun rose next
morning just as usual, and looked down on the
wreck of the Cold Tea party as if nothing had
happened.

Extraordinary Cure in
Sydney .
RECOVERY AFTER ALMOST EVERY
KNOWN REMEDY HAD
BEEN TRIED.
DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS FOR PALE
PEOPLE ACHIEVE A GREAT
VICTORY IN OUR MIDST.
INSOMNIA, DEBILITY, EXHAUSTION,
DYSPEPSIA, INDIGESTION,
PALPITATION OF THE HEART, &c., &c.
THOROUGH INVESTIGATION BY A
NEWSPAPER REPORTER.
(From the Australian Courier).
Hearing of a remarkable cure in the suburbs of
Sydney, and being anxious to verify the report,
we despatched a representative to the residence of
the lady in question—Mrs. Margaret Layfield, 69
Surrey-street, Darlinghurst—who, in response to
a request for a history of her sickness and re-
covery, told the following story, which is given in
her own words :
“ Up to the spring of 1894 I was always com-
paratively healthy, and could carry out my house-
hold duties without feeling in the slightest degree
fatigued, and would enjoy a sound and restful
night’s sleep. Then I began to suffer from weak-
ness, and a feeling of depression. I had a fulness
and pain at the chest and round the sides ; I was
also troubled with palpitation of the heart and a
sense of overbearing exhaustion, lost a great deal
of sleep ; in fact, I kept tossing about the beu all
night. In the morning I woke unrefreshed, and
looked at the day before me as one of positive
torture. Frequently I had to remain in bed all
day, and call in the assistance of a neighbor,
being really too weak to do anything; I also had a
terribly languid feeling. Having inherited a good
constitution, and being always averse to calling in
a medical man, I endeavored to battle through
my troubles, trusting that a certain amount of
determination would assist me to throw off this
terrible nausea. Exhausted nature, however, at last
gave way, and I was on the eve of sending for a
doctor, when a friend drew my attention to a re-
markable cure of dyspepsia and nervous debility
effected by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People. I got some of the Pills as an experiment,
and at once proceeded to take them in accordance
with the printed instructions. After a few days,
and before I had completed the first box, I felt a
visible improvement, my appetite returned, and
once more food agreed with me. I continued
taking the Pills, and am now happy to relate that
I seem as well and strong as ever, and have suffered
no more from that terrible indigestion. I feel
deeply grateful that chance threw in my way Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills, as I tell everyone ; in fact, I
should consider myself a heartless woman if I did
not make this matter public, and endeavor to alle-
viate the troubles of my suffering sisters.
“ I now feel happy and contented, being restored
to health, and can fully appreciate those beautiful
lines of the poet Thomson, ‘ Health is the vital
principle of bliss.’ ”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People are
not a patent medicine, but are a thoroughly
scientific preparation, the result of years of careful
study on the part of an eminent Edinburgh Uni-
versity physician, and they were successfully used
by him in his every-day practice for years before
being offered for general sale. They positively
cure Rheumatism, Sciatica, Lumbago, and Neu-
ralgia, and are a specific for all diseases of women,
such as anaemia, poor and watery blood, female
irregularities, nervous headache and hysteria.
They are not a purgative medicine, but brace up
and permanently strengthen the whole system.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are obtainable from
all leading chemists, or from the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney, who will
forward (post paid) on receipt of stamps or post
order, 1 box for 2s. 9d., or J-dozen for 14s. 9d.

Public Notices.
pUBLIC SERVICE BOARD.
50 Young Street, Sydney,
17th December, 1896.
The Public Service Board hereby notify that the first
general competitive examination for admission to the
Public Service of the colony of New South Wales will be
held on the 27th, 28th, and 29th days of JANUARY, 1897,
at various centres, which will be fixed to suit the conveni-
ence of candidates. The number of appointments to be
made is 20, all of which will be in a junior capacity. Can-
didates must be over sixteen and under twenty-one years
of age on the first day of examination ; be of good moral
character, and in sound health, and have resided in the
colony for at least three years at date of application.
Every person who desires to submit himself to examina-
tion must forward to the Secretary to the Board, to reach
him not later than the 12th JANUARY, 1897, a statement,
in his own handwriting, giving his full name and address,
and his age, and notifying in which of the principal
towns of the colony he wishes to be examined. He must
also submit with his application an extract from the
register in which his birth is recorded, or other satisfac-
tory evidence of his age ; a certificate to the effect that
he is of good moral character, signed by the headmaster
or teacher, if he is attending school, or has ceased to
attend school for a shorter period than six months, by his
employer if he is at present engaged in business duties, or
by a clergyman or justice of the peace if it is more than
six months since he left school and he is not in employ-
ment ; and a certificate from a reputable person to the
effect that the applicant has resided in the colony for at
least three years.
Every candidate whose name is not registered by the
Board will be so informed, with the reason why registra-
tion was refused. A list of registered candidates will be
advertised, with the times and places of examination ;
and, in addition, every registered candidate will be per-
sonally notified of the time and place when and where he
must present himself.
The subjects in which candidates will be examined are
Handwriting; English (dictation, grammar, and composi-
tion) ; Arithmetic (full course) and Euclid (first book)
Elementary Bookkeeping and Accounts ; Outlines of
Physical and Political Geography, and the Geography of
the Australasian colonies, particularly of New South
Wales; and English History from the middle of the
Eighteenth Century to the present time, especially with
reference to the Colonies and Dependencies. As an extra
subject, candidates will be allowed to take Mathematics,
Latin, French, German, Elementary Chemistry, or Ele-
mentary Geology and Mineralogy ; and they may also
elect to be examined in Shorthand. Every candidate
who wishes to be examined in a special subject or in
Shorthand must give notice of his desire in his application.
The fee for examination will be 10s., which must be paid
to a Clerk of Petty Sessions or into the Treasury, and the
receipt lodged with the Secretary of the Board prior to
the date of examination. A successful candidate must
produce a medical certificate to the effect that he is in
sound health and free from physical defects before he will
be allowed to begin his duties.
The regulations governing the examinations will appear
in a special issue of the Government Gazette.
9a22 CHARLES LYNE, Secretary.
