SPORTING NOTIONS.
A farcical prologue to the Martin-Parsons match
was perpetrated on a gullible public earlier in the week,
when “ the crack Victorian cyclist ” went through a form
of beating the mile record in a trial spin. Something had
to be done to excite interest in the coming gate-money
struggle, so Parsons ran a mile in the alleged time of
lmin. 53sec. The time may have been correctly taken, or
otherwise. Some people made it very much otherwise.
Anyhow, this “splendid performance” wasduly chronicled
by the little fraternity of bike reporters, and the public
were warmly exhorted to roll up and see the wondrous
record-breaker smash Bihnartin. Oddly enough, no
stories of gentle William’s prowess on the track found
their way into print. It was enough that the Victorian
had achieved his mile in lmin. 53sec.
The Melbourne public swallowed the bait
greedily, and a great crowd lined the St. Kilda ground
last Saturday. Parsons and Martin were once again to
meet in three races of 1,5, and 10 miles. 20,000 people,
or so, turned pale as the record-breaker and the Yank
came forth to test their rival powers of speed and endur-
ance over the mile course. Hero Parsons dashed away at
a funereal pace, and hero Martin crawled after him for
about five-sixths of the allotted distance. Then the
leader cycled his hardest, and the Yank, following suit,
pipped him on the post by twelve inches. Martin had
shown himself slightly faster than Parsons in a short
sprint, and the board showed 3min. 23 3-ssee., exactly a
minute and a-half slower than the aforesaid record which
had been put up for advt. purposes.
Presently the adversaries came out to race the
five-mile event, or, rather, sundry tandems, triplets, and
“quads” came out to pace, whilst Martin and Parsons
prepared themselves to follow their respective leaders.
The five-mile performance was a success. Martin’s
changing triplets want faster than Parsons’s “quads”
until the American hanger-on was a quarter of a mile in
front of the Victorian hanger-on, after which the latter
ceased from pedalling. Martin, preceded and supported
on the right hand by pacemakers, won the race and the
rubber in lOmiti. 23sec., beating the previous Australian
record by 2sec. He shook hands with Parsons, who con-
soled himself by explaining that his defeat was more or
less due to his pacemakers. They had taken him too far
out on the newly-laid asphalt, he said (which was quite
true). Also, he expressed his readiness to meet Martin
again at any time in three 1-mile heats, for Parsons, as a
popular idol, is loth to expire. Nevertheless, he may,
the present scribe sadly fears, consider himself dead and
gone forever, like many another fiction “champion.”
Parsons was a Victorian newspaper fiction from the first.
The five-mile was a pathetic, almost a brutal,
exhibition, and was reminiscent of the few fistic battles
when hopelessly inferior men get pounded round the ring
by merciful bruisers who vainly try for the point to “ put
the poor blanker asleep.” After a mile and a-half, Par-
sons showed signs of weakening ; and at three miles
hopelessly collapsed Thence out, he followed hopelessly,
pale and open-mouthed, and lost ground at every turn of
the pedal.
The fact, is that nobody is really aware of Mar-
tin’s capabilities. At present Bill is anxiously looking
for a “ mug” who will bet hi n that he can’t do five miles
under lOmin., or 10 miles under 21min., and when he has
cleaned out that incredulous person, Martin will probably
take down some simpleton who thinks he can’t do a mile
in a minute or ride up a church-spire in 39sec. It’s only
a matter of dollars.
According to the look «.,f things, Australia should
be holding some world’s records at no distant date. With
improved tracks and such pacing as was given Martin on
Saturday, it shouldn’t bustle the Yankee very badly to
cut down Michael’s 9min. 13sec. for the five miles.
In addition to his jaw, Martin rnust possess an
iron constitution, iron muscles, and an iron heart. After
lowering the Australian 10-mile record, Bill didn’t trouble
to get off his machine, but simply plugged it slowly
round to the start of the 5-Mile Scratch and had a go
at that. Nearly everybody was sorry he didn’t win it,
too, for he rode strongly and gamely and was only beaten
when the sprint came.
In this event Kellow, who finished third, backed
himself for a big amount, but his work seemed so heavy
that he couldn’t get in his customary sprint. Dismount-
ing, he found that his back wheel required to be dragged
to move, and, investigating further, he discovered a long
length of copper wire twisted round his hub. In case this
wasn’t enough to block him, the gentle “stiffener” had
provided for a fall by unscrewing the cotter-pins of his
pedal.
* * * *
Martin, having beaten Parsons in the one and
five mile fixtures, completed his contract by beating the
10-mile record handsomely. That is to say, the pace-
makers beat the record, and the man with the iron jaw
hung on to a steady succession of triplet wheels with in-
domitable pluck. The race against Time was rather
wearisome, but it had redeeming points about it. The
pacers were neatly and decently arrayed in pale blue, and
they took up the running, turn and turn about, as though
the glory of Biljmartin were their own. Pacemakers
somehow remind one of the Church of England burial
service. They bring everything into the world of bicycle
sport, yet they carry nothing worth mentioning out of it.
The triplets make the cause, while Bilmartin accom-
plishes the effect.
* * * *
The foreigners, Porta and Martin, remain boss
favorites with the multitudes. Porta, particularly, is the
beloved of their soul. His two victories in the St. Kilda
Wheel and International Scratch were probably the most
popular results of honest plugging that have happened in
Melbourne up to date. Twice during the handicap final
the Italian seemed to be in a hopelessly bad position, but
each time he managed to get through and placp himself
in front again, with a vigorous outside rush. The second
of these semi-circular efforts carried the toiler home
amidst shouts of “ He deserved it.” That’s the point.
Porta and Martin are the men who give the most strikirg
exhibitions of work for their prize-money. They supply
the sensation scenes, and a bike-race, as a rule, is a dull
affair without them. Of Australian riders Walne is about
the best entertainment at present.
* * * *
The tower of a church overlooking the St.
Kilda ground was crowded with a select coterie, who saw
Bilmartin beat Parsons for nothing. Trust a church for
getting a deadhead show.
* * * *
Five serious accidents (three fatal) to more or
less eminent persons were reported locally or per cable
one day last week in the Sydney papers.
* * * *
In his match with Martin, Parsons was foolish
enough to ride with a 92 gear, and that after just having
left the doctor’s hands. J.W. should remember the
advice of Zimmerman, who knows more than he does —
“ Stick to the low gear—flash, don’t plug.”
* * * *
The League of Yic. Wheelmen numbers now
2144 members—l 667 of whom come from clubs, while 467
are unattached. The League has a credit balance of
£628.
* * * *
A Southern cycling sheet, which is evidently
touting for the support of the Very Nicest people, spreads
itself out into a fulsome glorification of the amateur
cycling men, the lily-white youths who shave their legs
before appearing on the track being carefully dubbed
“ Mr.” while the common professional has to wander
through the columns as “J. W. Parsons” or merely
“Martin.” In a recent issue appears a horrified wail for
fear “ Mr. Berry, the son of the Speaker,” should carry
out his threat to turn pro., and concluding, “after all,
Mr. Berry will find more advantage and respect by re-
maining with his own people.” And yet that paper is
the official organ of the (professional) L.V.A. !
* * * *
Bicycle training-tracks about Melbourne are
haunted by a few bold-faced damsels, who stand around
in perfect composure watching the parade of scantily-clad
scorchers. Sometimes an accident or a displacement of
his small handful of clothes causes a rider to precipitately
dive for the dressing-room, in which case the female
visitors are the only persons present who seem disinclined
to blush.
* * * *
“ What strikes the visiting cyclist about Aus-
tralians,” remarked a big wheelman, the other day, “ is
the personal interest manifested by the crowd for the
trier. The Australians make gods of big athletes. The
Parisians want to see races and men. The Australians
want the men and races.”
* * » *
At all the Melb. cycling meetings so far held
this season, the reserves have been overrun by book-
makers yelling their prices. And the very worst meeting
yet in this respect was the amateurs’ gathering at St.
Hilda.
» * * »
“Many wom-n would never have fallen had
they not mounted the bicycle,” howls the humorless
Women’s Rescue League. Very likely, since to fall off
anything, even a pulpit or a pinnacie of stupidity, vou
must first get on it.
» * * *
Three months ago it was widely paragraphed
that the Melb. Austral wheel race would include' Michael,
the two Lintons, Huret, Jaequeldi, and almost every
English and continental cyclist of whom Australia has
heard. On his return from America, too, Parsons was
reported to have stated that John S. Johnson was follow-
ing per next boat. But not one of the men mentioned
has touched Australian shores, and apparently none of
them ever intended to, unless it was the Linton who died.
* * * *
A cycling marriage, the second in Vic., took
place at Violet Town on Monday week, the happy couple
mounting their wheels at the church door and pedalling
off on their honeymoon. The first marriage took place at
Bacchus Marsh, and next day the local rag, chronicling
the arrival of the pair at Ballarat, remarked expressively
that “making due allowance for stoppages, six hours was
very fair time.”
» * • *
Bilmartin, whose simple and serviceable suit of
blue serge with which he landed in Australia will be well
remembered by many, has begun to blossom out into
something of a dude. Bill now puts up at a swagger
hotel, and strolls out on St. Hilda Esplanade on Sundays
in tan boots, a straw decker and a tail-coat. A few more
big handicaps, and the iron-jawed one will swell it in
frock-coat, spats and belltopper. Australia is verily a
fine country for the working man.
’* * " * *
The bun for cheek is awarded to the message
sent by the N.S.W. League of Wheelmen (Mr. Percy
Hunter) to Justice Hodges on the occasion of his cycling
smash-up : —“ League of N.S. Wales Wheelmen expresses
deep sympathy with your Honor in painful accident, and
records its disapprobation of the reprehensible practice of
coasting in inhabited centres.” “ Reprehensible habit of
coasting ” is delicious. What does the League say about
the criminal practice of scorching in metropolitan
thoroughfares ?
* » * *
The re-appearance of Bilmartin from his forced
retirement may give a fillip to public interest, but pre-
sently sporting folks will grow very weary of witnessing
“ all the champions ” in perpetual competition. If they
ran straight and up to “ form ” every time they would
soon be a monotonous show. On the other hand, incon-
sistent performances, apart from pacing considerations,
make the sport too farcical for serious discussion among
men, and so, in either case, crowds at bike races are
bound to diminish as the season advances. Women up
to now have been mainly accountable for splendid gate
receipts, but even the fair sex must tire after a while of
gloating upon naked legs.
* * * *
A golden-haired damsel of West Melb. is wanted
on a charge of stealing 15 sovereigns from the pocket of
William Martin. There was no necessity for l’lugger Bill
to arise the following day and advertise that “ he was not
the William Martin,” &e. Everybody who has ever heard
of Bill knows well enough that, even if any fluffy-headed
girl did manage to get within a yard of his manly bosom,
she would stand a better chance of taking Tatt.’s big
sweep than of easing the gentle William of 15 of his hard-
won sovereigns.
* * » »
Cyclist Porta, coming from W.A. to Melb. re-
cently, was very seasick. The second day he observed
pathetically to a fellow-passenger : “ Ven I sprint on land
I do vin someding, but ven I sprint on my stomach I lose
everytings ! ”
* * * *
The N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union had the second day
of its lame and impotent meeting last Saturday. The
Sydney public stayed away comprehensively ; only some
2000 people had nothing better to do than come to the
Sydney Cricket Ground. Clearly, the sweat of death is
upon the brow of the lily-white amateur cyclist, who can’t
cycle.
The least curseable part of the programme was
the lap scratch-races ; and the final of these was won by
Cameron, who, riding with no judgment and little pace,
naturally beat the others, who rode with bad judgment
and no pace. Cameron, by the way, seems to have a
tendency to bore his competitors into the rails.
Has the Union no eyes that it could not see the
barefaced pacing in the Union Wheel race?
M. M. Roseingrave, the Irish athlete and
pigeon-shot, had some more pole-jumping exercise, and
raised the stature, of his former pole-jump by a liair’s-
breadth. It was advertised in the programme that M. M.
R. pole-jumped by special request, but nobody except
Roseingrave seemed to take any special interest in the
pole-jump proceedings.
» » * *
Kellow -will be prepared to tackle Bilmartin,
Lewis, Parsons, or anybody else, in a match for the cham-
pionship as soon as he can see a sufficiently fat stake
hanging to the “ honor.” If the M.H.C. were game to put
up 4:50 i for a two-mile scratch-race Australia would pro-
bably discover a real local champion.
» * * *
The amount netted by each member of the Aus-
tralian Eleven, over and above ordinary hotel exes., was
t (>3IJ. Yet one of them had the nerve to tell a Maoriland
press interviewer that anybody would be welcome to do
his share of the work on the same terms next time a team
goes to England. They rate themselves at full value,
these young professors of an easy “ science.” A capacity
for devoting a simple mind and a commonplace body to
the game, the whole game, and nothing but the game, is
the chief essential for a good cricketer.
• • * *
Cabled that H. H. Stephenson, captain of the
first team of English cricketers to visit Australia (1862),
has died, aged Oil. Stephenson's team was a private spec,
of Spiers and Pond, of the Caf<s de Paris, Melb., who
thereby obtained their first big leg-up. They sent their
manager, Mallam, to England to negotiate. One of the
team, Charles Lawrence (now a State pensioner), remained
in Australia and for years held an appointment on the
railways at Newcastle.
» * « «
An infantile ad. from last week's journals:
“ Racing men are hereby notified that the Patronage of
the L.V.W. has Not yet been granted to the Melbourne
Bicycle Club for their race-meeting on Ist and 2nd
January, 1897.” Wrangles between the League and the
Club merely expose the trumpery character of both insti-
tutions. If the St. Hilda Cricket Club, with its asphalted
track, had a mind to become the supreme power in bike-
racing by arranging a series of substantial prize-meetings,
the L.V.W. and M.B.C. couldn’t make the least difference
one way or other. If the quadrangle and track at the
Exhibition Building were bigger, J. C. Williamson, or any
other showman who could make arrangements to lease
the ground and run an attractive show every Saturday,
might crown himself king of the sport. And the fact that
nobody launches out in this way directly indicates a want
of faith in the stability of bike-racing as a draw. Public
taste is likely to turn against it pretty soon.
* * * *
The interprovincial cricket match N.S.W. v. S.
Aus. rather points the moral that the accumulation of fat
is death to the accumulation of runs. Iredale, who
weighs some two stone more than he did on leaving Aus.,
was bowled first ball first innings, and took one hour and
31 minutes to score 21 second innings. Gregory, who also
weighs considerably more, got 0 and 3. Kelly, who turns
the scale at 13st. odd, was, of course, run out for 3 second
innings. And the only man to distinguish himself in
batting on the N.S.W. side was the colt Noble, who made
in his second innings 69.
For S. Aus., Blondin Lyons, who didn’t go to
England, made top score, first innings, with 78, chiefly
because M’Kibbin was laboring under a two stone handi-
cap on a hot day. Giffen, also much heavier than
formerly, couldn’t scrape up more than 3 on his first

The composition of the N.S.W. team is open to
very much question. Garrett is captain because he can
win the match for his side when his side wins the match
for him. Coningham is in because his legs have grown
no shorter, despite continual chasing of the ball. And
Turner is in because he has consistently failed in club
matches all the present season.
* * * *
When last in Tasmania tenor Newbury played
two single-wicket matches. In the first match, at Zeehan,
Newbury won the toes, went in, and was bowled first
ball. Opponent folio s -lit. Newbury goes in again—is
bowled first ball. Ne b .y fields for a second time, and
his opponent is once more bowled first ball!—a record.
In Hobart the vocalist loses the toss and goes into the
field, and remains there —for one ball. He then goes to
bat, and remains there —also for one ball. Newbury fields
and bowls again. His first ball is played, likewise his
second ; but the third—a combination of Jones, Richard-
son, M‘Kibbin, and one or two others—takes the wicket.
Capt. Newbury’s side goes to bat again—gracefully mows
the first ball to square-leg, and wins the match by one
wioket and four runs.
♦ * * *
It is about time that the V.R.C. handicapper
reduced his opinion of chairman Miller’s decent little horse,
Preston, who has scored only one win out of many attempts
since he had the misfortune to dead-heat with Quiver (for
2nd place) in the Veteran Stakes at Flemington, thirteen
months ago. Legitimate handicapping seems bound to be
rough on any good goer that lacks brilliancy.
« * » *
Sam Cook, apparently, had an idea of finding a
Westralian purchaser for The Merry Boy. The nuggety
son of Trenton was entered, and duly scratched last week,
for Perth Cup. Last week he was entered, along with
infirm Harvester, for the V.R.C. B igot Handicap.
* * * *
Never Mind, by Van Cleve, the sire of Fritz,
made an easy win of the Australian Trotting Association
Cup at Richmond, Vic. Strange it is Van Cleve never
turned out a trotter whilst at Hobartville. Childe Harold
and Van Cleve were at Hobartville together, and many trot-
ting men believe that numbers of horses credited with
being Childe Harold stock are really Van Cleve’s. Certain
it is that many of the so-called Childe Harold get show a
remarkable likeness to the Van Cleves in conformation and
gait.
* * * •
To-day (Saturday), at Randwick, a field of high-
class two-year-olds will contest the valuable December
Stakes. Fleet Admiral (own brother to the Admiral) has
journeyed all the way from Adelaide to run, but in Patriot
he will find a formidable foe. Phillip, a Cranbrook colt
that will do big things later on ; Amberite, a smart son of
Carbine; and the recent winner, The Tola, make up a
powerful opposition, but the result will probably lie be-
tween Fleet Admiral and Patriot, of whom the latter may
just prove the better. A whispered dangerous outsider
is Burleigh, a staunch staying son of Lochiel.
* * * •
Handicappers are prone to underrate horses that
have been, for some reason or another, “on the shelf”
for some time. The scratching of Projectile and Ronda
has probably extricated the A.J.C. and Tatt.’s handi-
cappers from an awkward predicament, but in giving the
erstwhile champion sprinter Whakawatea only Bst. 71b.
the weight-adjuster has done his best to make a present
of the Carrington Stakes to this horse’s owner, provided
that the animal be all right, which is reported to be the
case.
* * * •
In the event of Cartridge and The Chief being
absentees for Villiers Stakes this rich prize looks like fall-
ing to the two-year-old Syerla, who is very well and
nicely treated in the way of weight. The Summer Cup
should fall to Sabretache, Fort or Cravat, whilst Whaka-
watea, Astronomer and The Chief have excellent chances
in the Carrington. There are also some prospects for
Akarini, who may just about win if Whakawatea has lost
all form. Should Fort miss the Summer Cup he should
carry off Tatt.’s trophy, as he is one of the few genuine
stayers at present in training.
* * * *
On two back-lane five-shilling totes in Sydney
£969 was invested last Saturday. The small punter pre-
fers to tote his half-crown rather than take Ikey Alo’s
ridiculously short straight-out prices.
* * * *
That handsome, but unlucky, mare, Response, is
just now in first-rate plight, and, though not too easily
treated with Bst. 111 b. in Tatt.’s Cup, yet her winning
chance is not to be despised.
* * * *
Joe Donnelly’s latest effort, the 22-footer Effie,
built to the order of Mr. M'Murtrie, showed what a flyer
she must be by donning sail first time the other Saturday,
and fairly running away from the 22-foqt fleet. Donnelly
is the genius who first left the beaten track of narrow-
gutted, narrow-tucked boats in vogue a score or more
years ago, and launched forth with his broad-tucked,
beamy, flange-bowed boats, since copied by all our
builders. Donnelly has now built the four best boats of
their class in the harbor —the Ida, 24ft.; Effie, 22ft.; Aus-
tralian 111., 18ft.; and the Wanda, 14ft.
* * * *
The treatment of A. W. Eales by the N.S.W.
Gun Club is one of the many unsportsmanlike acts per-
petrated in the name of Australian sport. The Club has
made a new mark, 34yds., for Eales only, and although it
may start him on 32yds. or 31yds. any quarter, he is
promptly shunted back on the first reasonable oppor-
tunity. Eales is quite willing to always stay on 32yds.,
the limit for such world-famous shots as Carver and
Brewer, and points out that, unlike these, he is a mere
amateur who does not live on the game. The unfair
treatment of Eales is brought about by those amateurs of
the N.S.W. Gun Club who do live on the game as much
as they can.
* * * *
The Sydney Enterprise Swimming Club put on
a good bill at the Natatorium last Wednesday week ; most
interest, of course, was in the meeting of Jack Hellings
and Percy Cavill in the 440vds. N.S.W. Championship.
Some seven men started, but Hellings and Cavill almost
at once got away from the others, and a grand struggle
ensued. The two raced neck and neck, down and up, up
and down : beginning the last lap Cavill by accident
kicked Hellings in the wind. This sad event settled
Hellings’ chance ; he struggled with all his wonted game-
ness, but was beaten by a couple of yards. He was so
done up that he had to be lifted out of the water. The
only lady-performer was “Connie” Stennett, infant
daughter of R. A. Stennett, of sporting name. The baby
did many clever feats ; one was rescuing her burly parent
from drowning; it was a practical illustration of the
mouse rescuing the lion.
*i * * •
Harry Lindgren, the sturdy No. 7 of several
Vic. interprovincial eights, is the latest oarsman to aban-
don amateurism and go over to the cash cyclists. If the
rule debarring cash cyclists is insisted upon, the cream of
Vic. rowing will soon be the spindle-shanked harrier
crowd who now hang round waiting for emergency seats
in maiden eights.
* * » *
At Murchison (W.A.) part of the racecourse has
been taken up by a local gold-mining co., and the racing-
club applied to the manager thereof for permission to
hold the meeting as usual. The co.’s manager received
the application in a frame of mind that was partly festive
and partly grumpy, and consented on these terms :
T. That a sum of » 500 be forthwith paid to me.
2. That a screen 100 ft. high, formed of galvanized-iron
and impervious to sound, be erected across the company’s
property.
3. That a grand-stand be erected of stone, to contain
billiard-room, swimming-bath, and orchid-house, design
to be approved of by me and the same to become the pro-
perty of the company.
4. That a fence be erected round the property, to be
composed of round timber not less than 14ft. long and not
less than Bin. at the thin end ; to be connected with Bulli-
vant’s steel mining rope.
5. That a sum of £250 be paid to this company’s officers
for loss of time.
6. That free drinks and banquets be allowed officials of
the company and their friends during the carnival and
until further notice.
7. That no horse run without having his boiler tested.
8. That no yacht racing be permitted and that steam
launches do not exceed 20 miles an hour.
9. That no barges be moored in front of the company’s
residence.
10. That the officials be allowed to bet on any race after
the event.
11. That the monopoly of the three-card trick and
thimble-rig be exclusively the property of the officials of
the company.
12. That no clothes or towel-horse be allowed to run
under any circumstances whatever.
13. That all willy-willies, false starts, and scratchings
become the absolute property of the company.
14. That no “ jumping ” be allowed, and that as the
company holds the surface rights, no racing be allowed
above ground, as the company’s levels are most suitable
for flat racing.
15. That the club use their best endeavors to obtain the
discharge of all drunks, pickpockets, Ms.L.A., and others
arrested during the carnival.
* * * *
Boarding-house keeper (to jockey who has lately
come into note): “ And now, Mr. Blank, will you try any
dessert?” Jockey (unused to such attentions): “Yes,
mum, I will, and if you’ll bring back that corned beef and
cabbage you’ve just took out it will just suit.”
**' * *
A very quiet old Vic. civil-servant had a bull-
pup that he wanted to back for a chewing-match against
anything for any money. A well-known horse-trainer,
who thought he was simply picking up money, looked
round and found just the very thing. Dogs were brought
to him and his new purchase chewed them all up—neither
weight nor size could stop him. Money came like water,
and, in “Banjo’s” immortal words, that sport had
“ plenty of milk in his tea.” But when the dogs faced
each other the trainer’s pup simply wouldn’t show fight.
The secret was that he had been beaten by that same dog
three times running. The cunning crowd lost more than
£lOO over the deal.
* * * *
The Walker’s Whisky Trophy, value 50 guineas,
is for 22-foot sailing-boats. The conditions are that it is
to be won first at Sydney in the Intercolonial Sailing Car-
nival, Jan. 23 ; secondly, at Brisbane in ’9B. Should a
different boat win on these occasions, the final is to be
sailed at Sydney in ’99. The winner in '97 will also secure
the Queen’s Ale Prize of 10 guineas.

The State Legislature of Indiana (U.S.A.) has
prohibited horse-racing for five months in the year.
Dear Bulletin, —In issue Oct. 17, “A.”
gives, as an instance of the late Julian Thomas’s
inaccuracy, his statement that the Liverpool to
London express stopped at Shrewsbury, and adds
“ that the absurdity of the yarn is heightened by
the fact that the express train had no lus ness
near Shrewsbury at all.” For some jea.-s I
travelled from Liverpool to London and invari-
ably called at Birkenhead, Chaster, Shrewsbury,
Wolverhampton, Birmingham and Oxford —in
fact, these stations must be passed by those using
the Great Western railway, as Beac-onsfield
usually did. Of course, travellers by the Great
Northern, Midland, or London and North-Western
lines would not go near Shrewsbury.—Salopian,
Fremantle.
Brisbane Magistrate Pinnock’s favorite
sentences are : for drunkenness, £2 or 48 hours ;
for drunk and disorderly, ss. or six hours ; aggra-
vated language, £3 or one month ; plain language,
£2 or 14 days. Most other magistrates are equally
erratic. A few sentences passed in Sydney last
week panned out as follows : £1 or seven days ; £1
or 14 days ; ss. or 21 days ; 255. or 21 days ; £2 or
two months; .£3 or two months; ,£5 or one
month; £5 or two months ; £2 or two months.
Why not pass a law setting forth exactly how
much imprisonment is equal to how much fine, so
that when the alternative is given it will be some-
thing like the same alternative every time?
Twenty-one days can’t be worth 255. in one case
and only ss. in the other.
Mr. Frank Farnell, M.L.A. of N.S.W.,
when interviewed at Sydney on his return from
London, apparently was most anxious to convey
that he was a modest man who loathed self-advt.
“While travelling,” he said, “several papers
asked me for interviews —in America they called
me a capitalist and a Minister of the Crown —and
at last 1 gave one to a weekly called the British
Australasian.” At last is good. The Bulletin
has been sent a card allegedly handed by this
shrinking violet of a politician to a business man
who met him at ’Ome. Here is a verbatim copy :
Member of Legislature N.S.W., Australia
Member of Parliamentary Committee
on Public Works ~
Justice of the Peace ~
Commissioner of Fisheries ~
Trustee of National Park (37,000 acres) ~
Captain St. George’s Rifles ~
Vice-Pres. Royal Agricultural Society ~
Vice-Pres. Sydney Hunt Club ~
Vice-Pres. Horticultural Society ~
Vice-Pres. Anthropological Society ~
Ex-Pres. Royal Commission on Fish-
eries, 1894-95 ~
Ex-Deputy Chairman of Committees
Legislative Assembly ~
And was connected with three governments in the
capacity of “ whip.”
Captain Farnell, M.P., M.P.C.P.W., J.P., C.F.,
T.N.P., C.S.G.K., V.P.R.A.S., V.P.S.H.C.,
V.P.H.S., V.P.A.S., X.P.R.C.F., X.D.C.C.L.A.,
etc., says it is a lie that he ever expressed strong
Republican views. In England they offered him
a seat in the Commons, but who “ they ” were, or
which seat it was, Mr. Farnell doesn’t say.
His pet hobby is the Australian fishing industry,
to promote which, it appears, a .£200,000 com-
pany, with Mr. F. as managing director at £2OOO
a year, is or was on the tapis ; but the project
drags a little owing to the outrageous «ay in
which the promoter has been deceived by the
N.S.W. Govt. To continue supporting that
Government, which has so often deceived him,
says Mr. Farnell, would be foreign to his nature.
So he contemplates retiring from politics and
going into the fish business—oysters, he explains,
were worth 4s. 6d. a dozen when he was in Lon-
don, and prawns 3s. Gd. On the whole, The Bul-
letin congratulates the anti-Republican Farnell
on his determination to abandon politics and trade
in prawns.
Rumor hath it that Cartridge is to be reserved
for Summer Cup. This would seemingly prove a costly
experiment, as he appears to hold a mortgage on the
Villiers Stakes, whereas he has never shown any ability to
compass a mile and three-quarters. The public take
eight to one about this horse for the sprint race, and
won’t they just howl if they don’t get a run for their
money.

From the police-court report of a Western
(N.S.W.) paper: “Prisoner was a depressed-
looking old man with a chronic cold named
George Smith.” This paper having once had a
bad cold named William Jones naturally feels
sympathetic.
Melbourne Presbyterians contemplate
starting a home for young girls—not for those
who have been convicted, but for those who have
been “guilty of comparatively trivial offences.”
Kissing the butcher’s boy would probably be re-
garded as a “ comparatively trivial offence ” by a
Presbyterian elder or parson.
The night-train had stopped for a few
moments at a way-station, when a gawky
“ colonial ” dropped his pipe under the train (on
the rails). In the shake of a tail, he was down
and up again, remarking, as he puffed the lost
pipe, “That’s luck—ain’t out.” Somebody re-
marked, “You might have lost your life.” “Yes,
but I might have lost the pipe, and I only bor-
rowed it—see ! ” and he smiled and puffed away, a
hero in a small way.
The Eye-sight measure sent per post by
the inventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough,
the well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney,
is a wonderful little contrivance which will enable
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection
such as long sight, short sight, old sight. Full
particulars from Messrs. Greenfield and Barra-
clough.*

Writes “ Nothing ” to The Bulletin :
I am told it is easily possible to ascertain the fate and
find the remains of the missing Lamont Young and party, |
and solve the “Bermagui Mystery.” The searchers
hitherto made the most “nohow” enquiries and were
easily put off the scent. The fact is that, just after the
disappearance of Young, the Bermagui camp and all the
surrounding district were in a state of terrorism in respect
of the crime, for crime it was and no mistake. Whenever
any digger, in his cups, hinted that he “ could speak more
if he liked ” —you know the sort of thing I mean—that
digger would be himself “missing” in a day or two.
Several cases like that occurred, and men became wise.
There was much gold being got, but men kept their
mouths shut as to how much. Young courted his fate by
always being too ready to flash his gold, he being a large
gold-buyer. To-day, Bermagui field is practically
deserted, and, at any rate, the district is “safe.” Were
any keen-brained man to go up there along with one of the
old residents who remembered all about everything—
there must be many, since I, a stranger to Bermagui,
know several —he might “ find out.” Is there still out the
Government reward of £2OO ? If so, that fact should in-
duce someone to follow up this hint. Further, for any
guidance :It was murder. The murder was committed
on one side of the river; the boat was taken out and
beached far away on the other side of the river—bottomed
up, and rifle-bullet holes shot in the ribs. That simple
manoeuvre seems to have fooled all the sleuth-hounds !
For the rest, terrorism kept the local residents and diggers
reticent, for there was evidently an unknown gang to fear.
The actual murderer was known right enough.—P.S.—No
fuss was made about the loose-tongued diggers who went
“missing” (by the way, the ferryman also); they were
nobodies and were simply “counted out” of the few
thousands of folk on the rush.

AN INTERESTING NOTICE OE A MOST

MARVELLOUS TESTIMONIALS.
FLATTERING PRESS OPINIONS.
- - - MIRACULOUS CURES.
A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfaline Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice ; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been "'ceived, and maybe seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for thtse health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his, own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W, A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W.A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common?” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,
when three hunters and trappers were travelling about
the ranges near my place on the Rose River, one was taken
very ill ; his mates intended taking him in a waggon to
Wangaratta (about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor.
I gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to inform
you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found
him up and pegging-out his skins and looking well. This
speaks volumes for your valuable Remedies.”

A backblocks (Q.) town which had suffered
from a three months’ drought was proposing to
pray for rain. A local farmer, hearing of the pro-
position, wrote indignantly to the Bugaboo Star
m protest. “Why, Mr. Editor,” he wrote, “I
got an acre and a-half of wheat in just now, and if
there was rain for the next few days through
them 1 harisees a-praying the whole blame crop
would be spoiled ! ”

The N.S. W. “ Mountain Mystery,” if pro-
ductive of no other result, will be of great assist-
ance to the Government Statistician, as proving
the large number of people who go prospecting.
So far, just about 153 persons had entered into
negotiations with Butler, but something prevented
them going, which only shows what a beastly
adventurous and speculative people we are. Of
the number who went, no reliable figures can be
quoted the returns are coming in slowly.
Some fine shipments of the well-known
Rambler bicycles have just reached N.S.W. and
Queensland. The Rambler is one of the most
serviceable and reliable cycles on the market, and
the man or woman who wants to buy a wheel for
speed and safety should not make a purchase until
he or she has examined a Rambler.*
Visitors to Sydney, who desire all the
comforts and good feelings of a home, should stay
at the Hotel Australia. This is the finest hotel
in the .Southern Hemisphere, not because its
appointments are the best, its position the best,
its lighting, baths, &c., &c., the most modern, but
because the charge, 12s. 6d. per day upwards, is
within reach of everybody possessing reasonable
means A good holiday can be ruined by a bad
hotel, but a good hotel makes a good holiday all
the more enjoyable.*

Tke sterling merits of the Yost typewriter,
and the magnitude of its sales, place this machine
first, and the rest nowhere. The public through-
out the five Australian provinces are adopting it
and all the experts speak of the “beautiful work
of the Yost.’’ It has no troublesome ink-ribbon •
its type strike the paper direct, producing the
cleanest and sharpest of impressions. The United
Typewriter and Supplies Co., sole agents for
Sydney Melb. and Perth, have furnished Yosts
to the Westralian Governor, the various colonial
Govt. Depts., newspaper offices, leading banks
insurance co’s, commercial firms, &c., &c.*
The triennial travelling scholarship offered
to students at Melb. National Gallery falls this
time to George Coates, for a picture somewhat
reminiscent-of Longstaff’s “Breaking the News.”
Coates gives a gloomy treatment of the prize
subject At Last. A stockman, badly injured
by a fall from his horse, lies upon a mattress in
his hut. bully dressed in moleskins, Crimean
shirt, leggings, etc., the sufferer, with one leg
drawn up convulsively, has a death-tinted face
Anxious wife in the background, and elderly male
friend holding the groaner’s wrist. Doctor hust-
ling through the doorway. A strong work at the
most important points, faulty in the background
scheme, but fairly entitled to the prize. Miss
Dora Meeson’s cheerful picture of a miner bursting
into his domestic circle with a nugget which he
has just struck, takes second place from only three
other competitors.

One Gunther, of Melbourne, has given the
world an antidote to those wearisome young men
who get invariably kicked off the premises by
their best girls’ papas. The other night, when
Gunther brought Miss Maud Lobb home from a
moonlight ramble to the parental roof, he was
more or less insulted and assaulted by the “old
man.” Whereupon he pasted Lobb pttre (under
the patronage of all the lady members of the
family) until his father-in-law-elect streamed with
gore. Also a charge of “unlawfully wounding ”
brought by Lobb against his daughter’s admirer
resulted in a moral victory for the accused. This
is rather hard upon , the everlasting music-hall
ditty, in which Augustus always comes to grief
and woe. The bull-dawg that lurks on the other
side of the wall, waiting to seize ’Gustus by the
seat of his pants, is under a cloud in Melbourne
for the present.
The controversy about the danger to health
of some of the sewer-ventilators of Svdney sug-
gests the question of legal liability in the event of
proved'injury. Recently, at Birmingham (Eng.)
Assizes the executors of one Thomas Smith sued
the District Council for £lO,OOO damages in re
his death, which resulted from blood-poisoning,
the alleged consequence of a defective sewer-
ventilator. The jury returned a verdict for £3500.

Victoria now has only 488 aborigines and

Women workers in tobacco-factories are
found to be peculiarly prone to abortion. Inhaled
nicotine seems to be the cause.

Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of GGOft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Eancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,

13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*
One day last week a baby was left on the
doorstep of the W.C.T.U. building, Melbourne.
Pinned to the bundle was a piece of paper in-
scribed : “ Please take care of my child, because
I cannot find a home for it. Good-bye. I am a
poor girl of 16, who has fallen.” Women
Christians and social purists in general may as
well wait for further evidence re the age and
history of this baby-depositor, ere taking the
above statement for granted. The “girl of 16 ”
is perhaps a respectable middle-aged matron or
widow, who loved not wisely in a moment of weak-
ness caused by too much reading of Eamily
Herald supplements.

“Papa’s Hair.”
A
A gentleman has sent us the following :—“ A few
weeks ago I bought a bottle of your ‘ Papa’s Hair,’
and it has acted with the most satisfactory results.
Previous to doing so, my hair had a mousey color
streaked with grey. The dear children would try
to count papa’s grey hairs, but, because of the
number, could not succeed. Being only 44 years
of age, I determined to improve my hair, and tried
your ‘ Papa’s Hair,’ and am pleased to write that,
though at first there seemed little difference, gradu-
ally the hair regained its original color (there was
no dyeing whatever) and it is now strong and glossy.
I asked my little daughter this morning to count
my grey hairs, hut when looking for them she cried
ou t —‘ Why, papa, there are no white hairs; mamma
must have pulled them all out.’ ”
rg" This Preparation is not a Dye, but acts gradu-
ally on the Roots of the Hair, and restores Grey
Hair to its natural color and lustre.
No preparation will make hair grow better than
“Papa’s Hair.”
It effectually prevents the formation of Dandriff,
as it is called,'the presence of which always denotes
diseased action in the cuticle of the scalp, causing
premature baldness, or a falling off of the hair.
DIRECTIONS.—WeII shake the bottle, and with
a small piece of sponge rub into the roots of the
hair ni'dit and morning, till the hair is restored to
its natural color ; aftervi ards apply twice a week.
3s. per Bottle, by post B<t. extra.
FiSbcr* Co.,
Manilla Infers and
Sole I’roprietors,

They are telling a story in London of a
South African diamond merchant who, after
struggling to get into the social swim, was at last
rewarded with a card to a very swell function.
Before the shivoo came off, however, the invitee
got mixed up with a discreditable police-court
matter, and when he was told that there was a
possibility of his invitation being withdrawn, he
remarked “ Dey can’t do it; dey can’t do it. I
haf send it to Zomerzet House and got it stambed,
und it is now a gontract.”
The second chapter of the story of that
British major who died fighting against big odds
in Matabelelarid (vide “Cheer, Boys, Cheers ”),
the time when they started singing the Gnashional
Anthem after the ammunition gave out, is now
published. A scout found the remains of the
major, dead and full of spears arid glory and bits
of unfinished anthem, and when he returned to
England he called on the dead officer’s fiancee
and told the story with such pathos that
he and the bereaved lady were married the
other day in Scotland. The local paper said the
event was surrounded by “ a halo of romance.” If
you can’t marry the man of your choice, the next
best thing is to marry the man who found his
body. That’s romance, and the halo comes in
just there.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman', the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extendi rig over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guararitee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
That long, gushful weakling, ex-Premier
Shiels, lately poured himself forth for over four
hours in Vic. Assembly, the State Bank Bill
being what was the matter with him. He was
very ill at the time, and had to discourse sitting
down; if he had been in good health the general
impression is that he wouldn’t have left off till he
uttered all his vitals and left them on the table.
In a general way, he was against everything, not
wholly on Conservative grounds, but maiuly be-
cause Shiels is a fluent, nervous, shattered man, j
too badly scared to attempt anything new for fear
it is loaded and may go off unexpectedly. Also,
he has a habit of seeing simultaneously all the 74
ways of doing anything, and the 92 ways of
leaving it undone, and the 1187 dangers that may
arise if it is done in any of these 74 ways; and
when he has explained all these things at full
length he invariably advises that it be left a little
longer, and shudders himself away into the corner
where he and his shadow keep each other com-
pany. Poor William Shiels is a walking streak of
fluency and nerves and indecision and wasted
learning, with a vast amount of natural goodness
washing about loose in a sea of eloquence, and a
shaky cough on top of all. Shiels is too good in
many ways for this world, but he ought to make a
very excellent, but undecided, angel in a better
sphere.

Mining yarn from W.A. A shaft was
going down vertically, and above it a whip was
rigged to do the hoisting per horse. Bill was
down below filling dirt (it is so obvious that the
man below must be Bill that this fact is hardly
worth mentioning), and his mate, Jim, was on top
—otherwise the story wouldn’t be complete. Sud-
denly something went wrong with the works, and
the iron-bound tub and 100 feet of rope dived
down the shaft with a crash. It was so sudden
that Jim didn’t even manage to call out the usual
“look out,” and the death of Bill seemed for a
moment too obvious to be worth talking about.
Then suddenly there rose from below a calm,
blasphemous voice—the voice of Bill —and the
words it used were: “ Say, throw down the
blessed horse too, when you’re at it.”
A German surgeon the other day removed
for cancer seven-eighths of the stomach of a
woman 58 years old, and sewed the two ends
together to form a new stomach the size of a hen’s
egg. The same evening the woman actually swal-
lowed and digested some milk and egg, took
a deal of milk food next day, and in a few
weeks left the hospital 221 b. heavier than she
entered it. This is the stomach-cutting record.
Lord Kenyon wound up his sentence on a
butler, convicted of stealing his master’s wine, in
the following unapproachable strain :
Dead to every claim of natural affection, and blind to
your own real interest, you burst through all the re-
straints of religion and morality, and have for many
years been feathering your nest with your master’s
bottles.
About 200 aboriginals recently had a battle
near Kalgoorlie. -They represented two hostile
tribes, almost equal in number. The fight lasted
two hours, during which period the air was thick
with waddies, boomerangs, and spears, and the
slaughter consisted of two men slightly damaged.
Like the Chinaman, the Australian aboriginal is
pretty harmless when he fights ; his most danger-
ous aspect is when he is at peace and gets to
windward, so that the germs blow off him.
A little romance comes from an inebriate
retreat down South. There are two people at
least in every romance ; one of them, in the pre-
sent instance, was a drinky capitalist—a young
man who inherited the pile and the family thirst
together, and who, in a moment of unusual bril-
liance, decided to winter in the retreat, with a
view to sobering up. There he met the other
party to the romance ; she was 27, golden-haired,
and beautiful, barring a transient flush about the
nose, and she was also sobering up. They fell in
love, and resisted their thirst together, and
exchanged experiences, and swore-off for each
other’s sake, and it was agreed that, when they
had kept off long enough to acquire confidence in
themselves, they would marry. The flush disap-
peared off her nose and his beer-pimples faded
away, and they both grew young again. Then
they quitted the institution, married, and started
on their honeymoon. The trouble which naturally
ensued began just 10 days later, at a certain
fashionable hotel. The bridegroom was out one
evening, and the bride was restless, and wandered
about the establishment as if she was looking for
something. This was at 8 p.m., and about
midnight the waiter laboriously carried upstairs to
her bedroom an armful of soft flounces and an in-
sensible bride with the flush returned to her nose.
She had been found lying in a passage with a bottle
in her hand and her head hanging downstairs.
The landlady and other sympathetic females
gathered round and wondered how they would
break the news to her poor husband. Somebody
with great presence of mind went out to look for
the poor husband and tell him about it, but as he
rushed down the front steps he fell over a heap of
something, and came down whack. The heap was
trying to unlock the pavement with its hat and
singing softly to itself. It was the bridegroom, of
course. With the beautiful unity of two lovin’
souls they had got the old thirst simultaneously,
and had fallen at the same time, and in beer they
were not divided.

Business, Bobbery, &c.
Mr. G. E. Ardill forwards the balance-sheets of
the hydra-headed Sydney charitable society of which he
is director; and, though the charity seems to be plentiful,
the balance-sheets leave a good deal to be desired. Occa-
sionally some of them skip a year, and two years’ accounts
are rolled into one ; and occasionally one pops up at only a
six-months interval. Further, in some 14 years, during
which Mr. Ardill has been running the show, there seems
only to have been one definite attempt to sum up assets
and liabilities, and that was when the original Ardill
enterprise was, as it were, floated into a co. six years
ago. The “ balance-sheets ” consist merely of a state-
ment of receipts and expenditure; and exactly how
the society, or any of it, stands in the matter of sol-
vency doesn’t appear. No less than eight charity agencies
apparently flourish or otherwise under one management:
the Home of Hope, Open All-Night Refuge, Woman’s
Crusade, Jubilee Home, Discharged Prisoners’ Mission,
Babies’ Home, Liverpool Home for Orphans, and Camden
Home for Boys, and the funds of the first five are all
lumped together in an indistinguishable heap. The last
two apparently issue a cash account of their own, and it
is vaguely understood that the Babies’ Home is lumped
with one section or the other, but after painstaking search
this paper can’t say decisively where the infants’ cash re-
poses. According to the report of the main batch of societies
to 31st Aug., 1895, which is the last to hand, Government
contributed, between Ist March, ’94, and 31st Aug., ’95,
£1734 in subsidy ; but it does not appear that Govt,
exercises any supervision over expenditure, or is repre-
sented on the committee, or audits the cash-account, or
endeavors to sort the funds out of the heap, or does any-
thing else which might reasonably be hoped for. Director-
of-Oharities Maxted, however, reported on most of the
charities in March, ’93, and found things fairly satisfac-
tory ; and at Aug., ’95, the society was still printing
favorable extracts from his report (without any date
attached), as an evidence of good faith.
The Sydney Rescue Work Society, which is
vaguely understood to run the whole heap of institutions,
took over Ardill’s charitable work in March, 1890. Mr.
Ardill’s zeal was partly demonstrated by the fact that
there had accumulated a large quantity of debts, and he
even seems to have owed himself £507 for money
advanced during seven years. Why the Sydney Rescue
Work Society should have taken over the debts as well as
Ardill doesn’t appear, but it did take over a considerable
quantity of them, and up to the date of last report it
seems to ’ have labored with one eye to charity
and the other to working off the dead horse.
So late as Feb., ’95, a Govt, grant is announced as being
devoted to the payment of “ old debts ” —presumably
those incurred before 1890.
Altogether, there are quite a lot of interesting
points about the Ardill institutions—one of them being
that Mr. George Verey, a member of committee of the
Society for Providing Homes for Neglected Children, is
seemingly identical with a Mr. George Verey who con-
sistently audits the committee’s own balance-sheet.
* » * *
Writes “Snapshot’ from Tasmania: “We are
on the eve of a mining boom—we always are. Almost in
every newspaper we hear of some new find. It is really
very easy to find reefs in Tasmania —easier than at Cool-
gardie, where nine out of ten are blows. In our reefs you
invariably find colors. There is consequently great ex-
citement. All the surrounding country is immediately
pegged out. As a rule it remains pegged out for months
and even years, till the pegs become old and rotten and
the notices are washed away. Then some poor fellow,
prospecting in what he considers eligible country, on
finding a color and applying for a lease is confronted by
a yawning apparition who sternly says “It’s my lease. 5 ’
Then the apparition goes to bed after seeing his notice
renovated. A lease once pegged out in Tasmania remains
more or less pegged out practically for ever, whether it is
worked or not. Consequently, in this Land of Sleep-a-
Lot, the real boom is a long while coming. I never yet
saw a properly-equipped prospecting party in Tasmania,
or one which seemed in real earnest.”
* * * *
How some English co’s are managed in Wes-
tralia. One 40-acre lease, writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent, has two shafts down, one working—three shifts of
four men each. It has a whim-boy and (don’t laugh) a
groom. There are also in course of erection a 10-head
battery of obsolete pattern and a toy winding-gear about
the size of a little steamer’s crab winch, but as the
machinery is being erected by contract the manager is
not called upon to exercise his vast brain on anything but
the mine. To manage this great concern there is a
manager at £l5OO a year, appointed in England (by the
way - ; the battery and toy winch were also appointed in
England, probably from some junk yard); a clerk at £6
a week, appointed by the manager, who had no trouble
in picking him, as he is the manager’s cousin ; and a
mining manager at £7 or £8 a week. All these get, in
addition, water and quarters. The £l5OO a year manager
uses hair-oil, keeps a black servant, and has nothing to do
except steady his hand once a fortnight to write his
report and sign the pay-sheet. Why does not mining in
W.A. pay?
* # * »
Sharebroking— lts Abuses and their Cure,”
by J. Wilson Reid, of Melbourne, is a treatise with many
amusing assumptions popping up in its midst. For
instance—“ The scale of charges, as commission, for buy-
ing and selling shares is not loyally adhered to, and con-
sequently the dishonest jobbing broker takes advantage
of his fellow members to gain and keep business by
cutting rates.” This strikes one as an Abuse which the
public would prefer to see stalking along its wicked way,
un-cured and unmolested. And, by the way, J. Wilson
Reid asks in a loud tone of voice—“ Why should a man
who advertises himself as a broker not give a guarantee
as well as an auctioneer ? The two positions are not dis-
similar.” What guarantee does an auctioneer give that
he will not settle his liabilities with Id. in the £ ? Vide
recent remarks in The Bulletin.
• • • •
A correspondent sends along a circular purport-
ing to be issued by a firm of London sharebrokers who are
apparently looking for flats in this guileless country. It
invites all manner of people to forward any amount of
money for the firm to speculate with on the stock ex-
change, the profits being remitted regularly. The profits
for September alone, it ’seems, were 5 per cent., and for
the first nine months of the year 56 per cent. The firm
expects that the profits for the whole year will be about
92 per cent., the same as last year. The firm never makes
lossesj apparently, and investors can rely on about
doubling their money every year. It could, of course,
swindle them if it liked ; no one knows what the money
is invested in, and ail it need do is to annex the remit-
tance, and write back that the coin was unfortunately
lost, or not write back at all; but it never does such
things. Why a firm should waste its life making 90 per
cent, profits for other people instead of for itself is a
question which the average flat seldom considers. The
discretionary investment business is a very old and
idiotic and transparent affair, but considering the
success of a man who went round Australia a few years
ago getting payment in advance for alleged Servian bonds
it ought to pay well in this new and fresh country.
* * * ♦
A well-known Sydney commercial man has a
terrace of houses, let at £1 19s. lid. each. If they were
let at £2 a 2d. stamp would have to be put on each
receipt; by reducing a penny, he saves stamp-duty. Also,
if a tenant pays two weeks’ rent at once, he will get two
separate receipts, the landlord candidly admitting that
two receipt-forms are cheaper than a 2d. stamp.
* * * *
It has leaked out that one man has been the cause
of numerous retail failures in one Australian city for some
years past. He and his nominees have long been playing
a most flourishing long-firm game, but this promises to
be stopped. He is a man of good means and in business.
He would fix upon his nominee, and advance him, say,
£5O to start with. The latter would magnify this into
£l5O. Credit was easily obtainable in consequence of the
cut-throat competition amongst the wholesalers. Pay-
ments would continue prompt for a few months and pur-
chases would steadily increase. Gradually the account
would get further and further behind, and then the crash;
liabilities about £BOO ; assets —stock not more than £75 —
div. next to nothing. The small stock is easily accounted
for, as the chief robber used to buy goods at half cost
from the nominee. These would go to stock the next ven-
ture. And so the game went on. Emboldened by success,
a more daring game was played. The chief robber would
buy out the nominee at a pre-arranged price, and
creditors simply had to take whatever was offered. The
frequency of these sales aroused the ire of the whole-
salers, who at last refused to accept the composition, but
forced the defaulter into insolvency. The proceedings
there gave the game away, but there being no direct
evidence to incriminate the chief culprit, he goes on his
way untouched.
* * * *
Pritchard Morgan, once a Q’sland lawyer, who
has long adventured in mining, goes to boom China at
the invitation of Li Hung Chang. The game has been
played before. Some years ago a Chinese Commission
with a big retinue of servants travelled through the U.S.
to pick up mining men, plant, and some money. An ex-
pedition went out, had a good time, spent a barrel of
money, but found no payable gold. It strikes one that
John, who can be found on more than half the tailings-
heaps and old alluvial fields of Australasia, working other
people’s leavings, is quite up to working gold in his own
country.
* * * *
One John J. Horrocks, to satisfy “numerous
applications,” has given his observations of the Westra-
lian goldfields. He is surprised that people 3000 miles
away should “glibly talk pessimistically with an assump-
tion of knowledge of the present condition and future
prospects of W.A.” He has seen the most perfect bat-
teries and ore-reduction plants being taken piecemeal by
camel and horse-teams through the desert—machinery
which, in the majority of cases, never would have been
shipped at the enormous expense had not preliminary
trials of the mines to which it was consigned justified the
expenditure. “At the present time 20,000 tons of
machinery is on its way to the Kalgoorlie district for the
proprietary claims alone.” These two “observations”
are quite enough to squelch Horrocks. Yet it is “ yab-
ber” like this, made at the right time and printed in the
right place, which makes a boom. All the machinery on
all the mines at Charters Towers would not amount to
the quantity which, according to Horrocks, is on the way
to one oo.’s mines. Had Horrocks contented himself by
saying Westralia was a great place for the tin-meat man
he would have been less laughed at.
* * * *
“Anxious ” is told by the Sabbath tipster that
the General Gordon is a remarkably good mine, having an
immense body of ore which will go 2oz. to 3oz. to the ton,
and all the disparaging reports are mere idle rumor. The
same paper says the ore body is 43ft. wide and it will
average 3oz. That is the Boulder average with a much
smaller lode ! Ninety per cent, at least of the great
things said of new mines are false, and-more than 90 per
cent, of the bad things said of any undeveloped mine are
as true as gospel. All unprejudiced people who have seen
this over-boomed mine tell the same story to the writer.
It is time the directors had the General Gordon examined
by some expert in W.A. in whom the public have con-
fidence.

A North-West Queensland town. In the main
street stands a wretched specimen of a bush pub.—iron
walls, iron roof, and hardly a dray-load of sawn timber in
the whole concern. If sold to-morrow the shanty
wouldn’t realise £l5O. Yet on this wretched tin-pot
caboose a certain bank advanced something like £lBOO
some few years ago, and it is still owing.
• * * *
The sulphuric acid contained in the fumes from
Mt. Lyell smelting works is already killing the timber in
the vicinity. It is also eating holes in galvanized-iron
roofing, and otherwise making the place a desirable resort
to stay away from. In view of the number of “ leaded ”
miners likely to be sent hopelessly adrift during the next
few years from this mine ajone, shareholders should make
a name for their “ good thing ’’ by subsidising a big in-
surance fund for the benefit of their incapacitated em-
ployes. Failing this, Govt, should impose a dividend tax
for the purpose.

Oh, the crash that must come ! In Broad Arrow
the other day, Govt, sold 84 town sections for a total of

there has not been an ounce of reef gold got for the last
year, and, at the wildest computation, not lOOoz. of allu-
vial. Yet shops and hotels are being built wholesale,
and the local paper has lately teemed with applications
for licenses. Certainly there are four batteries going up
which may turn out gold, but
* » * *
Lowles, English M.P. and boomer of Imperial
Federation, is booming Westralia in London. (Lowles,
who was accurately sized-up as a pompous windbag by
Australians during his visit here, has, since his return to
England, brought down the utmost ridicule upon himself
by professing to be the personal confidant of Lord Salis-
bury re the Eastern question.) There isany amount of gold,
he says ; the problem is to save it. “ I saw myself a bat-
tery made by an eminent firm of engineers,'with great
raw plates ” (they are usually cooked) “ without ripples or
any other extra appliance for catching the gold, which
was for all practical purposes positively worthless. It did
not give the ore a chance, and saved perhaps only a fifth
or sixth of the gold. Of course, the density of the water,
some containing 33 per cent, of salt, accounts for a deal
of trouble.” Lowles is a useful person in a boom, Ripples
are no part of plates, and can be added to a battery for
10 bob each. Water cannot hold 33 per cent, of salt. Pure
brine contains less salt.

Ravens wood goldfield has had very bad luck.

strain As usual, the Chink fattens. It is to be hoped

come along 1 bo improve the fortune of this failing parent
bush-town of the city of Charters Towers.
