Dad and the Two Donovans .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A sweltering summer’s afternoon. A heat that
curled and withered the very weeds. The corn-
blades drooping, sulking still. Mother and Sal
ironing, mopping their faces with a towel and
telling each other how hot it was. The dog
stretched across the door-way. A child’s bonnet
on the floor—the child out in the sun. Two horse-
men approaching the slip-rails.
Dad had gone down the gully to Farmer, who
had been sick for four days. The ploughing was
at a stand still in consequence, as we had only two
draught-horses. Dad erected a shelter over him,
made of boughs, to keep the sun off. Two or
three times a day he cut green stuff for him—
which the cows ate. He humped water to him
which he sullenly refused to drink ; and did all in
his power to persuade Farmer to get up and go on
with the ploughing. I don’t know if Dad knew
anything of mesmerism, but he used to stand for
long intervals dumbly staring the old horse full in
the eyes till in a commanding voice he would bid
him “Getup!” But Farmer lacked the patriotism
of the backblock poets. He was obdurate, and
not once did he even awake, not to mention
“ arise.”
This afternoon as Dad approached his dumb
patient he suddenly put down the bucket of
water which he was carrying and ran, shouting
angrily. A flock of crows flew away from Farmer
and “ cawed ” from a tree close by. Dad was ex-
cited, and when he saw that one of the animal’s
eyes was gone and a stream of blood trickled over
its nose he sat down and hid his face in his big
rough hands.
“ Caw, caw,” came from the tree.
Dad rose and looked up.
you ! ” he hissed—“you black wretches
of hell!”
“ Caw, caw, caw.”
He ran towards the tree as though he would
hurl it to the ground, and away flew the crows.
Joe arrived.
“ W-w-wuz they at him, Dad ? ”
Dad turned on him, trembling with rage.
Oh, you son of the Devil ! ” he commenced.
You worthless pup, you. Look there ! Do you
sec that? ” (He pointed to the horse.) Didn’t I
tell you to mind him? Didn .”
“Yes, but s-s-someone’s dog had a k-k-k-an-
geroo bailed up.”
“ Damn you, be off out of this!” And Dad
aimed a block of wood at Joe which struck him on
the back as he made away. But nothing short of
two broken legs would stop Joe, who the next
instant had dashed among the corn like an emu
into a scrub.
Dad returned to the house, foaming and vowing
to take the gun and shoot Joe down like a waf-
Jaby. But when he saw two horses hanging up
he hesitated and would have gone away again had
mother not called out that he was wanted. He
went in reluctantly.
Red Donovan and his son, Mick, were there.
Donovan was the publican, butcher, and horse-
dealer at the Overhaul. He was reputed to be
well-in, though some said that if everybody had
their own he wouldn’t be worth much. He was a
glib-tongued Irishman who knew everything—or
fondly imagined he did—from the law to horse-
surgery. There was money to be made out of
selections, he reckoned, if selectors only knew how
to make it—the majority, he proclaimed, didn’t
know enough to get under a tree when it rained.
Asa dealer, he was a hard nut, never giving more
than a “ tenner ” for a ,£2O beast, or selling a £lO
one for less than £2O. And few knew Donovan
better than did Dad, or had been taken in by him
oftener ; but on this occasion Dad was in no easy
or benevolent frame of mind.
He sat down, and they talked of crops and the
weather, and beat about the bush until Donovan
said:—
«‘‘ Ha ye y° u any fat Steers to sell? ” Dad hadn’t.
But, ’he added, “ I can sell you a horse.”
Which one ? asked Donovan, for he knew the
horses as well as Dad did—perhaps better.
“ The bay—Farmer.”
“ How much ? ”
“Seven pounds.” Now, Farmer was worth
£ll, if worth a shilling—f.e., before he took sick—
and Donovan knew it well.
. “ Seven,”he repeated, ponderingly. “ Give you
six.”
Never before did Dad show himself such an
expert in dissimulation. He shook his head
knowingly, and enquired of Donovan if he would
take the horse for nothing.
“ Split the difference, then—make it six-ten ? ”
Dad rose and looked out the window.
“ There he is now,” he remarked, sadly, “in the
gully there.”
“Well, what’s it to be—six-ten or nothing?”
renewed Donovan.
“All right, then,” Dad replied, demurely,
take him.”
The money was paid there and then and re-
ceipts drawn up. Then saying that Mick would
come for the horse on the day following, and after
offering a little gratuitous advice on seed-wheat
and pig-sticking, the Donovans left.
* * * *
Mick came the next day, and Dad showed
him Farmer, under the bushes. He wasn’t dead,
because when Joe sat on him he moved. “ There
he is,” said Dad, grinning.
Mick remained seated on his horse, bewildered-
looking, staring first at Farmer, then at Dad.
“ Well ? ” Dad remarked, still grinning. Then
Mick spoke feelingly.
“You swindling old crawler!” he said, and
galloped away. It was well for him he got a good
start.
* * * *
For long after that we turned the horses
and cows into the little paddock at night, and if
ever the dog barked Dad would jump up and go
out in his shirt.
We put them back into the big paddock again,
and the first night they were there two cows and a
four-year-old bullock got out and went away, tak-
ing with them the chain that fastened the slip-
rails. We have never seen or heard of them since.
STEELE RUDD.
The novelist Tschernischeffsky has been
in prison at Astrachan for over 40 years because
he wrote two romances in the year ’5O. There was
nothing particularly objectionable about them,
but the censor couldn’t understand them, and
he thought the author had better be locked
up until he found out what he was driving at.
Jben he got tired and forgot all about him, and
Ischernischeffsky'has stayed in gaol ever since.
N. : “ Thy gold perish with thee ! ” Thus
said the indignant apostle to Simon Magus, but it
is not a way they have in the Salvation Army,
they freeze on to the gold. Melb. has the law-
case of Buntine v. the Army. Here a “pro-
moted ” member, known as Holy Bob, diverted
away £2OOO by will from his children to this
religious organisation. One might think it would
refuse to take the money. But, no. The Devil’s
brigade is summoned up for real fighting. No
hallelujahs, but the cold, cruel steel of the legal
bayonet. Ho not to law before the unbelievers.”
Oh, pooh ! Two thousand quid. Yet they will
lose double as much through giving rope for
Purves to charge women-officers and other women
of the organisation specifically with immorality,
while the Judge contemptuously says the question
is not whether Holy Bob was moral, but whether
he was sane.
Jacob Lichtscheindl, of Pfahlert’s Hotel,
gives tne most recklessly-prodigal free-luncli show
ill bydney. With its sixpenn’orth of beer on
baturday mornings the crowd puts away (among
dozens of other items) as many as 20 large boiled
turkeys. The other day a free-luncher pounced
on one of these fat and helpless birds and com-
placently hacked off the breast and a leg, which he
was complacently carrying to a table, when some-
body jolted his elbow and the delicious morsels
fell to the floor, where they were, amidst the jeers
of the assemblage, instantly grabbed by a large
dog. ihe jaw of that free-luncher fell so far that
a gloom settled on the assemblage until a man
came in and, after viewing the wild turkey-rush,
• L j - y ’ haven>t you any soup ? ” He wasn’t
satisfied with the prospect of getting a turkey, a
1-nnt of beer, a table-full of other provender, a knife
and fork and plate, and a place to sit down in—
all for sixpence. Which shows how much grati-
tude there is in the world. Also, when a free-
feeder proceeded to wipe his hands on a snowy
tablecloth and was expostulated with by a waiter
he exclaimed: “ Well, why don’t you provide
serviettes ! ”
A Dissolution of Partnership.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They were old trackers. In Tasmania there are
no “swagmen” or “travellers” —they are all.
“ trackers,” whether male or female, old or young.
A windfall in the guise of a broken wheelman
had given them the necessary price of two beers,
and a ten-mile tramp supplied the unnecessary
thirst. The beers and old identities were being
discussed in the verandah of the pub. at The Dive
Corners.
Derwent Nugget and Barracouta had been mates
for two years. The former had “tracked” the
main road before, aye, hundreds of times. Barra-
couta was a comparative stranger in parts there-
abouts. Half-a-mile back they had passed the
ruins of a public-house, where, in a paddock ad-
joining, a weary-looking man was desultorily
looking for hens’-nests among the briar bushes.
The Nugget took a long drink, and tenderly
set his pint-pot down on the seat beside him.
“ That old bloke back yonder could tell a proper
yarn about the old days,” he said.
“Dessay he could, ” remarked Barracouta, lazily.
“Looks a bit of a case.”
“ Bum things happened in his family.”
( Barracouta was not an enthusiastic listener.
“ Speo so,” was all the encouragement he gave his
mate.
Nugget continued : “ A brother of his went
mad. His mother locked him up in the cellar for
cheeking her and forgot all about him for half a
day. When they opened the cellar-door the boy
was off his chump.”
‘ ‘ Booze ? ” queried Barracouta.
“Booze be hanged,” began Nugget. “No,
fright.”
Barracouta was growing interested, but silently
took another pull at his beer. Nugget did ditto,
very solemnly, then said : “ How’s that for mater-
nal forgetfulness, ’Couta ? ”
Barracouta agreed it was a “rum go.”
Nugget lit his pipe with a slow sulphur match
and smoked for a minute or two before proceeding
with his yarn.
The boy grew up, but never got over it. He
was always mad. One day he went out and sat
on a log and burnt himself to death. How’s that
for warm, ’Couta?”
Barracouta agreed that it was “red ’ot.”
That bloke in the briars did four years for a
bit of cattle-duffing before he was 20. It was his
father s ole the bullock, but the youngster swore
he did, and took the sentence. How’s that for
filial affection, ’Couta ? ”
‘My oath,” said Barracouta, carelessly. He
didn t seem to think much of the filial affection,
but said “ My oath ” from sheer politeness.
Nugget went on : “ They were going to make
the railway junction down there, and the Govern-
ment offered the old man a lump of money for the
bit of land they wanted ; but the old chap would
neither sell, rent, nor lend a single acre for a station
yard. That’s why the junction was made here.
In six months the grass grew up through the floor
of the bar down yonder, and blue ruin roosted in
the deserted skittle-alley.”
He wouldn t sell ? ” ejaculated Barracouta.
Not a foot. ”
Barracouta finished his beer.' Then he slowly
shouldered his swag 1 and reached for his billy.
“ Nugget,” he said reproachfully, “I do like a
liar ; but you suit me too bianky well.’ 5 With that
he marched off up the road, and the partnership
was irrevocably dissolved.
Nugget’s face wore a look of quiet surprise as he
-.watched his mate tramp out of sight along the
metal road. Then he cadged a pipeful of tobacco
from a railway fettler and went off up the branch
line.
“ ’Couta wasn't much of a tracker,” he said to
nobody in particular; “too touchy about old
tunes.
“That Derwent Nugget is a fearful liar,” said
Barracouta to some hop-pickers next day. “I
could stand his filial affection yarn, and the old
woman s forgetfulness, and the man sitting on a
log fire to burn himself to death ; but when it came
to a cattle-duffing publican refusing a railway
junction at his door, it filled me up altogether. So
I slung him.”
* * » *
I, who witnessed the falling-out of the Derwent
Nugget and Barracouta, made it my business to
have a yarn with the weary-looking man among
the briar-bushes down by the deserted pub. He
proved a by-no-means unintelligent though some-
what hopeless person. Heassured me that Nugget’s
yarn was absolutely true.
Tas.
JOHN DRAYMAN.
Ashore and Afloat, an English teetotal
lie-jerker, tells how, at the inquest recently on a
noted drunkard, “ on the skull being opem d, the
odor of alcohol was sickening. One of the sur-
geons then struck a match, and held it close to
the head. Immediately a blue flame enveloped
the whole of the exposed brain.” The writer
regrets he wasn’t present at that spirit stance,
owing to a prior engagement at the inquest on a
noted teetotal orator, at which, on the skull being
opened, a huge torrent of water belched forth with
such violence as to break a half-inch plate-glass
window, and drown every soul in a juvenile
Rechabite procession which was passing at the
time. The corpse was finally arrested on a charge
of murder by two policemen who only reached it
after three hours’ battling with the flood. The
poor children were not insured.
Dear Bulletin,—-This is Bourke. I met
Bob the other day. Knew him at once. He was
foaming at the mouth, his eyes were red and
glaring, and the hair was all burnt off his back by
the sun. He knew me, too, for as soon as he came
into the bar he said, “ Hullo, Tremens ; hot, isn’t
it?” “Yes,”lsaid —“ hot even for Bourke ; you
don’t get 112' in the shade every day for eight
days, and I haven’t been out of this bar now for
eleven days for more than two hours at a time.”
He looked at me sharply and said, “Drinking?”
“ No, killing snakes,” I answered. “ The blanky
place swarms with them. There’s one on you
now.” I thought I heard him laugh, but it was
only a fellow in the corner whom I didn’t know.
He’d been sitting there for a week knocking snakes
off the counter with a stick. This fellow now
offered to shout, but Bob said he’d sing first. They
argued it out, and I must have fallen asleep, for I
woke up with a start and heard Bob singing “ Oh !
don’t you remember Sweet Alice, Ben ” He
stopped suddenly, with his eyes fixed, and staring
with horror at the counter. We all looked. Sure
enough, there was the biggest snake I ever saw in
my life, lying full length along the bar-counter.
The fellow in the corner was up in a moment, and
made a terrific whack at him with his stick. But
Bob was quicker. Seizing the snake in his mouth
like a flash, he had him on the floor in an instant.
Then took place one of the strangest and fiercest
fights I ever witnessed. With one savage snap
Bob would bite the head clean off the snake, but
immediately another head would appear at the
other end. As fast as Bob bit one head off
another would come, only at the other end, and
all the time they were rolling over each other,
snapping and hissing like demons. The row
brought in the landlord, who, seeing the trouble
and seeming to be used to such scenes, seized a
bottle of whisky, dropped five drops into the
snake’s mouth, killing him instantly. This ended
the trouble, but it set the fellow in the corner
going for the rest of the day. Being so impressed
by what he had seen, he would do nothing else but
kill snakes by dropping whisky into their mouths.
This went on till all of a sudden a death-adder put
his head out of the bottle and stared into his face.
Hurriedly placing the bottle on the floor, he re-
sumed his seat in the corner, but never for one in-
stant did he take his eyes off the bottle again.
Bob and I leave here to-morrow for the Never-
Never.—D. Tremens.

“After being a victim to Typhoid, Brain, and Rheumatic fevers, in 1892, my
system was left in a terrible condition. Besides being very weak, I had numerous

surgeons in attendance informed me that I was suffering from blood poison. The
